Divine Transcendence

The Proper Interpretation of God's Hand: Introduction to the Book
Book introduction explaining the metaphorical interpretation of God's hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's methodology for interpreting anthropomorphic verses in the Quran, specifically the phrase 'hand of God'.

Core Idea
God's hand is not a limb but a metaphor for the covenant.

This introductory passage sets the stage for the entire book by tackling one of the most challenging issues in Islamic theology: how to understand verses that seem to attribute physical features to God. The author uses the example of 'hand of God' from the Quran to demonstrate his interpretive approach, emphasizing God's transcendence (tanzih) and the need for metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the opening and note the structure: basmala, hamdala, then the main argument.
Notice the abrupt ending; the passage is a fragment.
2
Identify the key verse and interpretation
Focus on how al-Zamakhshari interprets 'hand of God' as the covenant with the messenger.
The author cites Quran 48:10 as the starting point.
3
Understand the theological principle
Notice the author's reliance on verses like 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11) to justify figurative interpretation.
This is a foundation for the book's methodology.
4
Contrast with literalist view
See the criticism of those who lack skill in ta'wil and take the literal meaning.
The author positions his approach as the correct one.
5
Reflect on the book's methodology
Consider how this introduction sets the tone for the rest of the work.
The book will consistently apply this interpretive method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
التعريف بالكتاب :
الحمد لله رب العالمين، والصلاة والسلام على خاتم النبيين، محمد. وعلى آله وأصحابه، وعلى الأنبياء السابقين، ومن اهتدى بهديهم إلى يوم الدين.
أما بعد،
ففي القرآن الكريم قوله تعالى: {إن الذين يبايعونك إنما يبايعون الله. يد الله فوق أيديهم}، وهذا القول يفسره الإمام محمود بن عمر بقوله: هو يريد أن يد رسول الله - التي تعلو أيدي المبايعين – هي يد الله. والله - تعالى منزه عن الجوارح، وعن صفات الأجسام. وإنما المعنى: تقرير أن عقد الميثاق مع الرسول، كالعقد مع الله، من غير تفاوت بينهما. كقوله تعالى: {من يطع الرسول فقد أطاع الله}. يريد هذا الشيخ الجليل أن يقول: إن يد الله، لا تعني أن الله يد جارحة كأيدي البشر، وإنما تعني أن العقد مع الرسول، كالعقد مع الله. لأن الله وعد بأن يوفى المجاهدين أجرهم بغير حساب، ومن أوفى بعهده من الله؟ وقد التزم الشيخ الجليل بهذا المعنى لورود نصوص محكمة في القرآن، تنفي المثل عن الله عز وجل. منها قوله تعالى: {ليس كمثله شيء وهو السميع البصير}. والذين لا يحسنون التأويل من المسلمين، يقولون: إن الله له يد، لأنه أثبت لنفسه يداً في [؟]
(1) انظر الكشاف في سورة الفتح
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Introduction to the Book:
Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and blessings and peace be upon the seal of the prophets, Muhammad, and upon his family and companions, and upon the earlier prophets, and those who follow their guidance until the Day of Judgment.
To proceed:
In the noble Qur'an is His statement: {Indeed, those who pledge allegiance to you are only pledging allegiance to God. The hand of God is above their hands.} This saying is interpreted by Imam Mahmud ibn Umar saying: He intends that the hand of the Messenger of God – which is above the hands of those pledging allegiance – is the hand of God. And God – exalted is He – is transcendent above limbs and above attributes of bodies. Rather, the meaning is: affirming that the covenant with the messenger is like the covenant with God, with no difference between them. Like His statement: {Whoever obeys the messenger has obeyed God.} This venerable scholar intends to say: The hand of God does not mean that God has a physical hand like human hands, but rather it means that the covenant with the messenger is like the covenant with God. For God has promised to give the mujahideen their reward without account, and who is more faithful to His covenant than God? And this venerable scholar adhered to this meaning because of clear texts in the Qur'an that negate likeness from God, the Mighty and Majestic. Among them is His statement: {There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.} And those among the Muslims who do not excel in interpretation say that God has a hand, because He affirmed for Himself a hand in [?]
(1) See al-Kashshaf in Surah al-Fath.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a book introduction that defines the author's interpretive methodology regarding anthropomorphic verses. The author opens with the basmala and hamdala, then states the book's purpose: to clarify the correct understanding of divine attributes. He uses the Quranic verse about the pledge of allegiance (Quran 48:10) as an example. He cites Imam Mahmud ibn Umar (al-Zamakhshari) who explains that 'the hand of God' above the hands of the believers is not a literal hand but refers to the covenant with the messenger, analogous to obedience to God. The author emphasizes God's transcendence (tanzih) above physical attributes. He then reiterates the interpretation: 'hand' here means the covenant, not a physical limb. He supports this with another verse (Quran 4:80) linking obedience to the messenger with obedience to God. To justify this figurative interpretation, he points to Quranic verses that negate any likeness to God (Quran 42:11). Finally, he contrasts his approach with that of Muslims who lack skill in interpretation (ta'wil) and take the literal meaning. The passage thus establishes the book's commitment to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) in line with divine transcendence.

Key Terms
يد الله
التأويل
التنزيه
الجوارح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author is denying the attribute of 'hand' entirely.
Correction: The author does not deny the attribute but interprets it metaphorically as the covenant, affirming God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the phrase 'hand of God' in the Quran mean?
A) A literal physical hand of God
B) The authority and covenant with the messenger
C) A metaphor for power but still a limb
D) An unknowable attribute that cannot be interpreted

Key Takeaway
The hand of God in the Quran is a metaphor for the covenant, not a physical limb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'the hand of God is above their hands,' what assumptions about God's nature do you immediately bring? Are you willing to submit to a God whose attributes you cannot fully grasp, or do you secretly wish He were more like a human ruler you can visualize? What does your reaction reveal about your heart's attachment to creating a god in your own image?
?
The author argues that the covenant with the messenger is like a covenant with God. Have you ever treated your allegiance to the Prophet as seriously as your allegiance to God? In what area of your life today are you failing to honor that equivalence?
?
God is not like anything you have seen or imagined. Do you truly worship the God described in this passage, or have you constructed a comfortable mental image of God that feels safe? What would it cost you to let go of that image and submit to the utterly transcendent God?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Opening Praise and Metaphorical Interpretation of Divine Attributes
Al-Razi opens with praise of Allah and states the metaphorical interpretation of His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi establishes divine transcendence through metaphorical interpretation of problematic attributes in the opening of his work.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are transcendent; anthropomorphic terms must be interpreted metaphorically.

Al-Razi begins his masterpiece 'Asas al-Taqdis' with a concise yet profound statement of Islamic theology. In these opening lines, he not only praises Allah but lays the foundation for his entire methodology: the attributes of Allah are beyond literal human understanding and must be interpreted in a way that befits His majesty. This passage is the key to understanding the Ash'ari approach to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation. Notice the rhythm and the key terms used by Al-Razi.
Pay attention to the transition from praise to doctrinal statement.
2
Identify the structure
The passage has two main parts: praise of Allah's necessary attributes, and then the metaphorical interpretation of three specific terms. Mark where the transition occurs.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on the three terms: istiwā', nuzūl, and majī'. Understand why they needed interpretation to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbīh).
Each term is given a metaphorical meaning that befits Allah's majesty.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Ask yourself: Why does Al-Razi reject both literal reading and complete negation (ta'ṭīl)? What is the middle path of metaphorical interpretation?
This is the foundation of tanzīh (transcendence).
5
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this opening sets the stage for the rest of the book. This concise statement encapsulates the Ash'ari approach to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الواجب وجوده وبقاؤه الممتنع تغيره وفناؤه العظيم قدره واستعلاؤه العميم آلاؤه ونعماؤه الدال على وحدانيته أرضه وسماؤه المتعالى عن شوائب التشبيه والتعليل صفاته وأسماؤه فاستواؤه قهره واستيلاؤه ونزوله بره وعطاؤه ومجيئه حكمه
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, whose existence and perpetuity are necessary, whose change and annihilation are impossible, whose power and supremacy are great, whose favors and blessings are universal, who is indicated by His earth and sky as evidence of His oneness, who is exalted above the impurities of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) and causality (taʿlīl) in His attributes and names. So His istiwā' (settling) is His subjugation and domination, and His nuzūl (descending) is His benevolence and giving, and His majī' (coming) is His decree.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is the opening of al-Razi's work, functioning as both praise of Allah and a doctrinal statement. It begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, then immediately defines Allah's necessary attributes: His existence and perpetuity are wajib (necessary), while change and annihilation are mumtani' (impossible) for Him. The author then emphasizes His greatness and universal blessings. The phrase 'الدال على وحدانيته أرضه وسماؤه' indicates that the earth and sky are signs of His oneness. Crucially, al-Razi declares that Allah's attributes and names are transcendent above 'شوائب التشبيه والتعليل' – the impurities of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) and causality (taʿlīl). This sets the stage for his methodology of ta'wīl (metaphorical interpretation). He then gives three examples: istiwā' (settling) is interpreted as qahr (subjugation) and istīlā' (domination), not literal sitting; nuzūl (descending) is birr (benevolence) and atā' (giving), not literal movement; and majī' (coming) is hukm (decree), not literal arrival. This passage thus concisely presents the Ash'ari approach to problematic divine attributes, affirming transcendence while rejecting both literal anthropomorphism and complete negation (ta'ṭīl).

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
التشبيه
التعليل
التأويل
الاستواء
النزول
المجيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi denies the attributes of Allah and only focuses on transcendence.
Correction: Al-Razi affirms the attributes but interprets them metaphorically to avoid anthropomorphism, as seen in his explanation of istiwā', nuzūl, and majī'.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi in this passage, how should attributes like 'istiwā' (settling) be understood?
A) As literal sitting in a physical sense
B) As metaphorical expressions indicating subjugation and domination
C) As completely unknown and not interpretable
D) As only for the Hereafter, not for this world

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic attributes metaphorically, rejecting both literalism and complete negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses describing Allah's hand, face, or coming, do you instinctively form a mental image? How does al-Razi's insistence on metaphorical interpretation challenge the way you imagine Allah? What would it mean for your worship if you truly internalized that Allah is beyond any comparison?
?
Al-Razi interprets 'descending' as benevolence and giving. Reflect on a recent trial in your life. Can you see it as a descent of Allah's kindness rather than a literal lowering? Are you willing to let go of literal interpretations that might limit your trust in His absolute transcendence?
?
If Allah's 'coming' is His decree, then every event in your life is His direct command. Does this change how you respond to unexpected difficulties? Will you now face them as an encounter with divine justice and wisdom, not as random occurrences?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Divine Transcendence in Razi's Doxology
Razi praises God and declares His transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi combines praise of God with a strong affirmation of divine transcendence over anthropomorphism and causal reasoning.

Core Idea
God's attributes are beyond human comparison and physicality.

Razi opens his work with a doxology that sets the foundation for his entire theological method: God is necessarily existent, eternal, and utterly transcendent. The passage you will read establishes that God's attributes and names are free from any impurities of anthropomorphism (tashbih) and cause-effect thinking (ta'lil). This prepares the reader to accept the metaphorical interpretations that follow in subsequent sections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic praise
Read the opening lines slowly, noting the rhythm and the key divine attributes praised.
Notice the contrast between 'necessary existence' and 'impossible change'.
2
Identify the core transcendence claim
Find the sentence where Razi states that God's attributes are exalted above tashbih and ta'lil. This is the heart of the lesson.
Tashbih means likening God to creation; ta'lil means assigning causes to God.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does Razi begin his book with this declaration? How does it shape the way he will discuss specific attributes later?
This principle of transcendence underlies all his metaphorical interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الواجب وجوده وبقاؤه، الممتنع تغيره وفناؤه. العظيم قدره واستعلاؤه، العميم آلاؤه ونعماؤه. الدال على وحدانيته: أرضه وسماؤه، المتعالى عن شوائب التشبيه والتعليل: صفاته وأسماؤه. فاستواؤه: قهره واستيلاؤه، ونزوله: بره وعطاؤه، ومجيئه: حكمه وقضاؤه، ووجهه: جوده أو جوده وحباؤه، وعينه: حفظه وهونه واجتباؤه، وضحكه: عفوه أو إذنه وارتضاؤه، ويده: إنعامه أو إكرامه واصطفاؤه. ولا يجرى في الدارين من أفعاله إلا ما يريده ويشاؤه. والعظمة: إزاره، والكبرياء: رداؤه.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, whose existence and permanence are necessary, whose change and annihilation are impossible. Great is His power and His exaltation, abundant His favors and His blessings. That which indicates His oneness: His earth and His heavens. Exalted above the impurities of anthropomorphism and cause-effect (tashbih and ta'lil): His attributes and His names. So His istiwa (establishing Himself) is His subjugation and His domination; His nuzul (descending) is His kindness and His giving; His maji' (coming) is His judgment and His decree; His wajh (face) is His generosity, or His generosity and His gift; His 'ayn (eye) is His preservation, His gentleness, and His selection; His dahk (laughing) is His pardon, or His permission and His approval; His yad (hand) is His bestowal, or His honouring and His choosing. And nothing runs in the two abodes from His actions except what He wills and desires. And greatness is His izār (waist-wrapper), and majesty is His ridā' (robe).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a doxology that defines God's essential attributes and then interprets certain anthropomorphic descriptions metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence. The author begins by praising God as necessarily existent and eternal (الواجب وجوده وبقاؤه), and as impossible to change or perish (الممتنع تغيره وفناؤه). He affirms God's power, exaltation, and abundant favors. He then states that God's earth and heavens indicate His oneness, and that God's attributes and names are far removed from anthropomorphism (التشبيه) and cause-effect thinking (التعليل). The core of the passage is the list of interpretations: 'istiwa' (often understood as 'sitting') is reinterpreted as 'subjugation and domination' (قهره واستيلاؤه); 'nuzul' (descending) as 'kindness and giving' (بره وعطاؤه); 'maji'' (coming) as 'judgment and decree' (حكمه وقضاؤه); 'wajh' (face) as 'generosity' (جوده) or 'generosity and gift' (جوده وحباؤه); 'ayn' (eye) as 'preservation, gentleness, and selection' (حفظه وهونه واجتباؤه); 'dahk' (laughing) as 'pardon' (عفوه) or 'permission and approval' (إذنه وارتضاؤه); and 'yad' (hand) as 'bestowal' (إنعامه) or 'honouring and choosing' (إكرامه واصطفاؤه). The passage concludes that nothing occurs in this world or the next except by God's will, and that greatness and majesty are exclusively His, using the metaphors of a waist-wrapper and robe from prophetic hadith. Razi thus affirms the attributes mentioned in scripture but insists on their metaphorical interpretation to avoid corporealism.

Key Terms
الواجب الوجود
التشبيه
التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Razi denies all divine attributes or that he only pays lip service to transcendence while actually negating attributes.
Correction: Razi does not deny attributes; he affirms them but insists they are beyond human comprehension and free from any resemblance to creation. He praises God's attributes (صفاته وأسماؤه) while declaring them transcendent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the relationship between God's attributes and anthropomorphism?
A) God's attributes are exactly like human attributes
B) God's attributes are transcendent and free from anthropomorphism
C) God has no attributes at all
D) Anthropomorphism is a valid way to understand God

Key Takeaway
Razi's doxology establishes that God's attributes and names are absolutely transcendent and free from any likeness to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever unwittingly compare Him to something you love or fear in this world? What is that comparison, and how does Razi's warning about tashbih challenge you to purify your conception of Allah starting today?
?
You recite 'Alhamdulillah' daily, but do you truly feel the weight that Allah's existence is necessary and His change is impossible? In your moments of doubt or fear, do you rely on this certainty, or do you act as if Allah could change? What one practice can help you internalize this?
?
The earth and heavens are signs of Allah's oneness, yet you see them every day. What specific natural phenomenon (a sunset, a storm, a starry night) have you encountered recently that you could turn into a conscious sign of His oneness, and what will you do to remind yourself of that link tomorrow?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Metaphorical Interpretation of Anthropomorphic Attributes
Razi interprets seven anthropomorphic terms metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets specific anthropomorphic descriptions of God (like 'face', 'hand', 'eye') in a metaphorical manner that preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Scriptural anthropomorphisms must be understood figuratively to avoid likening God to creation.

After establishing the principle of transcendence, Razi turns to specific attributes mentioned in the Quran and Hadith that might be misunderstood literally. He provides metaphorical interpretations for each, showing that they refer to God's actions, not physical parts. This passage is a practical application of the method of tawil (figurative interpretation) widely used in Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of attributes and interpretations
Carefully read each pair: the Arabic term and its metaphorical meaning given by Razi. Notice the alternatives (e.g., 'or' for some).
Razi provides both a primary interpretation and sometimes a second possibility.
2
Identify the common theme
What do all these interpretations have in common? They all refer to God's actions, power, or attributes, not to physical limbs.
The pattern: each term is interpreted as an act of God (e.g., 'coming' as 'judgment').
3
Reflect on the concluding statements
Read the final lines about God's will and the metaphors of the izār and ridā. How do they reinforce the lesson?
The hadith metaphors 'izār' and 'ridā' stress that greatness and majesty belong exclusively to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الواجب وجوده وبقاؤه، الممتنع تغيره وفناؤه. العظيم قدره واستعلاؤه، العميم آلاؤه ونعماؤه. الدال على وحدانيته: أرضه وسماؤه، المتعالى عن شوائب التشبيه والتعليل: صفاته وأسماؤه. فاستواؤه: قهره واستيلاؤه، ونزوله: بره وعطاؤه، ومجيئه: حكمه وقضاؤه، ووجهه: جوده أو جوده وحباؤه، وعينه: حفظه وهونه واجتباؤه، وضحكه: عفوه أو إذنه وارتضاؤه، ويده: إنعامه أو إكرامه واصطفاؤه. ولا يجرى في الدارين من أفعاله إلا ما يريده ويشاؤه. والعظمة: إزاره، والكبرياء: رداؤه.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, whose existence and permanence are necessary, whose change and annihilation are impossible. Great is His power and His exaltation, abundant His favors and His blessings. That which indicates His oneness: His earth and His heavens. Exalted above the impurities of anthropomorphism and cause-effect (tashbih and ta'lil): His attributes and His names. So His istiwa (establishing Himself) is His subjugation and His domination; His nuzul (descending) is His kindness and His giving; His maji' (coming) is His judgment and His decree; His wajh (face) is His generosity, or His generosity and His gift; His 'ayn (eye) is His preservation, His gentleness, and His selection; His dahk (laughing) is His pardon, or His permission and His approval; His yad (hand) is His bestowal, or His honouring and His choosing. And nothing runs in the two abodes from His actions except what He wills and desires. And greatness is His izār (waist-wrapper), and majesty is His ridā' (robe).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a doxology that defines God's essential attributes and then interprets certain anthropomorphic descriptions metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence. The author begins by praising God as necessarily existent and eternal (الواجب وجوده وبقاؤه), and as impossible to change or perish (الممتنع تغيره وفناؤه). He affirms God's power, exaltation, and abundant favors. He then states that God's earth and heavens indicate His oneness, and that God's attributes and names are far removed from anthropomorphism (التشبيه) and cause-effect thinking (التعليل). The core of the passage is the list of interpretations: 'istiwa' (often understood as 'sitting') is reinterpreted as 'subjugation and domination' (قهره واستيلاؤه); 'nuzul' (descending) as 'kindness and giving' (بره وعطاؤه); 'maji'' (coming) as 'judgment and decree' (حكمه وقضاؤه); 'wajh' (face) as 'generosity' (جوده) or 'generosity and gift' (جوده وحباؤه); 'ayn' (eye) as 'preservation, gentleness, and selection' (حفظه وهونه واجتباؤه); 'dahk' (laughing) as 'pardon' (عفوه) or 'permission and approval' (إذنه وارتضاؤه); and 'yad' (hand) as 'bestowal' (إنعامه) or 'honouring and choosing' (إكرامه واصطفاؤه). The passage concludes that nothing occurs in this world or the next except by God's will, and that greatness and majesty are exclusively His, using the metaphors of a waist-wrapper and robe from prophetic hadith. Razi thus affirms the attributes mentioned in scripture but insists on their metaphorical interpretation to avoid corporealism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Razi denies the literal meaning of these attributes, implying the Quran is untrue or that God is unlike what He describes.
Correction: Razi does not deny the attributes; he affirms them as real but insists they are not physical. The metaphorical interpretations preserve the truth of the scripture while avoiding corporealism.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does 'yad' (hand) of God mean?
A) A literal hand
B) Bestowal or honouring and choosing
C) Power and strength
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
Razi interprets anthropomorphic scriptural terms as referring to God's actions and attributes, not physical limbs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about God's 'coming' or 'hand', do you instinctively imagine a physical entity? How can Razi's reinterpretation reshape your worship to be more mindful of God's absolute transcendence? Will you consciously remind yourself of these meanings next time you hear such verses?
?
If God's 'laughter' is His pardon, do you truly seek His pleasure or do you take His forgiveness for granted? How does knowing that His attributes are about His actions—not a body—affect your sense of accountability before Him? What will you change today to live more in awe of His judgment and mercy?
?
Razi says greatness and majesty are exclusive to God (His izar and rida). In what areas of your life do you still cling to your own 'greatness' or pride, forgetting that true majesty belongs only to Him? How will you actively humble yourself today to reflect that truth?

Metaphorical Interpretation of Anthropomorphic Descriptions
Imam Razi defines metaphors for six anthropomorphic terms and affirms divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical meanings of key anthropomorphic terms used for God, affirming His absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
God's attributes like hand and eye are metaphors for His actions and perfections.

In this passage, Imam Razi demonstrates how to interpret scriptural descriptions that might seem anthropomorphic. He shows that terms like 'face', 'eye', and 'hand' do not imply physical organs but refer to God's generosity, preservation, and beneficence. This lesson will guide you through each term's meaning and the underlying principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice how each phrase starts with a term followed by its interpretation.
The passage is concise; focus on the structure: term : meaning (or alternative meaning).
2
Understand Each Term's Metaphorical Meaning
Work through each anthropomorphic term (وجهه, عينه, ضحكة, يده, العظمة, الكبرياء) and its explanation. Compare the literal vs. metaphorical interpretations.
The commentary provides the exact meanings; note the use of 'or' indicating multiple valid interpretations.
3
Identify the Core Principle of Divine Transcendence
After the list, the passage states that nothing occurs except by God's will. Then it links 'grandeur' and 'pride' to the hadith about God's 'waist-wrapper' and 'cloak'. Reflect on how all interpretations preserve God's transcendence.
This step connects the specific examples to the broader theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضاؤه، ووجهه: جوده، أو جوده وحباؤه. وعينه: حفظه، وهونه اجتباؤه. وضحكة: عفوه، أو إذنه وارتضاؤه. ويده: إنعامه، أو إكرامه واصطفاؤه. ولا يجرى في الدارين من أفعاله إلا ما يريده ويشاؤه. والعظمة: إزاره، والكبرياء: رداوه.
And His decree, and His face: His generosity, or His generosity and His love. And His eye: His preservation, His condescension, His choosing. And His laughter: His pardon, or His permission and His pleasure. And His hand: His beneficence, or His honor and His selection. And nothing occurs in the two abodes of His actions except what He wills and wishes. And grandeur: His waist-wrapper, and pride: His cloak.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of key anthropomorphic terms used in divine descriptions. The author interprets each term metaphorically to affirm God's transcendence. He states that 'His face' refers to His generosity or His generosity and love. 'His eye' means His preservation, condescension, and choosing. 'His laughter' is His pardon or His permission and pleasure. 'His hand' is His beneficence, honor, and selection. The author then emphasizes that nothing occurs in this world or the next except what God wills. Finally, he explains that 'grandeur' is His waist-wrapper and 'pride' is His cloak, using the well-known hadith metaphor. This shows that physical attributes are not applied literally to God.

Key Terms
وجهه
عينه
ضحكة
يده
العظمة
الكبرياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that God has physical body parts like a human.
Correction: These terms are metaphors for God's actions and attributes; they do not imply physical organs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'His eye' (عينه) refer to?
A) A physical eye
B) His preservation, condescension, and choosing
C) His knowledge
D) His anger

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic terms in scripture are interpreted metaphorically to preserve God's absolute transcendence and unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's 'hand' is His beneficence and 'face' is His generosity, does that change how you perceive His closeness to you? Are you satisfied with a God whose nearness is in His actions rather than a physical presence? How does that affect your reliance on Him in your daily needs?
?
If nothing occurs in this world or the next except what God wills, what does that mean for the moments of pain or failure you have experienced? Can you truly accept that every event—even your own mistakes—falls under His will? How would your reaction to hardship change if you lived with this conviction?
?
The metaphor of 'grandeur as His waist-wrapper' and 'pride as His cloak' evokes a sense of awe. Do you feel that awe when you think of God's majesty, or have you become desensitized? What step will you take today to cultivate a deeper reverence for His greatness?

The First Section: Evidences for Divine Transcendence
Section heading announcing the first part on divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of this section heading and the key concepts it introduces for studying God's transcendence.

Core Idea
The first section establishes that God is beyond body and space.

Imam al-Razi begins his work by clearly stating the focus of the first major section: providing evidences that God is absolutely free from bodily characteristics and spatial location. This heading sets the stage for all subsequent arguments, so it's essential to grasp its meaning and implications before proceeding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic heading carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: القسم الأول, الدلائل, منزه, الجسمية, الحيز.
This is a straightforward announcement; let the words sink in.
2
Identify the three components
The heading has three parts: (1) it's the first section, (2) it contains evidences (دلائل), (3) the evidences prove God is transcendent (منزه) from body and space. Understand each component's role.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does al-Razi start with this? Because before discussing God's attributes, he must first remove false conceptions that God is like creation. This heading signals a polemical and defensive approach.
This connects to the broader project of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول
في
الدلائل الدالة على أنه تعالى
منزه عن الجسمية والحيز
The First Section: On the evidences indicating that He, exalted, is transcendent above corporeality and spatial location.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a section heading. It announces the start of the first major section of the work, which will present proofs (دلائل) that God (تعالى) is absolutely transcendent (منزه) from being a body (جسمية) and from occupying space (حيز). This establishes the thematic scope of the subsequent arguments.

C) That God occupies a specific location
D) That God created the universe

Key Takeaway
The first section heading establishes that God is absolutely transcendent, setting the foundation for the proofs to follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine a location in the sky or a form? The heading declares He is beyond these. Are you willing to strip away every mental image you have of Him, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
This section begins with the claim that God is free from body and space. Is your belief in Allah's transcendence merely intellectual, or does it affect how you turn to Him in prayer and supplication?

The First Premise: A Being Beyond Sensory Perception
Imam Razi defines the first premise with three formulations of divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's first premise: the necessity of affirming a being that transcends all spatial, temporal, and directional limitations.

Core Idea
The first premise of Islamic theology is a being not limited by space, time, or direction.

Imam Razi begins his theological treatise by laying down a foundational premise: there exists a being that cannot be perceived by the senses. This being is not located anywhere, not bound by time, and not confined to any direction. He presents three distinct formulations, each emphasizing a different aspect of transcendence, yet all pointing to the same reality. This lesson unpacks that premise and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read its literal English translation. Let the rhythm of the original come through before interpreting.
Notice the repetition: 'we claim' appears multiple times.
2
Identify the three formulations
The passage gives three different ways to describe the same being. Find each one and note how they differ in wording but not in meaning.
The first uses location (here/there), the second uses time and direction, the third uses indwelling/separateness.
3
Reflect on their unity
Imam Razi explicitly states that all three expressions mean the same thing. Ask yourself: why would he present multiple formulations instead of just one?
This prepares the reader for objections later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى في اثبات موجود لا يشار اليه بالحس. اعلم: أنا ندعى وجود موجود لا يمكن أن يشار إليه بالحس، أنه ههنا، أو هناك أو نقول: إنا ندعى وجود موجود غير مختص بشيء من الأحيان والجهات، أو نقول: إنا ندعى وجود موجود غير حال فى العالم، ولا مباين عنه في شيء من الجهات الست، التي العالم. وهذه العبارات متفاوتة والمقصود من الكل شيء واحد.
The First Premise: On Proving the Existence of a Being Not Pointed to by Sense. Know that we claim the existence of a being that cannot be pointed to by sense, (in that) it is not here or there, or we say: we claim the existence of a being not specific to any times or directions, or we say: we claim the existence of a being not indwelling in the world, nor separate from it in any of the six directions that [belong to] the world. These expressions are different, and the intended meaning of all is one thing.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the first premise of the book. The author explicitly states the goal: to prove a being that is not perceptible by the senses. He then gives three alternative formulations of the same doctrine: it is not located in space (here or there), not restricted to time or direction, and not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any of the six spatial directions (up, down, left, right, front, back). The author notes that although these expressions vary, they all denote the same concept. This precision is important for the subsequent argument, as the opponent will object that such a notion is self-evidently false.

Key Terms
موجود
لا يشار إليه بالحس
غير حال في العالم
مباين
الجهات الست

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the three formulations are contradictory.
Correction: Imam Razi explicitly states they all mean the same thing; the differences are merely verbal to cover different aspects of transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the common meaning of the three expressions given for the first premise?
A) God is not in any direction
B) God is not a body
C) God is not perceptible by sense and is beyond spatial/temporal location
D) God is separate from the world

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's first premise establishes that the being whose existence is to be proved is utterly beyond any spatial, temporal, or directional limitation—a reality expressed in three equivalent ways.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah in your daily life, do you ever catch yourself imagining Him in a particular direction—like above the sky? How does Razi's assertion that He is 'not specific to any direction' expose that mental habit, and what concrete thought pattern will you replace it with starting today?
?
Razi says the three formulations are different but all point to one meaning: a being beyond sense perception. Is your heart truly comfortable with a God you cannot visualize, or do you secretly long for an imagined form? What dua or Quranic verse will you memorize to reinforce this tanzih in your heart?
?
The premise declares that Allah is 'not indwelling in the world nor separate from it.' Does the way you speak about Allah's presence (e.g., saying 'He is everywhere') accidentally imply either indwelling or separation? Commit to one phrase from this lesson that will correct your speech from now on.

The Opponent's Objection: Self-Evident Falsity
Imam Razi quotes opponents who claim the premise is self-evidently false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' claim that the transcendence of Allah beyond space and direction is self-evidently false based on their division of relationships between existents.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that any two existents must either interpenetrate or be separate in a direction — making a spaceless being impossible.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the objection from those who deny that Allah can be free from spatial categories. They assert that their premise is so obvious it requires no proof. Let us examine their reasoning based on the necessary knowledge of how existents relate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the objection fully. Notice the confident language: 'known by necessity.'
2
Identify the claim
The opponents claim that the falsity of the premises (about a non-spatial God) is self-evident. Ask yourself: what does 'self-evident' mean here?
3
Understand the reasoning
They base their claim on a division: every two existents must be either indwelling or separate in one of six directions. This creates seven categories.
The six directions are up, down, front, back, left, right.
4
Consider the challenge
Reflect on why this objection is a serious challenge to the author. If the opponents are right, then a spaceless, directionless God is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المخالفين من يدعى: أن فساد هذه المقدمات: معلوم بالضرورة. قالوا: لأن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن كل موجودين، فإنه لابد وأن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر، أو مباينا عنه، مختصا بجهة من الجهات الست المحيطه به. وقالوا: وإثبات موجودين على خلاف هذه الأقسام السبعة: باطل في بدائه العقول.
And among the opponents is one who claims that the falsity of these premises is known by necessity. They said: because necessary knowledge is attained that every two existents must be such that one is either indwelling in the other, or separate from it, specific to a direction from the six directions surrounding it. And they said: proving two existents contrary to these seven divisions is invalid in the primary intuitions of intellects.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the objection from the opponents. They assert that the claim of a being not limited by space or direction is self-evidently false. Their reasoning is the division of relationships between any two existents: either one is inside the other (indwelling) or separate and located in one of the six directions. They argue that there are seven possibilities (two sets: indwelling in one, or separate in six directions) and that any existent must fall into one of these. Therefore, the idea of a being outside these categories is impossible according to primary rational intuition. This sets up the challenge that the author must address.

Key Terms
فساد
معلوم بالضرورة
العلم الضروري
الأقسام السبعة
بدائه العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the opponents are denying God's existence entirely.
Correction: The opponents are not denying God; they are denying that a non-spatial, non-directional being can exist based on their categories.

Checkpoint
How many divisions do the opponents claim exist for the relationship between two existents?
A) 6
B) 7
C) 3
D) 4

Key Takeaway
The opponents ground their objection in a division of relationships between existents, claiming that a non-spatial being is inconceivable to the human intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own intuition about God—like that He must be 'somewhere' or 'everywhere'—is self-evident and beyond question? What would it cost you to hold that assumption loosely?
?
The opponents claim that a non-spatial being is inconceivable to the human intellect. Is there an area of your faith where you have limited God to fit your mental categories (space, time, causality)? Will you ask Him tonight to expand your understanding beyond what feels 'necessary'?
?
When you read the phrase 'invalid in the primary intuitions of intellects', does it provoke any discomfort? Are you willing to have your 'primary intuitions' about God challenged, or do you secretly believe they are infallible?

First Proof: Consensus of Rational Thinkers
Imam Razi presents the first proof: consensus of rational people on God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the consensus of rational people to argue that the claim 'God is a body' is not self-evidently false.

Core Idea
If a belief were self-evidently false, rational people would not unanimously reject it.

Imam Razi challenges the opponent's claim that God's embodiment is self-evidently false. He presents his first proof: the majority of rational thinkers agree that God is not a body. This consensus, he argues, could not happen if the opposite were a self-evident truth. The passage also shows that even philosophers and some Muslim groups affirm non-bodily existents, undermining the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the calm, logical tone of the author.
Pay attention to the key terms listed below.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
Find where the author states the consensus and then explains why this consensus disproves self-evidence. The argument has two clear parts.
3
Examine the supporting examples
The author mentions philosophers (intellects, souls, prime matter) and Muslim groups (Mu'tazila, Rawafid) who accept non‑bodily existents. Think about why these examples strengthen the proof.
These examples show that belief in immaterial beings is not irrational.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: if this consensus exists, can the opponent's premise really be self‑evident? The lesson here is about the nature of self‑evidence and rational agreement.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: الذي يدل على أن هذه المقدمات ليست بديهية، وجوه: الأول: إن جمهور العقلاء المعتبرين، اتفقوا على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا مختص بشى من الجهات، وأنه تعالى غير حال في العالم، ولا مباين عنه في شيء من الجهات. ولو كان فساد هذه المقدمات معلوما بالبديهة، لكان إطباق أكثر العقلاء على إنكارها ممتنعا، لأن الجمع العظيم من العقلاء لا يجوز إطباقهم على إنكار الضروريات. بل نقول: الفلاسفة اتفقوا على إثبات موجودات، ليست بمتحيزة، ولا حالة فى المتحيز. مثل العقول والنفوس وألهيولى. بل زعموا: أن الشيء الذى يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله أنا: موجود، وليس بجسم ولا جسمانى. ولم يقل أحمد بأنهم في هذه الدعوى، منكرون البديهيات، بل جمع عظيم من المسلمين، اختاروا مذهبهم. مثل معمر بن عباد السلمى من المعتزلة، ومثل محمد بن النعمان من الرافضة.
So we say: That which indicates that these premises are not self-evident are points: The first: Indeed the majority of rational people of consideration agreed that He exalted is not a body nor specific to any direction, and that He exalted is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any direction. And if the falsity of these premises were known by intuition, then the agreement of most rational people upon denial of it would be impossible, because a great multitude of rational people cannot agree upon denying necessities. Rather we say: The philosophers agreed upon affirming existents that are not bodies nor indwelling in bodies, such as intellects, souls, and matter. Rather they claimed that the thing to which every human points by saying 'I' is existent, and is not a body nor bodily. And no one said that they in this claim are deniers of self-evident truths. Rather a great multitude of Muslims adopted their doctrine, such as Muammar ibn Abbad al-Sulami from the Mu'tazila, and such as Muhammad ibn al-Nu'man from the Rawafid.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that the premise is not self-evident. It argues by consensus (ijmāʿ of rational people). The proof proceeds in two steps: (1) The vast majority of rational thinkers accept that God is not a body, not in a direction, not indwelling, not separate spatially. (2) If the contrary were self-evident, such widespread consensus could not happen, because many rational people cannot unanimously deny a self-evident truth. Then the author strengthens the argument by noting that even philosophers affirm immaterial entities (intellects, souls, prime matter) and everyone acknowledges the self (the 'I') as a non-bodily existent. Moreover, Muslims influenced by such views (e.g., certain Mu'tazila and Shi'a) also adopted these positions without being charged with denying self-evident truths. This evidence shows that the opponent's claim of self-evidence is false.

Key Terms
جمهور العقلاء
متحيز
العقول
النفوس
الهيولى
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that the premise is not self-evident?
A) Revelation from prophets
B) Consensus of rational people on the opposite
C) Logical impossibility of the premise
D) Agreement of philosophers on matter

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses the consensus of rational thinkers to show that the belief in God's transcendence is not self-evidently false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God simply because it seemed 'obvious' to you, without examining whether it is truly self-evident? How does Razi's argument challenge you to rethink what you consider 'obvious'? This week, identify one such belief and set aside time to trace its justification.
?
Razi points out that even philosophers affirm the existence of non-material entities like the soul. Do you truly believe that your own self or soul is non-physical? If so, does your daily life reflect that conviction, or do you live as if you are only a body? What would change if you took that belief seriously?
?
The consensus of rational people here is used as evidence. Are you willing to reconsider a deeply held belief if you discovered that the majority of thoughtful people from different traditions disagree with you? What would it take for you to admit that your certainty might be misplaced?

Divine Transcendence

Razi's Consensus Argument
Razi presents the consensus argument against self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the non-bodiliness of God cannot be self-evident because many rational people reject it.

Core Idea
If a belief were self-evident, no rational person could deny it. Many do, so it isn't.

Razi begins his first proof by appealing to the consensus of rational people. He points out that many have affirmed God's transcendence beyond physical categories. If this were obviously false, it would be impossible for so many intelligent people to reject it. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Pay attention to key terms like متحيز, حال, مباين, بديهة.
2
Identify the agreed premise
Find the statement that Razi says rational people have agreed upon: God is not a body, not in a direction, not indwelling or separate.
3
Understand the logical argument
Notice the conditional: if this premise were self-evidently false, the consensus denying it would be impossible. Since the consensus exists, it cannot be self-evidently false.
This is a key move in Islamic theological methodology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does Razi use consensus of rational people rather than scripture or reason alone?
It shows that self-evidence cannot be used to dismiss a position held by many rational thinkers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن جمهور العقلاء المعتبرين ، اتفقوا على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا مختص بشى من الجهات ، وأنه تعالى غير حال في العالم ، ولامباين عنه في شيء من الجهات . ولو كان فساد هذه المقدمات معلوما بالبديهة ، لكان إطباق أكثر العقلاء على إنكارها ممتنعا ، لأن الجمع العظيم من العقلاء لا يجوز إطباقهم على إنكار الضروريات . بل نقول : الفلاسفة اتفقوا على إثبات موجودات ، ليست بمتحيزة ، ولا حالة فى المتحيز . مثل العقول والنفوس والهيولى . بل زعموا : أن الشيء الذى يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله أنا : موجود ، وليس بجسم ولا جسمانى . ولم يقل أحمد بأنهم في هذه الدعوى ، منكرون البديهيات ، بل جمع عظيم من المسلمين ، اختاروا مذهبهم . مثل . معمر بن عباد السلمى من المعتزلة ، ومثل محمد بن النعمان من الرافضة، ومثل أبي القاسم الراغب، وأبي حامد الغزالى من أصحابنا . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يمكن أن يقال بأن القول بأن الله تعالى ليس بمتحيز ، ولا حال فى المتحيز : قول مدفوع في بدائه العقول .
The first: Indeed the majority of respected rational people have agreed that He, exalted, is not a body (mutahayyiz) nor specific to any direction, and that He, exalted, is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any direction. And if the falsity of these premises were known by self-evidence, then the agreement of most rational people upon denying them would be impossible, because a great multitude of rational people cannot agree upon denying self-evident truths. Rather we say: the philosophers agreed upon affirming existents that are not bodies nor indwelling in a body, such as the intellects, souls, and prime matter. Rather they claimed that the thing to which every human points by saying 'I' is existent, and is not a body nor corporeal. And no one said that they, in this claim, are deniers of self-evident truths. Rather a great multitude of Muslims chose their doctrine, such as Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī from the Muʿtazila, and such as Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān from the Rāfiḍa, and such as Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib, and Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī from our companions. And if the matter is so, then how can it be said that the statement that God Almighty is not a body nor indwelling in a body is a statement rejected by the primary intuitions of intellects?
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's first proof. He argues that many rational people (jumhūr al-ʿuqalāʾ al-muʿtabarīn) have agreed that God is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor located in a direction, neither indwelling (ḥāll) in the world nor separate (mubāyin) from it. If this were a self-evident falsehood, it would be impossible for so many rational people to reject it, since a large group cannot unanimously deny a self-evident truth. Razi then points to philosophers who affirm non-physical existents like intellects (ʿuqūl), souls (nufūs), and prime matter (hayūlā). They even claim that the self ('I') is not a body or corporeal. Yet no one accuses them of denying self-evident truths. Moreover, many Muslims, including Muʿtazilites like Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī, Rāfiḍites like Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān (al-Shaykh al-Mufīd), and even Ashʿarites like Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib and Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī, accepted that God is not a body. Given this widespread agreement, it is absurd to claim that affirming God's non-bodiliness is rejected by primary intuition.

Key Terms
متحيز
حال
مباين
بديهة
ضروريات
جمهور العقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that self-evident truths are obvious to everyone, so any disagreement means the truth is not self-evident.
Correction: Razi shows that the fact that many rational people deny something is evidence that it cannot be self-evident. Self-evident truths are such that no rational person can deny them.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Razi draw from the fact that many rational people agree that God is not a body?
A) That this belief must be self-evident
B) That this belief cannot be self-evidently false
C) That this belief is irrational
D) That consensus is not a valid proof

Key Takeaway
The presence of a consensus of rational people on a theological statement shows that its opposite is not self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a theological truth is so obvious that anyone who denies it must be foolish? How does Razi's argument expose the pride in that assumption, and what will you do to guard against it?
?
Do you secretly rely on the majority opinion of rational people to confirm your faith, rather than seeking direct evidence? What step can you take today to move from consensus-based belief to conviction?
?
When you encounter a person who rejects Islamic teachings about God's transcendence, does your heart respond with anger or with humility? How can this lesson transform your reaction to intellectual disagreement?

Supporting Evidence: Philosophers and Muslim Scholars
Razi provides examples of non-physical entities accepted by philosophers and Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi supports his argument by citing philosophers and Muslim scholars who affirm non-physical existents.

Core Idea
Philosophers and many Muslims accept non-physical entities, so denying God's non-bodiliness is not irrational.

Razi strengthens his first proof by pointing to a wide range of thinkers—from philosophers to Muslim theologians—who believe in non-physical realities. This shows that the idea of God not being a body is far from self-evidently false. Let's explore his examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about philosophers
Read the part about philosophers affirming non-physical existents like intellects, souls, and prime matter.
Notice that these are accepted without accusing of denying self-evidence.
2
Examine the self analogy
Philosophers claim the 'I' is non-physical. This shows that non-physical entities are not against intuition.
3
List the Muslim scholars
Identify the Muslim scholars from different sects who accept the non-bodiliness of God.
Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād (Muʿtazila), Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān (Shiʿa), and from Ashʿarīs: al-Rāghib and al-Ghazālī.
4
Analyze the rhetorical question
Understand why Razi concludes that this position cannot be dismissed as against primary intuition.
Because many rational people accept similar ideas without controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن جمهور العقلاء المعتبرين ، اتفقوا على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا مختص بشى من الجهات ، وأنه تعالى غير حال في العالم ، ولامباين عنه في شيء من الجهات . ولو كان فساد هذه المقدمات معلوما بالبديهة ، لكان إطباق أكثر العقلاء على إنكارها ممتنعا ، لأن الجمع العظيم من العقلاء لا يجوز إطباقهم على إنكار الضروريات . بل نقول : الفلاسفة اتفقوا على إثبات موجودات ، ليست بمتحيزة ، ولا حالة فى المتحيز . مثل العقول والنفوس والهيولى . بل زعموا : أن الشيء الذى يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله أنا : موجود ، وليس بجسم ولا جسمانى . ولم يقل أحمد بأنهم في هذه الدعوى ، منكرون البديهيات ، بل جمع عظيم من المسلمين ، اختاروا مذهبهم . مثل . معمر بن عباد السلمى من المعتزلة ، ومثل محمد بن النعمان من الرافضة، ومثل أبي القاسم الراغب، وأبي حامد الغزالى من أصحابنا . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يمكن أن يقال بأن القول بأن الله تعالى ليس بمتحيز ، ولا حال فى المتحيز : قول مدفوع في بدائه العقول .
The first: Indeed the majority of respected rational people have agreed that He, exalted, is not a body (mutahayyiz) nor specific to any direction, and that He, exalted, is not indwelling in the world nor separate from it in any direction. And if the falsity of these premises were known by self-evidence, then the agreement of most rational people upon denying them would be impossible, because a great multitude of rational people cannot agree upon denying self-evident truths. Rather we say: the philosophers agreed upon affirming existents that are not bodies nor indwelling in a body, such as the intellects, souls, and prime matter. Rather they claimed that the thing to which every human points by saying 'I' is existent, and is not a body nor corporeal. And no one said that they, in this claim, are deniers of self-evident truths. Rather a great multitude of Muslims chose their doctrine, such as Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī from the Muʿtazila, and such as Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān from the Rāfiḍa, and such as Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib, and Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī from our companions. And if the matter is so, then how can it be said that the statement that God Almighty is not a body nor indwelling in a body is a statement rejected by the primary intuitions of intellects?
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's first proof. He argues that many rational people (jumhūr al-ʿuqalāʾ al-muʿtabarīn) have agreed that God is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor located in a direction, neither indwelling (ḥāll) in the world nor separate (mubāyin) from it. If this were a self-evident falsehood, it would be impossible for so many rational people to reject it, since a large group cannot unanimously deny a self-evident truth. Razi then points to philosophers who affirm non-physical existents like intellects (ʿuqūl), souls (nufūs), and prime matter (hayūlā). They even claim that the self ('I') is not a body or corporeal. Yet no one accuses them of denying self-evident truths. Moreover, many Muslims, including Muʿtazilites like Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī, Rāfiḍites like Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān (al-Shaykh al-Mufīd), and even Ashʿarites like Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib and Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī, accepted that God is not a body. Given this widespread agreement, it is absurd to claim that affirming God's non-bodiliness is rejected by primary intuition.

Razi rebuts the claim that God's non-spatiality is repugnant to primary intellects
Razi names two scholars and poses a rhetorical question

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the authority of respected scholars to argue that the doctrine of God's non-spatiality is not contrary to rational intuition.

Core Idea
If respected scholars accept it, it cannot be self-evidently false.

Razi presents a simple yet powerful argument: the fact that eminent theologians like al-Raghib and al-Ghazali affirm that God is neither a body nor an accident demonstrates that this view is not repugnant to reason. He challenges the claim that it is obviously false by pointing to the reasoned acceptance of these authorities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a citation, then a rhetorical question.
2
Identify the two scholars
Who are Abu al-Qasim al-Raghib and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali? Why does Razi mention them as 'from our fellows'?
They are prominent Ash'ari theologians.
3
Understand the rhetorical question
Razi asks: if these scholars accept the view, how can anyone claim it is rejected by primary intellects? Reflect on why this is an effective rebuttal.
The argument is not just appeal to authority but shows the view is rationally defensible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل أبي القاسم الراغب، وأبي حامد الغزالى من أصحابنا. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يمكن أن يقال بأن القول بأن الله تعالى ليس بمتحيز، ولا حال في المتحيز: قول مدفوع في بدائه العقول.
And among our fellows are Abu al-Qasim al-Raghib and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. And if that is the case, how can it be said that the statement that God Most High is not a body (mutahayyiz) nor an accident (hall) in a body is a statement repudiated in the primary intuitions (badā'i‘ al-‘uqūl)?
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal. Razi names two prominent Ash‘ari scholars—Abu al-Qasim al-Raghib (probably al-Raghib al-Isfahani) and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali—as examples of those who held that God is neither a body nor an accident. He then asks rhetorically: if such respected scholars accept this, how can anyone claim that it is obviously false to primary intellects? The argument is that the very fact that intelligent and orthodox theologians affirm this view shows that it is not a self-evident absurdity.

Razi's Argument from Comparative Certainty
Razi compares two propositions to show the intellect is not certain about the necessity of location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the disparity in the intellect's certainty between two premises to demonstrate that the necessity of spatial location for non-worldly things is not a self-evident truth but a product of imagination.

Core Idea
Certainty disparity reveals that spatial necessity is not self-evident.

This lesson examines Razi's second argument against the claim that every existent must occupy space. By comparing the intellect's response to two propositions—one about existence beyond the world and another about basic arithmetic—he shows that the former is not self-evident. The argument relies on a key distinction between rational certainty and habitual imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Razi's argument carefully. Note the two premises he compares.
The Arabic is concise; the translation clarifies.
2
Identify the two premises
Locate the first premise (an existent neither inside nor separated from the world by direction) and the second premise (one is half of two, etc.).
3
Notice the disparity
Observe that the intellect hesitates on the first but is certain on the second. Why does this disparity matter?
This disparity is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
The disparity shows the first premise is not self-evident; it is based on wahm and khayal.
Razi concludes the intellect is not decisive on the first premise.
5
Reflect on the implication
This argument undermines the opponent's claim that necessity of location is axiomatic.
The inclination comes from imagination, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إنا إذا عرضنا على العقل وجود موجود لا يكون حالا في العالم ولا مباينا عنه في شيء من الجهات الست، وعرضنا على العقل أيضا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان: وجدنا العقل متوقفا في المقدمة الأولى، جازما في المقدمة الثانية. وهذا التفاوت معلوم بالضرورة. وذلك يدل على أن العقل غير قاطع في المقدمة الأولى لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات. غاية ما في الباب: إنا نجد من أنفسنا ميلا إلى القول بأن كل ما سوى العالم لابد وأن يكون حالا فيه، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة والحيز. إلا أنا نقول: لما رأينا أن العقل لم يجزم بهذه المقدمة، مثل جزمه بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين: علمنا أنه غير قاطع بأن ما سوى العالم لا بد وأن يكون حالا فيه، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة، بل هو مجوز لنقيضه. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن ذلك الظن إنما حصل بسبب أن الوهم والخيال لا يتصرفان إلا في المحسوسات. فلا جرم كان من شأنهما أنهما يقضيان على كل شيء بالأحكام اللائقة بالمحسوسات. وهذا الميل إنما جاء بسبب الوهم والخيال، لا بسبب العقل البتة.
Secondly: when we present to the intellect the existence of an existent that is neither an accident in the world nor separate from it in any of the six directions, and we also present to the intellect that one is half of two and that negation and affirmation cannot coexist, we find the intellect hesitating regarding the first premise and certain regarding the second. This disparity is known necessarily. That indicates that the intellect is not decisive regarding the first premise, neither by negation nor by affirmation. The upshot is: we find in ourselves an inclination toward saying that everything other than the world must be either an accident in it or separate from it by direction and space. But we say: since we see that the intellect is not as certain about this premise as it is that one is half of two, we know that it is not decisive that what is other than the world must be an accident in it or separate from it by direction; rather, it is possible to conceive its opposite. When this is established, we say: that assumption only occurred because the estimative faculty (wahm) and imagination (khayāl) only operate in the domain of sensible things. Consequently, it is in their nature to pass judgments on everything according to the principles appropriate to sensibles. This inclination came only from the estimative faculty and imagination, not from the intellect at all.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a proof by comparative certainty. Razi presents two propositions: (1) an existent other than the world must be either an accident in the world or separate from it by direction; (2) one is half of two and negation and affirmation cannot coexist. The intellect is certain about (2) but hesitant about (1). This difference in certainty shows that (1) is not a self-evident truth. The belief in (1) is therefore merely an inclination based on the imagination and the estimative faculty, which habitually apply sensory categories to everything. Thus, the necessity of spatial location for every existent is not rationally grounded.

Key Terms
وهم
خيال
جهات الست
متحيز
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of spatial location for non-worldly things is a self-evident rational truth.
Correction: Razi shows that this belief is not self-evident; it is a product of the estimative faculty and imagination, which apply sensory categories to non-sensory matters.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the disparity in the intellect's certainty between the two premises?
A) The first premise is false
B) The first premise is not a self-evident truth
C) The intellect is unreliable
D) The second premise is also doubtful

Key Takeaway
Razi's comparative certainty argument shows that the necessity of spatial location for non-worldly things is not rationally self-evident but arises from the habit of the estimative faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you honestly examine your own thoughts to see if you have been picturing Allah in a place? What will you do to stop that habit starting today?
?
Your intellect is certain that one is half of two, but when it comes to Allah's relationship to the world, you may feel a strong pull to picture Him outside or inside. That pull is not from pure reason. Are you willing to distrust that feeling and instead rely on what revelation says—that nothing is like Him? What specific thought can you train yourself to think when you catch yourself imagining Allah spatially?
?
The estimative faculty judges everything by what it senses. How many of your daily thoughts about Allah are actually shaped by imagination rather than by what He has revealed about Himself? What change in your mental habits are you ready to make today to worship Allah as He truly is, not as your mind pictures Him?

The Tripartite Division of Existence Includes Non-Spatial Being
Razi presents a logical division that includes non-spatial being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that the intellect naturally accepts a threefold division of existence, which leaves room for a non-spatial, non-accidental being.

Core Idea
A complete division of existence requires a category for what is neither body nor accident.

Razi presents a simple but powerful logical argument: when we classify all existents, the intellect immediately sees that there are three possibilities—body, accident, or neither. Leaving out the third possibility makes the division incomplete. This establishes that the concept of a non-spatial being is logically admissible; the question of its actual existence must be settled by a separate proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let Razi's logical structure come through first.
Pay attention to the three categories presented.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three divisions: (1) something that is a body (mutahayyiz), (2) something that is an accident in a body (hall fī l-mutahayyiz), and (3) something that is neither a body nor an accident.
The third category is the key to the argument.
3
Understand the claim about the intellect
Razi claims that the intellect is certain of the validity of this tripartite division. He also says that if we omit the third, the division is clearly incomplete.
This appeal to immediate intellectual certainty is a hallmark of rational theology.
4
Grasp the logical consequence
Since the third division is a genuine possibility that the intellect does not reject, any claim that a non-spatial being does or does not exist requires a separate proof (dalīl munfaṣil).
Razi is not proving God's existence here; he is establishing that the concept is not self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إنا إذا قلنا: الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزا، أو حالا في المتحيز، أو لا متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز: وجدنا العقل قاطعا بصحة هذا التقسيم. ولو قلنا: الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزا، أو حالا في المتحيز واقتصرنا على هذا القدر: علمنا بالضرورة أن هذا التقسيم غير تام، ولا منحصر وأنه لا يتم إلا بضم القسم الثالث. وهو أن يقال: وإما أن لا يكون متحيزا، ولا حالا في المتحيز. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، علمنا بالضرورة: أن احتمال هذا القسم، وهو وجود موجود لا يكون متحيزا، ولا حالا في المتحيز: قائم في العقول من غير مدافعة ولا منازعة، وأنه لا يمكن الجزم بنفيه ولا بإثباته إلا بدليل منفصل.
Thirdly: when we say: the existent is either a body (mutahayyiz), or an accident (hall) in a body, or neither a body nor an accident: we find the intellect certain of the validity of this division. And if we say: the existent is either a body or an accident and restrict ourselves to this extent, we know necessarily that this division is incomplete and not exhaustive, and that it is only completed by adding the third division, which is to say: or it is neither a body nor an accident. If that is so, we know necessarily that the possibility of this division, namely the existence of an existent that is neither a body nor an accident, stands in the intellect without resistance or contestation, and that it cannot be decisively negated or affirmed except by a separate proof.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a logical division argument. Razi proposes a tripartite classification: every existent is either (1) a body (mutahayyiz), (2) an accident in a body, or (3) neither. The intellect immediately affirms this division as sound. If we omit the third category, the division is incomplete. The third category is a genuine logical possibility that the intellect does not reject. Therefore, the existence of a non-spatial being is not self-evidently impossible; it can only be denied or affirmed through independent proof.

Key Terms
موجود
متحيز
حال
تقسيم
دليل منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is proving that God exists by this tripartite division.
Correction: Razi is not proving existence; he is showing that the concept of a non-spatial being (such as God) is logically possible. Actual existence requires a separate proof.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the division 'existent is either a body or an accident'?
A) It is complete and exhaustive
B) It is incomplete without adding a third category
C) It is logically impossible
D) It is rejected by primary intellects

Key Takeaway
Razi's tripartite division shows that the intellect admits the possibility of a non-spatial existent; only a separate proof can affirm or deny its reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you unconsciously picture a being with a body or location? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your worship if God were beyond all spatial concepts?
?
The intellect accepts the possibility of a non-spatial being 'without resistance'. But have you ever resisted a truth about God simply because it was unfamiliar or uncomfortable? What would it cost you to truly accept that God is unlike anything you've ever known?
?
Razi says that only a separate proof can affirm the existence of the third category. Do you live as though you have that separate proof, or do you rely on assumptions and habits? What step will you take today to strengthen your certainty in the unseen?

Abstraction of Humanity Shows Non-Spatial Existence
Razi argues from concept abstraction to non-spatial existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the abstraction of common concepts to argue that non-spatial existence is rationally conceivable.

Core Idea
If humanity can be thought apart from space, so can the Creator.

Razi presents his fourth argument for Allah's transcendence of space. He begins with a simple observation about human individuals sharing a common concept of 'humanity'. This concept, when abstracted from particular shapes and locations, shows that we can think of something non-spatial. From this, he argues that the Creator, who is beyond the sensible world, can also be conceived without spatial attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key observation: all humans share the concept of 'humanity' despite differing in particular attributes.
Identify the phrase 'ما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة' (that by which they share is other than that by which they differ).
2
Understand the abstraction process
Razi claims that 'humanity as such' is abstracted from specific shape and location. Think about how you can conceive of 'humanity' without picturing any particular person. This shows that the concept is an 'abstract intelligible' (معقول مجرد).
The abstraction is mental, not ontological—humanity does not exist separately from individuals.
3
Apply the argument to the Creator
If sensible objects yield abstract concepts free from spatial attributes, it is not improbable that the Creator of sensibles is entirely free from spatial properties. Ask yourself: why does this analogy work?
The conclusion is that divine transcendence of space is rationally acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن أشخاص الناس مشتركة في مفهوم الإنسانية. ومتباينة بخصوصياتها وتعيناتها. وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة. وهذا يقتضي أن يقال: الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية: مجردة عن الشكل المعين والحيز المعين. فالإنسانية من حيث هي، معقول مجرد. وإذا ثبت ذلك، فقد أخرج البحث والتفتيش عن المحسوس: ما هو معقول مجرد. وإذا كان كذلك، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المحسوسات منزها عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال؟
Fourthly: we know necessarily that individual humans share in the concept of humanity and differ by their particularities and individuations. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. This requires that it be said: humanity as such is abstracted from specific shape and specific location. So humanity as such is an abstract intelligible. When that is established, then investigation and examination of the sensible has produced an abstract intelligible. If that is so, how can it be deemed improbable by the intellect that the Creator of sensibles is transcendent of the accidents of sense and the attachments of imagination?
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is an argument from abstraction. Razi observes that different human individuals share the common concept of 'humanity' while differing in particular characteristics. The 'humanity' that is shared is not tied to any specific shape or location. This shows that we can conceive of an abstract intelligible reality that is free from spatial attributes. Since such abstraction is possible from sensible objects, it is not irrational to conceive of the Creator, who is the source of all sensibles, as being entirely free from the properties of sensation and imagination.

Correction: Razi is not making an ontological claim; he is arguing that the concept can be abstracted mentally, not that humanity exists independently. The abstraction is conceptual, not real.
