The Fifth Argument: Conceiving Quiddities Without Location
Razi argues from the introspective conception of quiddities to the possibility of non-spatial existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues from the nature of conceptual thought to establish that existence without location is rationally possible.

Core Idea
Quiddities can be conceived without location, so location is not essential to existence.

Imam Razi presents a simple yet powerful argument from introspection. When we think about definitions—like 'what is knowledge?'—we are not thinking about where that definition is located. This shows that our minds can separate the essence of a thing from its spatial attributes. Razi uses this to argue that an existent free from location is not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi uses the example of defining knowledge and nature to make his point.
Pay attention to the introspective method: he asks you to reflect on your own mental state.
2
Identify the key insight
Razi observes that when we are deeply absorbed in understanding a definition, we are heedless of location, shape, and magnitude. This shows that these spatial attributes are not part of the quiddity itself.
3
Consider the implication
If location is not part of the concept of a quiddity, then it is not necessary for its existence. Therefore, it is possible for something to exist without location, which supports the transcendence of Allah.
This argument does not prove that such an existent actually exists, only that it is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: إن كل ماهية، إذا اعتبرناها بحدها وحقيقتها، فإنا قد نعقلها حال غفلتنا عن الوضع والحيز. فكيف والإنسان إذا كان مستغرق الفكر في تفهم أن حد العلم ما هو؟ وحد الطبيعة ما هو؟ فإنه في تلك الحالة يكون غافلا عن حقيقة الحيز والمقدار. فضلا عن أن يحكم بأن تلك الحقيقة لا بد وأن تكون مختصة بمحل أو بجهة. وهذا يقتضي أنه يمكننا أن نعقل الماهيات حال ذهولنا عن الحيز والشكل والمقدار.
Fifthly: every quiddity (māhiyya), when we consider it by its definition and reality, we can conceive it while being heedless of position (waḍ‘) and location (ḥayyiz). Indeed, when a person is deeply absorbed in understanding what is the definition of knowledge? And what is the definition of nature? In that state, he is heedless of the reality of location and magnitude. Not to mention that he judges that such a reality must be attached to a place or direction. This implies that it is possible for us to conceive quiddities while being oblivious to location, shape, and magnitude.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is an argument from introspection. Razi points out that when we engage in conceptual thought—like trying to define 'knowledge' or 'nature'—we do not think about location or shape. This shows that our minds can grasp quiddities without requiring spatial attributes. Therefore, it is not impossible for there to be an existent that is entirely free from location, shape, and magnitude.

Proof from Humanity's Abstraction
Imam Razi proves that humanity's essence is abstracted from physical particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fourth proof that the concept of humanity is abstract and how this supports the rational plausibility of God's transcendence.

Core Idea
The shared concept of humanity proves abstraction is real, so God's transcendence is rational.

Imam Razi presents a simple but profound observation: all humans share the concept of humanity yet differ in particulars. From this, he argues that humanity as such is an abstract intelligible, stripped of shape or location. If even a concept derived from physical beings can be abstract, then it is even more fitting that the Creator of all physical things be absolutely free from physical attributes. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Don't analyze yet—just absorb.
Notice the logical connectors like 'ف' and 'وإذا'.
2
Identify the core observation
Find the premise: all humans share in 'humanity' but differ by particularities. Understand the distinction between the factor of commonality (mushāraka) and the factor of differentiation (mumāyaza).
3
Trace the abstraction conclusion
See how Razi concludes that humanity in itself is abstract—free from specific shape and location—and is thus an abstract intelligible (maʿqūl mujarrad).
The phrase 'من حيث هي هي' is key.
4
Reflect on the analogical leap
Consider the final rhetorical question: if we can conceive of an abstract concept from the sensible world, how can it be far-fetched that the Creator of sensibles is even more abstract and transcendent?
This is an argument from the lower to the higher (a fortiori).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن أشخاص الناس مشتركة في مفهوم الإنسانية . ومتباينة بخصوصياتها وتعيناتها . و ما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة. وهذا يقتضى أن يقال : الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية: مجردة عن الشكل المعين والحيز المعين فالإنسانية من حيث هي هي ، معقول مجرد . وإذا ثبت ذلك فقد أخرج البحث والتفتيش عن المحسوس : ما هو معقول مجرد . وإذا كان كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المحسوسات، منزهاً عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال ؟
Fourth: We know by necessity that individual humans share in the concept of humanity and differ by their particularities and individuations. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. This requires that it be said: humanity insofar as it is humanity is abstracted from specific form and specific location. So humanity insofar as it is it is an abstract intelligible. And if this is established, then investigation of the sensible has yielded an abstract intelligible. And if this is so, how can it be remote in reason that the Creator of sensibles is transcendent of the accidents of sense and the attachments of imagination?
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth of Imam Razi's proofs for the possibility of conceiving an abstract existence, which supports the doctrine of God's transcendence over physical attributes. The argument begins with a self-evident observation: all human individuals share in the common concept of humanity, yet each is distinct through particular characteristics. The factor of commonality (mā bihi al-mushāraka) is different from the factor of differentiation (mā bihi al-mumāyaza). From this it follows that the essence of humanity, considered purely as such, is stripped of any specific shape or location — it is an abstract intelligible (maʿqūl mujarrad). Once this is accepted, the argument concludes that if we can conceive of created humanity as abstract, then it is far more plausible that the Creator of all sensibles is absolutely free from any physical or sensory attributes. The proof uses a 'from the lower to the higher' analogy: if the effect can be abstract, the cause is even more so.

Key Terms
إنسانية (insāniyya)
معقول مجرد (maʿqūl mujarrad)
مشاركة (mushāraka)
ممايزة (mumāyaza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that abstraction means humanity exists as a separate Platonic form in a realm of ideas.
Correction: Razi's argument only uses conceptual abstraction (maʿqūl mujarrad) — a mental concept derived from individuals. It does not claim ontological independence like Platonic forms.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi conclude from the fact that humanity can be conceived without shape or location?
A) That humanity must exist in a Platonic realm
B) That the Creator of sensibles is even more free from physicality
C) That humans are essentially immaterial
D) That individuals don't really exist

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses the self-evident shared concept of humanity to show that abstraction is thinkable, and then argues a fortiori that God is beyond physical description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you catch yourself imagining a form or location? If so, are you willing to repent from that and affirm, based on this lesson, that He is beyond any attachment of imagination? How will you train your mind to worship Him as the abstract reality He is?

Does your daily worship reflect this transcendent reality, or do you still unconsciously limit God to your own mental constructs? What concrete step will you take today to correct that?

Fifth Proof: Essences Are Conceived Without Space
Imam Razi argues that essences are conceived while oblivious to space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fifth proof that essences can be conceived independently of spatial attributes, making it possible to conceive of Allah as transcendent.

Core Idea
We can think about essences without thinking about where they are.

Imam Razi presents a simple but profound argument based on introspection. When we think about abstract concepts like the definition of knowledge, we are not thinking about location or shape. This shows that our intellect can grasp essences without physical attributes, which is key to understanding how Allah can be transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a general claim, an example, and a conclusion.
The example of defining 'knowledge' and 'nature' is meant to be accessible.
2
Reflect on the argument's basis
Imam Razi uses a psychological observation: when we are absorbed in abstract thought, we are oblivious to location. Ask yourself: is this true in your own experience?
The argument is not deductive but experiential.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
If essences can be conceived without location, then the Divine Essence (being beyond all physicality) can be conceived as non-spatial. This proof supports the possibility of God's transcendence.
This was part of a larger effort to establish that Allah is not in a place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن كل ماهية ، إذا اعتبرناها بحدها وحقيقتها . فإنا قد تعقلها حال غفلتنا عن الوضع والحيز فكيف والإنسان إذا كان مستغرق الفكر فى تفهم أن حد العلم ما هو ؟ وحد الطبيعة ما هو ؟ فإنه في تلك الحالة يكون غافلا عن حقيقة الحيز والمقدار . فضلا عن أن يحكم بأن تلك الحقيقة لا بد وأن تكون مختصة بمحل أو بجهة . وهذا يقتضى أنه يمكننا أن نعقل الماهيات حال ذهولنا عن الحيز والشكل والمقدار
Fifth: Every essence, when we consider it by its definition and reality, we indeed conceive it while we are oblivious to position and location. For when a person is absorbed in thought trying to understand what is the definition of knowledge? And what is the definition of nature? In that state he is oblivious to the reality of location and magnitude, let alone judging that that reality must be specific to a place or direction. This requires that we can conceive essences while being heedless of location, shape, and magnitude.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This is the fifth proof. Imam Razi invites us to introspect. When we engage in abstract thinking — for example, trying to define 'knowledge' or 'nature' — we are completely unaware of any particular location, shape, or magnitude. We are not thinking about where this definition is located. This shows that our intellect can grasp essences (māhiyyāt) independently of any spatial or physical attributes. The argument is empirical and psychological: it relies on the common experience of abstract thought. The conclusion is that it is possible for an essence to be conceived without physical attributes, which supports the possibility of conceiving God as transcendent.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya) – essence
وضع (waḍʿ) – position
حيز (ḥayyiz) – location

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that essences are entirely independent of matter in reality.
Correction: It only claims that we can conceive them without physical attributes; it does not assert that they actually exist without matter.

Key Takeaway
Essences can be conceived without spatial attributes, as shown by our experience of abstract thinking, paving the way to understand Allah's transcendence.

The Self-Knowledge Proof for Divine Transcendence
Imam Razi presents an introspective proof for the intelligibility of abstract entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the universal experience of self-awareness during deep thought to prove that knowledge of a thing does not require knowledge of its physical attributes.

Core Idea
Self-awareness occurs without spatial awareness, proving abstraction is possible.

Imam Razi presents a powerful introspective argument. When we are absorbed in solving a difficult problem, we are aware of ourselves as thinkers, yet we are not thinking about our body's location or shape. This shows that knowledge of a thing can exist without any knowledge of its physical attributes—a key step in establishing that God, who is free from spatial limitations, can be known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the introspective scenario to the logical conclusion.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the conditional phrasing.
2
Identify the introspective scenario
Imam Razi describes a person deeply focused on a difficult problem who says 'I have judged such-and-such.' Recognize that in that moment, the person is self-aware.
This self-awareness is the starting point of the proof.
3
Trace the inference of self-knowledge
The argument says: if the person were not self-aware, they could not attribute a judgment to themselves. Since they do, self-knowledge is present.
This establishes necessary self-awareness.
4
Note the absence of spatial awareness
While self-aware, the person is oblivious to location, direction, shape, and magnitude. They don't even think about their body's position.
This dissociation is key for the conclusion.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Since knowledge of the self occurs without any spatial attributes, it proves that a thing can be known as an abstract intelligible. This supports that God, who is not in a place, can be known.
The proof is introspective and does not depend on empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: وهو أن الواحد منا حال ما يكون مستغرق الفكر والرؤية ، في استخراج مسألة معضلة ، قد يقول في نفسه : إلى قد حكمت بكذا أو عقلت كذا فحال ما يقول في نفسه : إنى عقلت كذا أو حكمت بكذا : يكون عارفا بنفسه . إذ لو لم يكن عارفا بنفسه ، لا متنع منه أن يحكم على ذاته بأنه حكم بكذا ، أو عرف كذا ، مع أنه في تلك الحالة قد يكون غافلا عن معنى الحيز والجهة ، وعن معنى الشكل والمقدار ، فضلا أن يعلم كون ذاته فى الحيز ، أو كون ذاته موصوفة بالشكل والمقدار . فثبت : أن العلم بالشيء قد يحصل عند عدم العلم بحجزه وشكله ومقداره . وذلك يفيد القطع بأن الشيء المجرد عن الوضع والجهة ، يصح أن يكون معقولا .
Sixth: That one of us, when he is absorbed in thought and reflection in extracting a difficult problem, may say to himself: 'I have judged such-and-such' or 'I have conceived such-and-such'. So when he says to himself: 'I have conceived such-and-such' or 'I have judged such-and-such', he is cognizant of himself. For if he were not cognizant of himself, it would be impossible for him to attribute to himself that he judged such-and-such or knew such-and-such, while in that state he may be oblivious to the meaning of location and direction, and to the meaning of shape and magnitude, let alone knowing that his essence is in a location or that his essence is described by shape and magnitude. It is thus established that knowledge of a thing may occur without knowledge of its delimitation, shape, and magnitude. And this yields certainty that a thing abstracted from position and direction is valid to be an intelligible.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This is the sixth proof. Imam Razi uses self-reflection. When we are deeply focused on solving a difficult intellectual problem, we often say to ourselves, 'I have judged this' or 'I have understood that.' In that moment, we are necessarily aware of our own self (nafs). Yet we are not thinking about our body's location, shape, or magnitude. In fact, we are completely oblivious to such physical attributes. The argument shows that knowledge of the self occurs without any accompanying knowledge of spatial attributes. Therefore, it is possible for a thing (like God) to be known as an abstract intelligible, free from position and direction. The proof is powerful because it uses direct introspective evidence.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs) – self
عارف (ʿārif) – cognizant
ذات (dhāt) – essence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-awareness during deep thought proves the soul is an immaterial substance.
Correction: The proof only shows that self-awareness does not depend on awareness of bodily attributes; it does not in itself prove the soul's immateriality, though it is used to support that God can be known without spatiality.

Checkpoint

A) The self is located in the brain
B) Knowledge of a thing can occur without knowledge of its physical attributes
C) The self is a material substance
D) Thought requires a physical location

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's introspective proof shows that we can know something—our own self—without any reference to location or shape, establishing that abstract knowledge of a non-spatial entity is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your self-awareness does not depend on being in a specific location, then why do you still imagine God as being 'up there' in the sky? If you can conceive of a reality beyond the physical, then why do you still live as if only the material world matters? What change in your daily habits would reflect a genuine belief in the unseen?

Seventh Proof: Senses Cannot Perceive Themselves
Imam Razi argues that the senses' inability to perceive themselves proves non-sensible realities exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the inability of the senses to perceive themselves provides a rational argument against the necessity of God being perceptible.

Core Idea
If our own faculties are invisible to themselves, why must the Creator be visible?

Imam Razi continues his series of rational arguments for God's transcendence. This seventh proof draws on a simple observation about our own senses. By showing that even our own visual and imaginative faculties are not subject to themselves, he undermines the opponent's insistence that existence is limited to what we can sense or imagine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two examples—sight and imagination—and how they lead to a conclusion.
2
Identify the analogy
See how Imam Razi uses the fact that the eye cannot see itself and the imagination cannot imagine itself as evidence that not everything must be subject to sense or imagination.
3
Understand the implication
The argument opens the door to the possibility that God, being beyond sensation and imagination, can exist without being perceived. This counters the opponent's demand for physicality.
4
Reflect on the opponent's position
Consider the opponent's stance: that God must be perceivable. How does this proof weaken that demand? How does it strengthen the case for divine transcendence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إنا نبصر الأشياء . إلا أن القوة الباصرة لا تبصر نفسها : وكذلك القوة الخيالية تتخيل الأشياء. إلا أن هذه القوة لا يمكنها أن تتخيل نفسها . فوجود للقوة الباصرة يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون كل شيء متخيلا . وذلك يفتح باب الاحتمال المذكور .
Seventh: We see things. However, the visual faculty does not see itself. Likewise, the imaginative faculty imagines things, but this faculty cannot imagine itself. So the existence of the visual faculty indicates that it is not necessary that everything be imagined. And this opens the door to the mentioned possibility.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This is the seventh proof. Imam Razi points to a common observation: our eyes can see many things, but they cannot see themselves. Similarly, our imagination can picture many things, but it cannot picture itself. This shows that there exist things (like the visual faculty itself) which are not subject to sight or imagination. Therefore, it is not necessary that everything be either visible or imaginable. This weakens the opponent's insistence that God must be perceptible by the senses. If our own faculties are not visible, then why must the Creator be visible? The argument opens the door (yafatḥu bāb al-iḥtimāl) to the possibility that existence includes realities beyond sensory and imaginative perception.

Key Terms
قوة باصرة (quwwa bāṣira) – visual faculty
قوة خيالية (quwwa khayāliyya) – imaginative faculty
متخيل (mutakhayyal) – imaginable

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God exists.
Correction: The argument does not prove God's existence; it only shows that the opponent's demand that God be perceptible is not necessary. It removes an obstacle but does not establish existence.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Imam Razi draw from the fact that the eye cannot see itself?
A) The eye is defective
B) Not everything is subject to sight or imagination
C) The soul is invisible
D) God is visible to the inner eye

Key Takeaway
The inability of our faculties to perceive themselves shows that existence is not limited to what is sensible or imaginable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own eyes cannot see themselves, why do you demand that your Creator be visible to you? What does this say about your assumption that you can fully comprehend God?
?
You have an imaginative faculty that can picture countless things, yet cannot picture itself. Is there a part of you that remains hidden even from your own imagination? How does that humble you before the One who knows you completely?
?
The argument 'opens the door to possibility.' What doors has this lesson opened in your heart regarding what you think you know about God? Are you willing to accept that your senses are not the measure of reality?

Self-Awareness Proves Knowledge Without Space
Razi argues from self-awareness during deep thought that knowledge can exist without spatial attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the experience of self-awareness during deep thought demonstrates that knowledge can exist without spatial attributes, laying the groundwork for affirming that God can be known without being in a place or direction.

Core Idea
Self-awareness while oblivious to location proves knowledge does not need space.

Razi presents a powerful argument from common experience: when we are deeply absorbed in solving a problem, we are aware of ourselves but not of our physical location or shape. This shows that self-knowledge does not depend on spatial attributes, which has profound implications for understanding how we can know God, who is beyond space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and scenario
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the scenario: a person deeply absorbed in thought who says 'I have judged such-and-such.'
The scenario is meant to be a familiar experience.
2
Identify the inference about self-awareness
Razi argues that at the moment of saying 'I have judged,' the person must be self-aware, otherwise they couldn't attribute the judgment to themselves. Yet at that moment they are oblivious to their own location and shape.
Self-awareness here is a direct knowledge, not dependent on physical attributes.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The argument establishes that knowledge of a thing can occur without knowledge of its spatial properties. Therefore, a thing abstracted from position and direction can be intelligible.
This supports the possibility of knowing God, who is free from spatial attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: وهو أن الواحد منا حال ما يكون مستغرق الفكر والرؤية، في استخراج مسألة معضلة، قد يقول في نفسه: إلى قد حكمت بكذا أو عقلت كذا. فحال ما يقول في نفسه: إني عقلت كذا أو حكمت بكذا: يكون عارفا بنفسه. إذ لو لم يكن عارفا بنفسه، لا متنع منه أن يحكم على ذاته بأنه حكم بكذا، أو عرف كذا، مع أنه في تلك الحالة قد يكون غافلا عن معنى الحيز والجهة، وعن معنى الشكل والمقدار، فضلا أن يعلم كون ذاته في الحيز، أو كون ذاته موصوفة بالشكل والمقدار. فثبت: أن العلم بالشيء قد يحصل عند عدم العلم بحجزه وشكله ومقداره. وذلك يفيد القطع بأن الشيء المجرد عن الوضع والجهة، يصح أن يكون معقولا.
The sixth: That one of us, when absorbed in thought and reflection in extracting a difficult problem, may say to himself: 'I have judged such-and-such or I have known such-and-such.' At the moment he says to himself: 'I have known such-and-such or I have judged such-and-such,' he is knowing himself. For if he were not knowing himself, it would be impossible for him to pass a judgment upon his essence that he judged such-and-such or knew such-and-such, while in that state he may be heedless of the meaning of place and direction, and of the meaning of shape and magnitude, let alone that he knows his essence to be in place, or his essence to be described by shape and magnitude. So it is established that knowledge of a thing may occur without knowledge of its confinement, shape, and magnitude. And that entails certainty that a thing abstracted from position and direction can be intelligible.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument for the possibility of knowing an abstract entity (free from spatial attributes). Razi observes that when a person is deeply absorbed in solving a difficult problem, they may say to themselves 'I have judged such-and-such' or 'I have known such-and-such.' At that moment, they are self-aware: they know themselves as the one who judged or knew. Yet at that same moment, they may be completely heedless of their own location (al-ḥayyiz), direction (al-jiha), shape (al-shakl), or magnitude (al-miqdār). They are not thinking about where their body is or what shape it has. This shows that self-knowledge does not depend on knowledge of these physical attributes. Razi argues: if knowing oneself did require knowing one's place and shape, then it would be impossible to be self-aware while being ignorant of those attributes. But we find that self-awareness occurs even when we are oblivious to such things. Therefore, knowledge of a thing (here, oneself) can exist without knowledge of its spatial and physical properties. From this, it follows that a thing that is abstracted (mujarrad) from position (al-waḍʿ) and direction (al-jiha) can be intelligible (maʿqūl). This argument paves the way for affirming that God, who is not in a place or direction, can be known.

Key Terms
مستغرق الفكر
الحيز
الجهة
الشكل
المقدار
الشيء المجرد عن الوضع والجهة
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: To know a thing, one must know its location and physical shape.
Correction: Razi refutes this using the case of self-awareness: one can know oneself without any awareness of one's place or shape, showing that knowledge does not require spatial attributes.

Checkpoint
What does Razi use to show that knowledge can exist without knowledge of place and shape?
A) The fact that the eye sees but does not see itself
B) The experience of self-awareness while absorbed in thought
C) The argument that if God were sensible He would be divisible
D) The admission of the Hanabila that God's essence differs from created things

Key Takeaway
Self-awareness reveals that knowledge does not require spatial attributes, establishing that a spaceless entity like God can be known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you were completely absorbed in a problem and said to yourself 'I have understood this.' Were you aware of your own body's location at that moment? This lesson shows that self-awareness does not depend on spatial knowledge. How would your relationship with God change if you truly internalized that He is not located anywhere—not above the heavens, not in your heart—but is known through pure intellect? Commit to five minutes of silent reflection today on the implications of a spaceless God.
?
Razi uses an ordinary experience to prove a profound truth. When was the last time you paid careful attention to your own thoughts as evidence for something beyond the physical? What would it mean for you to treat your daily mental experiences as signs of God? Challenge yourself: for the rest of the day, notice every time you are self-aware and consciously remind yourself that this self-awareness points to the possibility of knowing the Absolutely Abstract.
?
If you believe that God is not in a place, yet you find yourself thinking of Him as 'up there' or 'in the sky,' what does that say about the state of your knowledge? This lesson argues that knowledge of a thing does not require spatial concepts. Are you willing to accept a God who is absolutely transcendent, or do you cling to comfortable spatial images? Decide on one specific way you will purify your concept of God today—perhaps by repeating 'Lā makān lahu' (He has no place) with intention.

The Seventh Argument: Limits of Perception
Razi's seventh argument: sensory limits show not all must be imaginable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the limitations of sensory and imaginative faculties open the door to affirming God's transcendence beyond perception.

Core Idea
What cannot perceive itself proves that not everything must be imaginable.

In this seventh argument, Imam al-Razi presents a logical proof from the very nature of our sensory and imaginative faculties. He observes that these faculties, though they perceive other things, cannot perceive themselves. This simple fact challenges the assumption that everything real must be imaginable or sensible. Let's examine how this argument opens the door to affirming God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the structure: an observation followed by a conclusion.
2
Understand the two examples
Razi uses two parallel examples: the visual faculty cannot see itself, and the imaginative faculty cannot imagine itself. Reflect on why this is significant.
3
Grasp the logical implication
Razi argues that if a faculty exists but cannot perceive itself, then it is not a requirement for every existing thing to be perceivable by that faculty. This opens the possibility that God exists beyond our perception.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
This argument directly refutes the anthropomorphist claim that what is not imaginable cannot be real. Understand how this defends God's transcendence.
5
Review the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms: القوة الباصرة, القوة الخيالية, متخيل, باب الاحتمال.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع: إنا نبصر الأشياء. إلا أن القوة الباصرة لا تبصر نفسها: وكذلك القوة الخيالية تتخيل الأشياء. إلا أن هذه القوة لا يمكنها أن تتخيل نفسها. فوجود للقوة الباصرة يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون كل شيء متخيلا. وذلك يفتح باب الاحتمال المذكور.
The seventh: We see things. However, the visual faculty does not see itself. Similarly, the imaginative faculty imagines things, but this faculty cannot imagine itself. So the existence of the visual faculty indicates that it is not necessary that everything be imaginable. And that opens the door to the mentioned possibility.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This is the seventh argument, which uses the limitations of sensory and imaginative faculties to open the possibility that God is not perceptible by sense or imagination. Razi states that we see objects, but the visual faculty (al-quwwa al-bāṣira) does not see itself. Similarly, the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-khayāliyya) imagines things, but it cannot imagine itself. This shows that it is not necessary (lā yajibu) that every object be imaginable (mutakhayyal) or sensible. If a faculty can exist without being perceptible to itself, then the fact that we cannot perceive God with our senses or imagine Him does not mean He cannot exist. This argument undermines the anthropomorphist claim that what is not imaginable or sensible cannot be real. Razi's point is that the limitation lies in the faculties, not in the object itself. Therefore, it is possible that God exists without being subject to sensory perception or imagination, just as the visual faculty exists without being seen by itself.

Key Terms
القوة الباصرة
القوة الخيالية
متخيل
باب الاحتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anything real must be imaginable.
Correction: Razi shows that the imaginative faculty itself cannot imagine itself, so being real does not require being imaginable.

Checkpoint

A) That God can be seen by the mind
B) That it is not necessary for everything to be imaginable
C) That the eye is imperfect
D) That imagination is the only way to know God

Key Takeaway
The inability of sensory and imaginative faculties to perceive themselves shows that it is not necessary for every existing thing, including God, to be imaginable or sensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you struggle to feel God's presence or imagine His essence, do you ever doubt that He is real? How does Razi's argument—that a faculty can exist without perceiving itself—confront that doubt? What will you remind yourself of next time you feel that distance?
?
Think of a time you dismissed something as impossible simply because you couldn't grasp it. How might this lesson transform the way you approach the 'unimaginable' aspects of your faith, like the attributes of God? Are you willing to let that shift happen starting today?
?
Your own mind cannot fully comprehend itself—you cannot see your own thoughts or imagine your own imagination. What does that limitation reveal about the arrogance of demanding that God fit within your mental categories? Will you humble yourself before that mystery instead of demanding that He be 'imaginable'?

Refuting the Karramiyya: The Dilemma of Sensible Pointing
Razi presents a dilemma to the Karramiyya about sensible pointing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dilemma against the Karramiyya: if God is sensible, He must be either divisible or indivisible, both of which they reject, and how Razi exposes the contradiction in their position.

Core Idea
If God were sensible, He would be either composite or a point—both impossible for Allah.

Razi presents a powerful dilemma to the Karramiyya, who claim God can be pointed to by sense. He forces them to choose between two unacceptable alternatives, and then shows that their attempted escape leads to a logical contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the translation. Let the argument's structure sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the dilemma: divisible or indivisible?
Notice that Razi presents two possibilities for any sensible object. Where do these lead?
If divisible → composite (rejected). If indivisible → point (rejected).
3
Examine the Karramiyya's response
They claim God is one, not composite, not small. How does this fit with the dilemma?
They try to escape both horns.
4
Understand Razi's refutation of their response
Razi shows that the Karramiyya's claim leads to a contradiction: either extension (leading to division) or no extension (leading to being a point).
Their position 'indivisible yet great' is a logical impossibility.
5
Connect to the broader argument about direction
Razi asks: if they accept such a contradiction, how can they reject the Ash'ari position of no direction?
This shows their inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن: إن خصومنا لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بوجود شيء على خلاف حكم الحس والخيال. وذلك لأن خصومنا في هذا الباب: إما الكرامية، وإما الحنابلة. أما الكرامية - فإنا إذا قلنا لهم لو كان الله تعالى مشاراً إليه بالحس، لكان ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون منقسما فيكون مركبا - وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك - وإما أن يكون غير منقسم، فيكون في الصغر والحفارة، مثل النقطة التي لا تنقسم، ومثل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ - وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك. وعند هذا الكلام قالوا: إنه واحد منزه عن التركيب والتأليف، ومع هذا، فإنه ليس بصغير ولا حقير. ومعلوم: أن هذا الذي التزموه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال، بل لا يقبله العقل أيضاً. لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس، إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحيان، كان أحد جانبيه مغايرا للجانب الثاني. وذلك يوجب الانقسام في بديهة العقل. وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات، لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت، كان نقطة غير منقسمة، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة. وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه غير قابل للقسمة، مع كونه عظيما، غير متناه في الامتداد (مع أن هذا جمع بين النفي والإثبات، ومدفوع في بدائه العقول) فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه - تعالى - غير حال، ولا مباين عنه بحسب الجهة: مدفوع في بدائه العقول؟ وأما الحنابلة الذين التزموا الأجزاء والأبعاض، فهم أيضاً معترفون بأن ذاته تعالى مخالف لذوات هذه المحسوسات. فإنه تعالى لا يساوي هذه الذوات في قبول الاجتماع والافتراق والتغير والفناء، والصحة والمرض، والحياة والموت. إذ لو كانت ذاته - تعالى - مساوية لسائر الذوات في هذه الصفات، لزم: إما افتقاره إلى خالق آخر - وعلى هذا يلزم التسلسل - أو يلزم القول بأن الإمكان والحدوث غير محوج إلى الخالق - وذلك يلزم منه نفي الصانع - فثبت: أنه لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بأن خصوصية ذاته
The eighth: Indeed, our adversaries cannot avoid admitting the existence of something contrary to the rule of sense and imagination. That is because our adversaries in this matter are either the Karramiyya or the Hanabila. As for the Karramiyya—when we say to them: 'If God were sensibly pointed to, then that thing would either be divisible and thus composite—and you do not say that—or it would be indivisible and would then be in smallness and lowliness, like the indivisible point and like the part that is not further divisible—and you do not say that either.' Upon this statement they say: 'He is one, transcendent over composition and arrangement, and yet He is not small nor contemptible.' It is known that what they have committed to is not accepted by sense and imagination, nor is it even accepted by reason. For something pointed to by sense, if it has extension in directions and dimensions, then one of its sides would be different from the other side, and that necessitates division by the intuition of reason. And if it has no extension in any direction, neither right nor left nor above nor below, then it would be an indivisible point, and would be in extreme smallness and contemptibility. Since they do not find it far-fetched to commit to its being indivisible while being great and infinite in extension (though this combines negation and affirmation and is refuted by the intuitions of reason), how can they rule that the statement that He—exalted is He—is neither indwelling nor distinct in terms of direction is refuted by the intuitions of reason? As for the Hanabila, who commit to parts and limbs, they too admit that His essence—exalted is He—is different from the essences of these sensible things. For He—exalted is He—does not equal these essences in undergoing combination, separation, change, annihilation, health, illness, life, and death. For if His essence—exalted is He—were equal to other essences in these attributes, it would necessitate either His need of another creator—and this would entail infinite regress—or it would necessitate the statement that possibility and temporal origination do not require a creator—and that entails the denial of the Maker. So it is established that they must admit that the specificity of His essence...
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which is a two-part refutation of anthropomorphic adversaries (the Karramiyya and the Hanabila). Razi first asserts that even the adversaries must admit the existence of something that is not subject to the laws of sense and imagination (i.e., God). He then addresses each group.

**Against the Karramiyya:** Razi presents a dilemma: If God is a sensible object (mushār ilayhi bil-ḥiss), then He must be either divisible (munqasim) or indivisible (ghayr munqasim). If divisible, He would be composite (murakkab)—which the Karramiyya reject. If indivisible, He would be like a point or an atom—extremely small and lowly—which they also reject. In response, the Karramiyya claim that God is one, free from composition, yet not small or lowly. Razi points out that this claim is rejected by sense, imagination, and even reason. He explains: if something has sensible extension in directions, it must have distinct sides, leading to division (by rational intuition). If it has no extension, it is a point, extremely small. The Karramiyya's claim that God is indivisible yet great and infinite in extension is a contradiction (jamʿ bayn al-nafy wa-l-ithbāt) that is refuted by rational intuition. Razi then asks: If they accept such a contradiction, how can they reject the claim that God is neither in-dwelling (ḥall) nor in opposition (mubāyin) with respect to direction? The implication is that their own position is more problematic.

**Against the Hanabila:** They admit that God's essence (dhāt) is different from the essences of sensible things, since God does not share with them attributes like combination, separation, change, annihilation, health, illness, life, and death. Razi argues that if God were equal to other essences in these attributes, it would lead to one of two absurdities: either God would need another creator (leading to infinite regress) or the principle that possibility and temporal origination require a creator would be false (leading to atheism). Therefore, the Hanabila must admit that God's essence has a specificity (khuṣūṣiyya) that distinguishes Him from all sensible things, which already implies that God is not subject to the laws of sense and imagination.

The passage ends mid-sentence (indicating the conclusion that God's essence is unique), but the argument is complete in its core: both schools are forced to acknowledge a transcendent reality beyond sense and imagination.

Key Terms
الكرامية
مشار إليه بالحس
منقسم
مركب
نقطة
الامتداد
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not in a direction, then He must be nonexistent.
Correction: Razi shows that even the Karramiyya, who affirm direction, end up with a contradictory position. Therefore, the Ash'ari position that God is beyond direction is more coherent and does not imply non-existence.

Checkpoint
If God had no extension in any direction, what would He be, according to Razi?
A) Immaterial
B) An indivisible point
C) Infinite in all directions
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma shows that claiming God is sensible leads to absurdities, thus God must be beyond sense and imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about Allah that, upon honest reflection, contains a contradiction you’ve ignored because it felt safer or more familiar? What is that contradiction, and will you examine it today?
?
Razi points out that the Karramiyya accepted one absurdity while rejecting another. In your own life, are there areas where you hold double standards—accepting flaws in your own positions that you would not tolerate in others? Will you repent from that inconsistency?
?
This lesson shows that reason alone can reveal deep errors in our conception of God. Are you willing to let your intellect—guided by revelation—challenge cherished assumptions you have about Allah, even if it feels uncomfortable? What will you change?

Refuting the Hanabila: God's Essence Beyond Sensible Attributes
Razi argues against the Hanabila that they must admit God's essence is unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that even the Hanabila, who attribute parts to God, must admit that God's essence is fundamentally different from sensible objects, otherwise absurdities follow.

Core Idea
If God's essence were like other essences, He would need a creator or there would be no creator.

Razi now turns to the Hanabila, who affirm that God has limbs and parts. He points out that even they must acknowledge that God's essence is not like other essences, because if it were equal in properties like change and mortality, it would lead to either infinite regress or atheism. Thus, they too are forced to admit a transcendence beyond sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Razi builds his argument.
2
Identify the Hanabila's admission of difference
The Hanabila already admit that God's essence is different from sensible things, because He does not undergo change, death, etc.
This is their own starting point.
3
Consider the condition: what if God were equal to sensible things?
Razi asks: if God's essence were equal to other essences in attributes like change and mortality, what would follow?
This is a hypothetical.
4
Follow the two absurd consequences
First: God would need a creator (leading to infinite regress). Second: the principle that contingent things need a creator would be false (leading to atheism).
Both are impossible.
5
Conclude: specificity of essence proves transcendence
Therefore, the Hanabila must admit that God's essence has a unique specificity that distinguishes it from all sensible things.
This supports the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن: إن خصومنا لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بوجود شيء على خلاف حكم الحس والخيال. وذلك لأن خصومنا في هذا الباب: إما الكرامية، وإما الحنابلة. أما الكرامية - فإنا إذا قلنا لهم لو كان الله تعالى مشاراً إليه بالحس، لكان ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون منقسما فيكون مركبا - وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك - وإما أن يكون غير منقسم، فيكون في الصغر والحفارة، مثل النقطة التي لا تنقسم، ومثل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ - وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك. وعند هذا الكلام قالوا: إنه واحد منزه عن التركيب والتأليف، ومع هذا، فإنه ليس بصغير ولا حقير. ومعلوم: أن هذا الذي التزموه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال، بل لا يقبله العقل أيضاً. لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس، إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحيان، كان أحد جانبيه مغايرا للجانب الثاني. وذلك يوجب الانقسام في بديهة العقل. وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات، لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت، كان نقطة غير منقسمة، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة. وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه غير قابل للقسمة، مع كونه عظيما، غير متناه في الامتداد (مع أن هذا جمع بين النفي والإثبات، ومدفوع في بدائه العقول) فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه - تعالى - غير حال، ولا مباين عنه بحسب الجهة: مدفوع في بدائه العقول؟ وأما الحنابلة الذين التزموا الأجزاء والأبعاض، فهم أيضاً معترفون بأن ذاته تعالى مخالف لذوات هذه المحسوسات. فإنه تعالى لا يساوي هذه الذوات في قبول الاجتماع والافتراق والتغير والفناء، والصحة والمرض، والحياة والموت. إذ لو كانت ذاته - تعالى - مساوية لسائر الذوات في هذه الصفات، لزم: إما افتقاره إلى خالق آخر - وعلى هذا يلزم التسلسل - أو يلزم القول بأن الإمكان والحدوث غير محوج إلى الخالق - وذلك يلزم منه نفي الصانع - فثبت: أنه لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بأن خصوصية ذاته
The eighth: Indeed, our adversaries cannot avoid admitting the existence of something contrary to the rule of sense and imagination. That is because our adversaries in this matter are either the Karramiyya or the Hanabila. As for the Karramiyya—when we say to them: 'If God were sensibly pointed to, then that thing would either be divisible and thus composite—and you do not say that—or it would be indivisible and would then be in smallness and lowliness, like the indivisible point and like the part that is not further divisible—and you do not say that either.' Upon this statement they say: 'He is one, transcendent over composition and arrangement, and yet He is not small nor contemptible.' It is known that what they have committed to is not accepted by sense and imagination, nor is it even accepted by reason. For something pointed to by sense, if it has extension in directions and dimensions, then one of its sides would be different from the other side, and that necessitates division by the intuition of reason. And if it has no extension in any direction, neither right nor left nor above nor below, then it would be an indivisible point, and would be in extreme smallness and contemptibility. Since they do not find it far-fetched to commit to its being indivisible while being great and infinite in extension (though this combines negation and affirmation and is refuted by the intuitions of reason), how can they rule that the statement that He—exalted is He—is neither indwelling nor distinct in terms of direction is refuted by the intuitions of reason? As for the Hanabila, who commit to parts and limbs, they too admit that His essence—exalted is He—is different from the essences of these sensible things. For He—exalted is He—does not equal these essences in undergoing combination, separation, change, annihilation, health, illness, life, and death. For if His essence—exalted is He—were equal to other essences in these attributes, it would necessitate either His need of another creator—and this would entail infinite regress—or it would necessitate the statement that possibility and temporal origination do not require a creator—and that entails the denial of the Maker. So it is established that they must admit that the specificity of His essence...
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which is a two-part refutation of anthropomorphic adversaries (the Karramiyya and the Hanabila). Razi first asserts that even the adversaries must admit the existence of something that is not subject to the laws of sense and imagination (i.e., God). He then addresses each group.

**Against the Karramiyya:** Razi presents a dilemma: If God is a sensible object (mushār ilayhi bil-ḥiss), then He must be either divisible (munqasim) or indivisible (ghayr munqasim). If divisible, He would be composite (murakkab)—which the Karramiyya reject. If indivisible, He would be like a point or an atom—extremely small and lowly—which they also reject. In response, the Karramiyya claim that God is one, free from composition, yet not small or lowly. Razi points out that this claim is rejected by sense, imagination, and even reason. He explains: if something has sensible extension in directions, it must have distinct sides, leading to division (by rational intuition). If it has no extension, it is a point, extremely small. The Karramiyya's claim that God is indivisible yet great and infinite in extension is a contradiction (jamʿ bayn al-nafy wa-l-ithbāt) that is refuted by rational intuition. Razi then asks: If they accept such a contradiction, how can they reject the claim that God is neither in-dwelling (ḥall) nor in opposition (mubāyin) with respect to direction? The implication is that their own position is more problematic.

**Against the Hanabila:** They admit that God's essence (dhāt) is different from the essences of sensible things, since God does not share with them attributes like combination, separation, change, annihilation, health, illness, life, and death. Razi argues that if God were equal to other essences in these attributes, it would lead to one of two absurdities: either God would need another creator (leading to infinite regress) or the principle that possibility and temporal origination require a creator would be false (leading to atheism). Therefore, the Hanabila must admit that God's essence has a specificity (khuṣūṣiyya) that distinguishes Him from all sensible things, which already implies that God is not subject to the laws of sense and imagination.

The passage ends mid-sentence (indicating the conclusion that God's essence is unique), but the argument is complete in its core: both schools are forced to acknowledge a transcendent reality beyond sense and imagination.

Key Terms

أجزاء وأبعاض
خصوصية الذات
التسلسل
نفي الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has parts, then He must be a physical body like us.
Correction: Razi shows that even the Hanabila, who affirm parts, must admit God's essence is fundamentally different from all physical bodies—He does not undergo change, death, etc.

Checkpoint
What is one of the absurd consequences Razi mentions if God's essence were equal to other essences?
A) God would be eternal
B) God would need a creator
C) God would be infinite
D) God would be unique

Key Takeaway
Razi forces the Hanabila to admit that God's essence is unique, proving that God is beyond all sensible attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi uses the Hanabila's own admission against them. Are there beliefs you hold that, if followed consistently, would lead to a conclusion you reject? Where might you be inconsistent in your understanding of God?
?
If someone asked you why God cannot have a body, could you explain using Razi's argument—or do you rely on feelings? Commit today to memorizing the logical steps from this passage so you can defend tawhid clearly.

Razi's Reductio Against the Anthropomorphist Objection
Razi defends the mutakallimun position via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum to defend the mutakallimun position that God is neither indwelling nor separate by showing it is no more paradoxical than the anthropomorphist's own commitment.

Core Idea
If you accept an indivisible infinite being, you cannot reject the mutakallimun position as irrational.

In the ongoing debate about God’s relationship to the world, the mutakallimun hold that God is neither inside the world (ḥall) nor outside it (mubāyin). Some opponents claim this is self-contradictory. Razi counters by pointing out that those very opponents accept an even more paradoxical notion: an indivisible infinite being. This lesson unpacks his clever reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the rhetorical question at the end.
The argument is tight; reading it twice helps.
2
Identify the two positions
Razi contrasts the mutakallimun position (God is neither indwelling nor separate) with the anthropomorphist commitment (an indivisible infinite being). Identify each in the text.
The anthropomorphists here are those who accept a bodily God but also claim He is indivisible and infinite.
3
Understand the reductio structure
Razi argues that if the anthropomorphists can accept the impossibility of an indivisible infinite being, then they cannot call the mutakallimun position rationally impossible. Trace this comparison in the commentary.
Key phrase: 'how did they judge that the statement… is repudiated by the intuitions of intellects?'
4
Reflect on the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that the mutakallimun position is obviously irrational. Razi shows it is no less rational than the opponent's own view. Consider how this changes your perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل النقطة التي لا تنقسم، ومثل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ - وأنتم لا تقولون بذلك. وعند هذا الكلام قالوا: إنه واحد منزه عن التركيب والتأليف، ومع هذا، فإنه ليس بصغير ولا حقير. ومعلوم: أن هذا الذي التزموه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال، بل لا يقبله العقل أيضاً. لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس، إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحيان، كان أحد جانبيه مغايرا للجانب الثانى. وذلك يوجب الانقسام في بديهة العقل. وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات، لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت، كان نقطة غير منقسمة، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة. وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه غير قابل للقسمة، مع كونه عظيما، غير متناه في الامتداد، مع أن هذا جمع بين النفي والإثبات، ومدفوع في بدائه العقول، فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه - تعالى - غير حال، ولا مباين عنه بحسب الجهة: مدفوع في بدائه العقول؟
Like the point that does not divide, and like the part that does not break apart—and you do not say that. And at this statement they said: He is one, transcendent from composition and arrangement, and despite this, He is not small or insignificant. And it is known that what they committed to is not accepted by sense and imagination, nor even by reason. Because that which is pointed to by sense, if it obtains extension in directions and dimensions, one side of it is different from the other side, and that necessitates division in the intuition of reason. And if it does not obtain extension in any of the directions, not in the right nor in the left nor in the above nor in the below, it would be an indivisible point, and would be extremely small and insignificant. And if they did not find it far-fetched to commit to His being indivisible, while being great, infinite in extension, although this combines negation and affirmation, and is repudiated by the intuitions of intellects, then how did they judge that the statement that He—exalted—is neither indwelling nor separate with respect to direction is repudiated by the intuitions of intellects?
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by reductio ad absurdum. Razi presents the position of those who affirm that God is neither indwelling (ḥall) in the world nor separate (mubāyin) from it by direction—the position of the mutakallimūn. He then compares it to an even more paradoxical commitment: accepting an indivisible yet infinite being (as some anthropomorphists do). He argues that if they accept the latter despite its contradiction (combining indivisibility with infinite extension), then they cannot consistently reject the former as self-contradictory. The argument proceeds: a sensible object either has extension and thus divides, or lacks extension and is a point, small and insignificant. To claim an infinite being is indivisible combines affirmation and negation, yet it is not considered impossible by them. So their claim that the mutakallimūn's position is repudiated by reason is hypocritical.

Key Terms
نقطة
انقسام
حلول
مباينة
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun position that God is neither indwelling nor separate is obviously irrational and self-contradictory.
Correction: Razi shows that this position is no more contradictory than the anthropomorphist's own acceptance of an indivisible infinite being, which combines affirmation and negation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what inconsistency do the anthropomorphists commit when they reject the mutakallimun's position?
A) They accept an indivisible infinite being, which combines affirmation and negation.
B) They deny that God is small.
C) They reject sense perception.
D) They affirm that God has parts.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses a reductio to show that the anthropomorphists' objection to the mutakallimun position is hypocritical, as they themselves accept a more paradoxical doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When your 'common sense' or intuition tells you a doctrine is false, do you pause to check if that intuition is aligned with the logical implications of your own commitments? This lesson shows that even a 'self-evident' intuition can be inconsistent. What will you do to test your intuitions against rigorous reasoning?
?
If someone pointed out a contradiction in your belief about God, would you be more likely to defend it emotionally or to reconsider it rationally? Razi uses reason to expose hypocrisy. Are you prepared to accept a logical critique even if it challenges a deeply held assumption?

Imagination Cannot Grasp God's Essence
Razi argues that imagination fails to grasp God, so God can be without spatial relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi uses the failure of imagination to argue for God's transcendence beyond spatial categories.

Core Idea
Imagination's limits are evidence of a higher reality, not a proof of impossibility.

In this passage, Razi makes a concise argument about divine transcendence. He points out that our imagination cannot fully grasp God's essence, and this very limitation serves as a starting point for affirming that God exists in a way that surpasses our ordinary concepts of location and direction. Let's examine how he builds this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Razi.
2
Identify the key claim about imagination
Razi states that imagination (al-wahm) and fantasy (al-khayal) do not reach God's essence (kunnah). This is presented as a fact.
3
Understand the 'admission' argument
Razi says this failure is an 'admission' (iʿtiraf) that there is something contrary to what imagination rules. This turns a limitation into evidence.
Think about why he uses the word 'admission'.
4
Apply the principle to spatial relation
Razi then asks: if imagination cannot conceive something, that doesn't make it impossible. So why would we be surprised that God exists without being inside or outside the world?
This is the conclusion of the passage.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument defends divine transcendence (tanzih) and challenges the tendency to equate imaginative conceivability with possibility.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢١) لا يصل الوهم والخيال إلى كنهها . وذلك اعتراف بثبوت أمر على خلاف ما يحكم به الوهم ويقضى به الخيال . . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فأى استبعاد في وجود موجود غير حال في العالم ولا مباين بالجهة للعالم ، وإن كان الوهم والخيال لا يمكنهما إدراك هذا الموجود ؟
(21) Imagination and fantasy do not reach its essence. That is an admission of the existence of something contrary to what imagination rules and fantasy decrees. And if this is the case, what improbability is there in the existence of an existent that is neither indwelling in the world nor separate from it by direction, even if imagination and fantasy cannot perceive this existent?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument for divine transcendence. Razi states that imagination (al-wahm) and fantasy (al-khayāl) cannot comprehend God's essence (kunnah). He notes that this very limitation is an admission that there is a reality contrary to the judgments of imagination. Applying this principle: it is not far-fetched that God exists without being 'in' the world or 'separated' from it by direction (jiha). The inability of imagination to conceive such a mode of existence does not invalidate it.

Key Terms
الوهم
الخيال
الكنه
المباينة بالجهة
غير حال في العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagination's inability to conceive something is a proof of its impossibility.
Correction: Razi argues the opposite: the failure of imagination is an admission that there is a reality beyond its grasp, so impossibility cannot be inferred from imaginative inconceivability.

Checkpoint

A) Because it proves that God does not exist.
B) Because it shows that there is a reality beyond imagination's grasp.
C) Because it indicates that imagination is the ultimate judge of reality.
D) Because it implies that God must be like created things.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the inability of imagination to grasp God's essence actually supports the possibility of God existing beyond spatial categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You often rely on your imagination to make sense of the unseen—angels, the afterlife, even God. But Razi says imagination cannot reach God's essence. How has your mental image of God actually limited your worship or caused you to think of Him in human terms? Tonight, in your prayer, will you consciously turn away from every image of God and submit to the One whom no image can contain?
?
Razi argues that our inability to conceive something is an 'admission' of a reality beyond us. Think of a time you rejected a truth because it seemed 'far-fetched' to your imagination. How might you need to humble your imagination today to accept a difficult teaching about Allah's attributes—even if you cannot picture it?

Razi's Hanbali Argument for Transcendence
Razi uses Hanbali methodology to argue for spatial transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Hanbali methodology to support the doctrine that God exists neither inside nor outside the world, despite being inconceivable to imagination.

Core Idea
If Hanbalis accept unimaginable attributes, accept unimaginable spatial transcendence.

Razi presents a clever argument by analogy with Hanbali practice. He notes that Hanbalis, when encountering verses that suggest bodily parts, affirm the attributes but in a way utterly unlike creation—something imagination cannot grasp. Razi then applies this same logic to the question of God's spatial relationship to the world: just as Hanbalis accept an inconceivable face and hand, so should we accept that God exists without being inside or outside the world, even if imagination fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow: Hanbali method → example of face/hand → conclusion about spatial transcendence.
The passage is dense; let the argument build naturally.
2
Identify the Hanbali methodological principle
Notice the phrase 'they declare: We affirm this meaning for God Almighty in a way contrary to how it is established for creation.' This is the key principle: affirming attributes while denying any likeness to creation.
This principle is central to the argument.
3
See the example of face and hand
Razi gives concrete examples: face and hand. Hanbalis affirm these attributes but in a way that is beyond human imagination. Understand that this is not anthropomorphism; it is an affirmation of real attributes that are inconceivable.
Imagination cannot picture a 'face unlike faces'.
4
Understand the analogical leap
Razi then asks: if we can accept that for attributes, why not accept that God exists without being inside or outside the world? The same limitation of imagination applies. This is the punchline.
The argument targets those who reject spatial transcendence as unimaginable.
5
Reflect on the wider implication
Consider how this argument uses an opponent's own method to refute them. It does not endorse Hanbali theology but shows consistency: if you accept one inconceivable, you must accept another.
This is a common dialectical strategy in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فعمدة مذهب الحنابلة أنهم متى تمسكوا بآية أو بخبر يوهم ظاهره شيئا من الأعضاء والجوارح ، صرحوا بأنا نثبت هذا المعنى الله تعالى على خلاف ما هو ثابت للخلق . فأثبتوا الله - تعالى - وجهاء على خلاف وجوه الخلق ، ويداً على خلاف أيدي الخلق . ومعلوم أن اليد والوجه بالمعنى الذى ذكروه مما لا يقبله الخيال والوهم . فإذا عقل إثبات ذلك على خلاف الوهم والخيال . فأى استبعاد في القول بأنه تعالى موجود ، وليس داخل العالم ولا خارج العالم . وإن كان الوهم والخيال قاصرين عن إدراك هذا الموجود ؟
And also: the foundation of the Hanbali school is that whenever they adhere to a verse or a report whose apparent meaning suggests something of limbs and organs, they declare: 'We affirm this meaning for God Almighty in a way contrary to how it is established for creation.' So they affirm for God - exalted - a face unlike the faces of creation, and a hand unlike the hands of creation. And it is known that the hand and the face in the sense they mention are not accepted by fantasy and imagination. If, then, it is intelligible to affirm that contrary to imagination and fantasy, what improbability is there in saying that He - exalted - exists, and is neither inside the world nor outside it, even if imagination and fantasy are incapable of perceiving this existent?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by analogy with Hanbali methodology. Razi notes that the Hanbalis, when encountering verses that suggest bodily parts, explicitly state that they affirm the attribute (e.g., face, hand) but in a manner incomparable to creation. Such affirmations are not conceivable by imagination (al-wahm) and fantasy (al-khayāl). Yet they accept them. By the same token, one should accept that God exists without being spatially related to the world—neither inside nor outside—even if imagination cannot picture it. The argument is a redirection against anthropomorphists: if you accept the inconceivable for attributes, you should accept it for spatial transcendence.

على خلاف ما هو ثابت للخلق

خارج العالم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage suggests that Hanbalis are anthropomorphists.
Correction: Razi is not endorsing anthropomorphism; he is using their own method to show that even they accept what is inconceivable, thus arguing for spatial transcendence.

D) They are only for the prophets.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses Hanbali acceptance of inconceivable attributes to argue for accepting God's existence without spatial location, challenging the imagination's limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt uncomfortable with an Islamic belief because it seemed too abstract or beyond your imagination. Did you reject it, or did you seek to understand it on its own terms? How does Razi's argument challenge you to lean into what your mind cannot fully picture?

Razi says that the Hanbalis accept attributes like 'hand' and 'face' even though they are unimaginable. Are there aspects of God that you have implicitly tried to shrink down to something imaginable? What would it mean for you to let go of that need and say, 'I affirm it, but I do not know how'?
?
If you were to apply the same logic from this argument to your daily relationship with God, where might you be limiting Him by insisting on a spatial or emotional 'location' for Him in your life? Write down one concrete step you can take this week to worship a God who is 'neither inside nor outside' your limited categories.

The Anthropomorphist Argument for Direction
Razi presents the anthropomorphist argument for direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) argue that God must be separate from the world by direction, based on a dichotomy of indwelling or separation.

Core Idea
Imagination forces a false choice: either indwelling or separation by direction.

Imam Razi begins the ninth argument by presenting the reasoning of the anthropomorphists (ahl al-tashbih). They claim that two existents must be either one indwelling in the other or separate by direction. Since indwelling is impossible for God, they conclude He is separate by direction, implying He occupies space. This lesson examines their claim and the dichotomy they assume.

Guided Path
1
Read the anthropomorphist argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the dichotomy they propose: two existents are either indwelling or separate.
This dichotomy is presented as self-evident (ma'lum bil-darura).
2
Identify the conclusion
Find how they rule out indwelling (hulul) and conclude separation by direction (mubayana bil-jiha).
This leads to attributing space and direction to Allah.
3
Reflect on the role of imagination
Consider that Razi is setting this up for refutation. Why might this dichotomy be flawed?
Hint: imagination (wahm) imposes divisions that may not apply to the Divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن أهل التشبيه قالوا : العالم والبارى موجودان وكل موجودين فإما أن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه . قالوا : والقول بوجوب هذا الحصر معلوم بالضرورة . قالوا : والقول بالحلول محال فتعين كونه مبايناً للعالم بالجهة. وبهذا الطريق احتجوا بكونه تعالى مختصا بالحيز والجهة . وأهل الدهر . قالوا : العالم والبارى موجودان. وكل موجودين فإما أن يكون وجودهما معاً أو أحدهما قبل الآخر . ومحال أن العالم والبارى معا . وإلا لزم . إما قدم العالم ، أو حدوث الباري . وهمه محالان. فثبت : أن الباري قبل العالم . ثم قالوا : والعلم الضروري حاصل. بأن هذه القبلية لا تكون إلا بالزمان والمدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فتقدم البارى على العالم إن كان بمدة متناهية ، لزم حدوث البارى . وإن كان بمدة لا أول لها ، لزم كون المدة قديمة . فأنتجوا بهذا الطريق: قدم المدة والزمان.
The ninth: The anthropomorphists said: 'The world and the Creator are two existents, and whenever there are two existents, either one is indwelling in the other or separate from it.' They said: 'The necessity of this exhaustive division is known necessarily.' They said: 'Indwelling is impossible, so it is determined that He is separate from the world by direction.' By this way they argued that He - exalted - is specific to space and direction. The materialists said: 'The world and the Creator are two existents, and whenever there are two existents, either their existence is together or one is before the other. It is impossible that the world and the Creator are together, otherwise it would necessitate either the pre-eternity of the world or the temporal origination of the Creator. Both are impossible. So it is established that the Creator is before the world. Then they said: 'Necessary knowledge obtains that this priority is only by time and duration. If this is established, then the Creator's precedence over the world: if it is by a finite duration, it necessitates the temporal origination of the Creator; if it is by an infinite duration, it necessitates that duration is pre-eternal.' So they concluded by this way the pre-eternity of duration and time.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from different groups, both relying on what imagination (al-wahm) considers necessary. First, the anthropomorphists (ahl al-tashbīh) argue that two existents must be either indwelling or separate by direction; since indwelling is impossible for God, He must be separate by direction (jiha), implying He occupies space. Second, the materialists (ahl al-dahr) argue that two existents must be either coexistent or sequential; since coexistence is impossible, God precedes the world. They then claim that priority must be by time (zamān) and duration (mudda), leading to the pre-eternity of duration. Both arguments assume that the dichotomies they propose are exhaustive and necessary, a point Razi will later refute.

Key Terms
التشبيه
الحلول
المباينة بالجهة
الحيز
الجهة
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must be either indwelling in creation or separate from it by a direction.
Correction: This dichotomy is based on physical objects and does not apply to God, who is beyond space and direction.

Checkpoint
What do the anthropomorphists claim about the dichotomy of indwelling and separation?
A) It is only probable.
B) It is known necessarily.
C) It is based on revelation.
D) It is uncertain.

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphists use a false dichotomy to argue that God occupies space and direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God in your mind, do you unconsciously assign Him a direction or space? How does this argument expose your own reliance on imagination? Today, commit to purifying your concept of God using only what He has revealed, not your mental images.
?
This argument feels logical at first glance. Have you ever accepted a seemingly rational conclusion about God that actually limits Him? What will you do to ensure your intellect remains subordinate to revelation when thinking about Allah?
