The Materialist Argument for Eternal Duration
Razi presents the materialist argument for eternal time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the materialists (dahriyya) argue for the pre-eternity of time and duration using a dichotomy of coexistence or priority.

Core Idea
Imagination imposes time on God, leading to the pre-eternity of duration.

Razi then presents the materialist argument. They claim that two existents must be either coexistent or sequential, and since coexistence is impossible for God and the world, God must precede the world. But they then argue that priority must be by time and duration, leading to a dilemma: if the duration is finite, God becomes temporal; if infinite, duration is eternal. They conclude that duration and time are pre-eternal. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the materialist argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: a dichotomy, elimination, and a disjunction about duration.
The materialists are known as dahriyya.
2
Analyze the dilemma
They propose that God precedes the world. Then they ask: is this precedence by a finite or infinite duration? Both options lead to conclusions they accept: either God is temporal (if finite) or duration is eternal (if infinite).
They choose the latter, concluding duration is pre-eternal.
3
Identify the flaw
Consider why Razi includes this argument. What assumption do they make?
They assume priority always involves time, but for God, priority is timeless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن أهل التشبيه قالوا : العالم والبارى موجودان وكل موجودين فإما أن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه . قالوا : والقول بوجوب هذا الحصر معلوم بالضرورة . قالوا : والقول بالحلول محال فتعين كونه مبايناً للعالم بالجهة. وبهذا الطريق احتجوا بكونه تعالى مختصا بالحيز والجهة . وأهل الدهر . قالوا : العالم والبارى موجودان. وكل موجودين فإما أن يكون وجودهما معاً أو أحدهما قبل الآخر . ومحال أن العالم والبارى معا . وإلا لزم . إما قدم العالم ، أو حدوث الباري . وهمه محالان. فثبت : أن الباري قبل العالم . ثم قالوا : والعلم الضروري حاصل. بأن هذه القبلية لا تكون إلا بالزمان والمدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فتقدم البارى على العالم إن كان بمدة متناهية ، لزم حدوث البارى . وإن كان بمدة لا أول لها ، لزم كون المدة قديمة . فأنتجوا بهذا الطريق: قدم المدة والزمان.
The ninth: The anthropomorphists said: 'The world and the Creator are two existents, and whenever there are two existents, either one is indwelling in the other or separate from it.' They said: 'The necessity of this exhaustive division is known necessarily.' They said: 'Indwelling is impossible, so it is determined that He is separate from the world by direction.' By this way they argued that He - exalted - is specific to space and direction. The materialists said: 'The world and the Creator are two existents, and whenever there are two existents, either their existence is together or one is before the other. It is impossible that the world and the Creator are together, otherwise it would necessitate either the pre-eternity of the world or the temporal origination of the Creator. Both are impossible. So it is established that the Creator is before the world. Then they said: 'Necessary knowledge obtains that this priority is only by time and duration. If this is established, then the Creator's precedence over the world: if it is by a finite duration, it necessitates the temporal origination of the Creator; if it is by an infinite duration, it necessitates that duration is pre-eternal.' So they concluded by this way the pre-eternity of duration and time.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from different groups, both relying on what imagination (al-wahm) considers necessary. First, the anthropomorphists (ahl al-tashbīh) argue that two existents must be either indwelling or separate by direction; since indwelling is impossible for God, He must be separate by direction (jiha), implying He occupies space. Second, the materialists (ahl al-dahr) argue that two existents must be either coexistent or sequential; since coexistence is impossible, God precedes the world. They then claim that priority must be by time (zamān) and duration (mudda), leading to the pre-eternity of duration. Both arguments assume that the dichotomies they propose are exhaustive and necessary, a point Razi will later refute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's precedence over creation must involve time and duration.
Correction: God is prior in essence, not in time; priority does not require temporal sequence.

Checkpoint
What do the materialists claim about the nature of priority?
A) It can be non-temporal.
B) It is always by time and duration.
C) It is unknown.
D) It is only by will.

Key Takeaway
The materialists erroneously conclude that time is eternal because they imagine God's priority as temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Will you now examine whether they come from revelation or from your own imagination? What practical step can you take today to replace such assumptions with what Allah and His Messenger have taught?
?
When you think of Allah's existence before the world, do you feel a sense of distance? This lesson shows that our concepts of time cannot contain Him. How does this truth change the way you approach Allah in your prayers and reliance upon Him? Right now, commit to one specific way you will relate to Allah as the Eternal who is beyond time.

Razi's Refutation: The Dilemma of Imagination
Razi refutes anthropomorphists and materialists via a dilemma on the authority of imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a dilemma about the authority of imagination to refute both anthropomorphists and materialists simultaneously.

Core Idea
If imagination is accepted, both sides contradict themselves; if rejected, their arguments collapse.

Imam Razi presents a powerful refutation of two opposing theological errors: anthropomorphism and materialism. He shows that both groups rely on imagination (al-khayāl) to make their claims about God. By posing a simple dilemma, Razi demonstrates that either way, their positions are untenable. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Summarize the positions
Identify what each group claims: anthropomorphists say separation from the world must be by direction; materialists say priority must be by time.
2
Pose the dilemma
Razi asks: Is the ruling of imagination accepted regarding God or not? This is the key fork in the argument.
3
If accepted: contradiction
Work through what happens if imagination is accepted. Each group is forced into the other's conclusion, which they reject, so they contradict themselves.
Anthropomorphists must accept eternal time; materialists must accept spatial location.
4
If rejected: premises collapse
If imagination is not accepted, then both groups' foundational claims are baseless imaginative statements. Their arguments fall apart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : حاصل هذا الكلام : أن المشبهة زعمت أن مباينة البارى تعالى عن العالم ، لا يعقل حصولها إلا بالجهة . وأنتجوا منه : كون الإله في الجهة. وزعمت الدهرية : أن تقدم البارى تعالى على العالم، لا يعقل حصوله إلا بالزمان . وأنتجوا منه : قدم المدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : حكم الخيال فى حق الله تعالى ، إما أن يكون مقبولا أو غير مقبول . فإن كان مقبولا ، فالمشبهة يلزم عليهم مذهب الدهرية ، وهو أن يكون البارى تعالى متقدما على العالم بمدة غير متناهية ، ويلزمهم القول بكون الزمان أزلياً . والمشبهة لا يقولون بذلك . والدهرية يلزم عليهم مذهب المشبهة - . وهو مباينة البارى تعالى عن العالم بالجهة والمكان فيلزمهم القول بكون البارى تعالى مكانيا - وهم لا يقولون به - فصار هذا التناقض واردا على الفريقين . و أما إن قلنا : حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول البتة في ذات الله تعالى وفي صفاته ، فحينئذ تقول : قول المشبهة : إن كل موجودين فلابد وأن يكون أحدهما حالا فى الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة : قول خيالى باطل ، وقول الدهرية بأن تقدم البارى تعالى على العالم ، لا بد وأن يكون بالمدة
We say: the summary of this discourse is that the anthropomorphists claimed that the separation of the Creator - exalted - from the world is only intelligible by direction. And they concluded from it that God is in a direction. And the materialists claimed that the priority of the Creator - exalted - over the world is only intelligible by time. And they concluded from it the pre-eternity of duration. And when this is established, we say: the ruling of fantasy regarding God - exalted - is either accepted or not accepted. If it is accepted, then the anthropomorphists are necessarily committed to the position of the materialists, which is that the Creator - exalted - precedes the world by an infinite duration, and they are committed to saying that time is eternal, but the anthropomorphists do not say that. And the materialists are necessarily committed to the position of the anthropomorphists, which is the separation of the Creator - exalted - from the world by direction and place, so they are committed to saying that the Creator - exalted - is spatial, but they do not say that. So this contradiction applies to both parties. And if we say: the ruling of imagination and fantasy is absolutely not accepted regarding the essence of God - exalted - and His attributes, then you say: the statement of the anthropomorphists that 'every two existents must have one indwelling in the other or separate by direction' is a false imaginative statement, and the statement of the materialists that the priority of the Creator over the world must be by duration
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is the refutation proper. Razi summarizes the positions of both groups: anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) say separation from the world must be by direction; materialists (dahriyya) say priority must be by time. He then poses a dilemma: either the ruling of fantasy (al-khayāl) is accepted regarding God or not. If accepted, each group is forced to accept the other's conclusion – anthropomorphists must admit infinite time (making time eternal), materialists must admit spatial location – but both reject those conclusions, so they contradict themselves. If fantasy is not accepted at all regarding God's essence and attributes, then both groups' premises collapse: the anthropomorphists' dichotomy (indwelling or separation by direction) is false, and the materialists' claim that priority must be by duration is likewise baseless. Razi's resolution is to reject imaginative constructs as authoritative in theology.

Key Terms
المشبهة
الدهرية
الخيال
الوهم
المباينة بالجهة
الزمان
المدة
التناقض
قول خيالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is rejecting all rational argument in theology.
Correction: Razi is specifically rejecting the authority of imagination (khayāl) and fantasy (wahm), not rational demonstration or revelation. He uses rational argument (the dilemma) to show that imagination leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
What happens if the ruling of fantasy (al-khayāl) is accepted regarding God?
A) Both mushabbiha and dahriyya are consistent.
B) Both groups are forced into positions they reject, creating a contradiction.
C) Only mushabbiha are refuted.
D) Only dahriyya are refuted.

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma shows that reliance on imagination in theology leads either to self-contradiction or to the collapse of the argument itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to picture God, do you unconsciously assign Him a location or a time? How does Razi's dilemma expose that as reliance on imagination? Will you actively train your mind to submit to tanzih instead?
?
Are there any comfortable assumptions you hold about God that, if examined, are based merely on your own mental pictures rather than revelation and reason? Would you be willing to let go of those pictures today, even if it feels unsettling?
?
Razi says imagination is absolutely not accepted regarding God. Is there any part of your worship or belief that depends on an imagined version of God? What would it cost you to replace that with pure submission to His transcendence?

Karramiyya positions on God's relation to the Throne
Razi enumerates three Karramiyya positions on God's relation to the Throne and their internal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct positions held by the Karramiyya regarding God's spatial relation to the Throne and their internal disagreement on the nature of that specification.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya affirm spatial location for God without limbs, but disagree on how.

This lesson introduces the Karramiyya school's specific doctrine on God's relation to the Throne. Imam al-Razi carefully distinguishes their view from crude anthropomorphism and then enumerates the three possible interpretations that emerged among them. You will see how a single theological stance can branch into multiple sub-positions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Karramiyya's basic claim
Read the passage and note that the Karramiyya deny limbs but affirm that God is specifically associated with what is above the Throne.
This distinguishes them from anthropomorphists who affirm bodily parts.
2
Learn the three possible interpretations
Identify the three positions: contact (mulaq), finite separation (muba'in bi-bu'd mutanah), and infinite separation (muba'in bi-bu'd ghayr mutanah).
Each position has a group of adherents among the Karramiyya.
3
Note the further disagreement
Understand that there is an additional dispute: whether God's specification to the Throne-direction is by His essence (li-dhatihi) or by a pre-eternal attribute (li-ma'na qadim).
This disagreement cuts across the three positions.
4
Reflect on Razi's presentation
Observe that Razi reports these views without endorsement, marking them as part of the preliminary discussions before presenting his own arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكرامية فهم لا يقولون بالأعضاء والجوارح، بل يقولون: إنه مختص بما فوق العرش. ثم إن هذا المذهب يحتمل وجوهاً ثلاثة: فإنه تعالى إما أن يقال إنه ملاق للعرش. وإما أن يقال: إنه مباين منه ببعد متناه. وإما أن يقال: إنه مباين عنه ببعد غير متناه. وقد ذهب إلى كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة طائفة من الكرامية واختلفوا أيضاً: في أنه تعالى مختص بتلك الجهات لذاته، أو لمعنى قديم؟ بينهم اختلاف في ذلك. فهذا تمام الكلام في المقدمات وبالله التوفيق.
As for the Karramiyya, they do not affirm limbs and bodily parts, but rather they say: He is specifically associated with what is above the Throne. Then this school of thought entails three aspects: either it is said that He is touching the Throne, or it is said that He is separated from it by a finite distance, or it is said that He is separated from it by an infinite distance. And a group of the Karramiyya adopted each of these three divisions, and they also disagreed: whether He is specifically associated with those directions by His essence or by a pre-eternal attribute? There is disagreement among them about that. This is the completion of the discussion on the preliminaries, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a summary and classification of the Karramiyya theological school's views on God's spatial relation to the Throne. Imam al-Razi first distinguishes the Karramiyya from anthropomorphists who affirm bodily limbs: the Karramiyya deny limbs and body parts (al-a'da' wa al-jawarih). Instead, they affirm that God is specifically associated with (mukhtass bi) what is above the Throne. Then Razi explains that this position itself branches into three possibilities: (1) God is in contact (mulaq) with the Throne; (2) God is separated from it by a finite distance (muba'in bi-bu'd mutanah); (3) God is separated from it by an infinite distance (muba'in bi-bu'd ghayr mutanah). Each of these three has been held by a subgroup of the Karramiyya. Beyond this, there is a further internal disagreement: whether God's specification to the Throne-direction is by His essence (li-dhatihi) or by a pre-eternal attribute (li-ma'na qadim). Razi reports this dispute without endorsing any view. He then closes the section, marking that this concludes the preliminary discussions (al-muqaddimat) of the book.

Key Terms
كرامية
عرش
ملاق
مباين
بعد متناه
بعد غير متناه
اختصاص بالذات
معنى قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya affirm bodily limbs for God.
Correction: They explicitly deny limbs (al-a'da' wa al-jawarih) but affirm spatial location without limbs.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three Karramiyya positions mentioned regarding God's relation to the Throne?
A) Contact with the Throne
B) Separation by finite distance
C) Separation by infinite distance
D) God is identical with the Throne

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya, while denying anthropomorphism, diverged on whether God is in contact with the Throne, separated by finite or infinite distance, and on whether His specification is essential or attributive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya tried to balance affirming Allah's connection to the Throne while denying limbs. In your own understanding, where do you feel tension between Allah's majesty and His nearness? What step can you take this week to strengthen your conviction in the correct understanding from the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
If you held the view that Allah is separated from His creation by an infinite distance, would that change how you feel when you call upon Him? Write down a specific way you will remind yourself of Allah's closeness despite His transcendence in your daily prayers.
?
The Karramiyya debated whether Allah's specification to the Throne is by His essence or an attribute. Have you ever pondered the meaning of 'Allah is above'? This week, when you recite 'Allahu Akbar', pause and reflect on what it truly implies about His transcendence. Commit to one moment of deeper reflection in your next prayer.

First Scriptural Proof from Surah Al-Ikhlas
Razi introduces the second chapter and begins the first scriptural proof from Surah Al-Ikhlas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses Surah Al-Ikhlas as a clear textual proof for God's transcendence over body, space, and direction, and how he establishes its authority by classifying it as muhkamat.

Core Idea
Surah Al-Ikhlas is the decisive answer to the question of God's nature.

Imam al-Razi opens the second chapter of Asas al-Taqdis by turning to scriptural proofs. He selects Surah Al-Ikhlas as the first evidence, noting that it was revealed as a direct answer to a question about God's essence. This lesson explores the introductory context and Razi's crucial methodological claim that the sura belongs to the clear (muhkamat) verses, which gives it decisive authority. We then see the beginning of his argument from the word 'Ahad'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: chapter introduction, proof citation, context, muhkamat argument, and initial deduction.
The passage ends mid-argument; the full proof continues.
2
Identify the context of revelation
Razi mentions that the Prophet was asked about the quiddity, attribute, and description of God, and awaited revelation. This context frames the sura as a direct answer.
Knowing this context helps understand why Razi insists on its clarity.
3
Understand the muhkamat argument
Razi argues that since the sura was revealed in response to a question at a time of need, it must be among the clear (muhkamat) verses, not ambiguous (mutashabihat). This establishes its authority.
This is a key methodological move: any interpretation contradicting the sura is false.
4
Follow the deduction from 'Ahad'
Razi claims that 'Ahad' negates corporeality, spatial location, and direction. He begins explaining that a body is composed of two substances, implying that absolute oneness precludes composition.
The argument is incomplete here; you will learn the full reasoning in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في تقرير الدلائل السمعية على أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحيز والجهة. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: قل هو الله أحد. الله الصمد لم يلد ولم يولد. ولم يكن له كفواً أحد. واعلم: أنه قد اشتهر في التفسير: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن ماهية ربه، وعن نعته وصفته؟ فانتظر الجواب من الله تعالى فأنزل الله سبحانه وتعالى هذه السورة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: هذه السورة يجب أن تكون من المحكمات، لا من المتشابهات. لأنه تعالى جعلها جواباً عن سؤال السائل وأنزلها عند الحاجة. وذلك يقتضى كونها من المحكمات، لا من المتشابهات. وإذا ثبت هذا، وجب الجزم بأن كل مذهب يخالف هذه السورة يكون باطلا. فنقول: إن قوله تعالى: أحد، يدل على نفى الجسمية، ونفى الحيز والجهة. أما دلالته على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم، فذلك لأن الجسم أقله أن يكون مركبا من جوهرين، وذلك
The Second Chapter: On establishing the scriptural proofs that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent over corporeality, spatial location, and direction. And it is indicated by several proofs: The First Proof: His saying: Say: He, God, is One. God, the Eternal Refuge. He neither begets nor is born. Nor is there to Him any equivalent. And know: it is well-known in tafsir that the Prophet, peace be upon him, was asked about the quiddity of his Lord, and about His attribute and description? So he awaited the answer from God, then God, glorified and exalted, revealed this sura. If you understand this, we say: This sura must be among the clear (muhkamat), not among the ambiguous (mutashabihat). Because He, exalted, made it an answer to the questioner's question and revealed it at the time of need. And that requires it to be among the clear, not among the ambiguous. And if this is established, it is necessary to assert that every school that contradicts this sura is false. So we say: His saying 'One' indicates the negation of corporeality, and the negation of spatial location and direction. As for its indication that He is not a body, that is because the minimum of a body is that it be composed of two substances (jawharayn) and that...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the second chapter of Asas al-Taqdis, where Imam al-Razi presents scriptural proofs (dalā'il sam'iyya) for God's transcendence over body, space, and direction. He begins by stating the chapter's purpose. The first proof is from Surah Al-Ikhlas. Razi first provides the context: it is well-known in tafsir that the Prophet was asked about God's quiddity (māhiyya), attribute (na't), and description (sifa), and he awaited revelation, whereupon this sura was sent down. Razi then argues that since the sura was revealed as a direct answer to a question about God's nature at a time of need, it must belong to the category of muhkamat (clear and decisive verses) rather than mutashabihat (ambiguous verses). This is a crucial methodological move: if the sura is muhkamat, then any theological position that contradicts it is false. Razi then extracts the key term 'Ahad' from the sura and asserts that it negates corporeality (jismiyya), spatial location (hayyiz), and direction (jiha). He then begins to explain the negation of body: the minimum of a body is composition from two substances (jawharayn). The argument is that 'Ahad' implies absolute oneness, which precludes composition. The passage ends abruptly here, but the direction of the proof is clear: oneness negates composition, hence negates body.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'Ahad' only negates numerical multiplicity (i.e., God is one, not three) but does not negate physical attributes.
Correction: Imam al-Razi argues that 'Ahad' also negates composition, which is the basis of corporeality. Thus, it negates body, space, and direction, not just multiplicity of gods.

Checkpoint

C) Because all verses of the Quran are muhkamat
D) Because it contains no difficult vocabulary

Key Takeaway
Surah Al-Ikhlas is a decisive clear proof that God is not a body, not in a place, and not in a direction, as demonstrated by the word 'Ahad'.

Ahad Negates Corporeality: The Kalam Proof from Surah al-Ikhlas
Imam Razi argues from 'Ahad' to negate corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the word 'Ahad' to prove that Allah is not a body, based on the incompatibility between absolute oneness and composition.

Core Idea
The intensified oneness of 'Ahad' excludes even the minimal composition of a body.

Imam Razi presents a concise but powerful argument from the Qur'an itself. The word 'Ahad' in Surah al-Ikhlas is not just any word for 'one'—it is an intensified form that implies a oneness so absolute that it cannot coexist with any form of composition or multiplicity. This passage unpacks that argument, showing why the simplest definition of a body (composition from two substances) directly contradicts the divine oneness proclaimed in the surah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi uses a very direct and compressed style.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the central terms: 'Ahad' (أحد), 'jismiyyah' (الجسمية), 'jawharayn' (جوهرين), 'wahidiyyah' (الواحدية), and 'mubalaghah' (مبالغة). Understanding these will unlock the logic.
'Ahad' is an intensified form of 'one'.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow Razi's argument: claim (Ahad negates corporeality), definition (body = composite of two substances), contradiction (composition conflicts with oneness), conclusion (Ahad's intensified oneness rules out body).
This is a simple but rigorous logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: إن قوله تعالى: أحد، يدل على نفي الجسمية، ونفي الحيز والجهة. أما دلالته على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم، فذلك لأن الجسم أقله أن يكون مركبا من جوهرين، وذلك منافٍ للواحدية. ولما كان قوله أحد مبالغة في الواحدية، كان قوله أحد منافياً للجسمية.
So we say: Indeed His statement, exalted, 'Ahad' indicates the negation of corporeality, and the negation of spatial location and direction. As for its indication that He, exalted, is not a body, that is because the minimum of a body is that it be composed of two substances (jawharayn), and that conflicts with oneness. And since His statement 'Ahad' is an intensification of oneness, His statement 'Ahad' was contradictory to corporeality.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof that the word 'Ahad' in Surah al-Ikhlas negates that Allah is a body. The author explains that the minimal definition of a body is that it is composed of at least two substances (jawharayn). Anything composite is not truly one in an absolute sense, because it has parts. The word 'Ahad' is an intensified form of 'one' (mubalaghah fi al-wahidiyyah), implying a oneness that admits no multiplicity. Therefore, it directly contradicts the idea of a body, which is necessarily composite. This argument shows that corporeality is incompatible with the divine oneness proclaimed in the surah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Ahad' simply means 'one in number', like any other word for one.
Correction: Actually, 'Ahad' is an intensified form (mubalaghah) implying absolute oneness that precludes any multiplicity or composition, including the minimal composition of a body.

C) Because bodies occupy space and 'Ahad' denies spatial location.
D) Because bodies are created and 'Ahad' implies eternal.

Key Takeaway
The word 'Ahad' in Surah al-Ikhlas is an intensified expression of oneness that logically rules out Allah being a body, because a body is always composite and thus not absolutely one.

Razi's Proof: 'Ahad' Negates Substance
Imam Razi argues that 'Ahad' negates substance for deniers of atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute 'Ahad' (One) negates that Allah is a substance (jawhar) according to the argument for those who deny atomic substance.

Core Idea
Being 'Ahad' means indivisible, so Allah cannot be an extended substance.

Imam Razi presents a rational argument to show that the divine name 'Ahad' (the One) implies that Allah is not a substance. This argument specifically addresses those who deny the existence of indivisible atoms. By analyzing spatial extension and divisibility, Razi demonstrates that anything extended must be divisible, and thus cannot be truly one. This reinforces divine transcendence beyond physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully. Notice the logical flow from extension to division to oneness.
The argument uses conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the key premise about extension
Find the claim about any extended thing (mutahayyiz) having distinct sides. Why is that necessary?
3
Understand the divisibility argument
Notice how having distinct sides leads to divisibility. If right were same as left, contradiction arises.
This shows the logical necessity of division.
4
Connect to 'Ahad' and substance
Since everything extended is divisible and not 'Ahad', Allah as 'Ahad' cannot be extended. Therefore He is not a substance.
Substances are by definition extended.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider what this argument says about Allah's nature. He is beyond physical categories like extension and division.
This is a negative theological statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دلالته على أنه ليس بجوهر. فنقول: أما الذين ينكرون الجوهر الفرد، فإنهم يقولون: إن كل متحيز، فلابد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الثاني. وذلك لأنه لابد من أن يتميز يمينه عن يساره، وقدامه عن خلفه، وفوقه عن تحته. وكل ما يتميز فيه شيء عن شيء، فهو منقسم، لأن يمينه موصوف بأنه يمين لا يسار، ويساره موصوف بأنه يسار لا يمين. فلو كان يمينه عين يساره، لاجتمع في الشيء الواحد: أنه يمين لا يمين، ويسار لا يسار. فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات في الشيء الواحد. وهو محال. قالوا: فثبت: أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم، وثبت أن كل منقسم فهو ليس بأحد. ولما كان الله سبحانه وتعالى موصوفاً بأنه أحد، وجب أن لا يكون متحيزاً أصلاً، وذلك ينفي كونه جوهراً.
And as for its indication that He is not a substance (jawhar). So we say: As for those who deny the atomic substance (al-jawhar al-fard), they say: Indeed every extended thing (mutahayyiz) must necessarily have one of its sides distinguished from the other. That is because it is necessary that its right be distinguished from its left, its front from its back, its above from its below. And everything in which something is distinguished from something else is divisible, because its right is described as right and not left, and its left is described as left and not right. For if its right were identical to its left, then in one thing there would combine that it is right and not right, and left and not left. Thus the conjunction of negation and affirmation in one thing would follow, which is impossible. They said: So it is established that every extended thing is divisible, and it is established that everything divisible is not 'Ahad'. And since Allah, glorified and exalted, is described as 'Ahad', it is necessary that He is not extended at all, and that negates His being a substance.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the word 'Ahad' also negates that Allah is a substance (jawhar), specifically addressing those who deny the existence of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard). The argument proceeds as follows: (1) Anything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) has distinguishable sides (right/left, front/back, etc.). (2) Anything with distinguishable sides is divisible, because if right is different from left, then the object has parts. (3) If the object were truly one (Ahad), it could not have distinct parts; indeed, if right were identical to left, then a single thing would be both right and not-right, a contradiction. (4) Therefore, everything extended is divisible, and everything divisible is not 'Ahad'. (5) Since Allah is described as 'Ahad', He cannot be extended. Hence, He is not a substance, because substances are extended. This argument relies on the impossibility of a thing being both right and not-right simultaneously.

Key Terms
جوهر
الجوهر الفرد
متحيز
منقسم
أحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being extended does not necessarily imply division, because a simple object could be extended without parts.
Correction: The argument shows that spatial extension requires distinct sides (right/left, etc.). Distinct sides entail that the object has parts (e.g., right side vs. left side). If the sides were identical, then a single thing would be both right and not-right, a contradiction. Therefore, extension necessarily implies divisibility.

Checkpoint

A) Because substances are eternal and 'Ahad' means created.
B) Because every extended thing is divisible, and every divisible thing is not 'Ahad'.
C) Because substances are composed of atoms and 'Ahad' denies composition.
D) Because substances can be seen and 'Ahad' means invisible.

Key Takeaway
Allah's oneness ('Ahad') logically entails that He is not extended and therefore not a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself imagining Allah as having a direction—like 'up in the sky' or 'in front of you'? This proof argues that ‘Ahad’ prohibits any spatial extension. How will you train your mind to worship Him without any mental image of a place or form?
?
The logic here forces us to affirm that Allah’s oneness is not just about numbers, but about indivisibility. Are there beliefs or practices in your life that inadvertently treat Allah as made of parts—for example, separating His mercy from His justice as if they were distinct pieces? How will you hold to the truth that His essence is absolutely simple?
?
If you were to explain to a non-Muslim that Allah cannot be a substance or extended, what personal experience of tawhid would you draw upon to make this more than a cold argument? How does this truth change the way you approach Him in your most sincere prayers?

Ahad Negates Substance: Alternative Argument for Atomists
Imam Razi presents an alternative argument that 'Ahad' negates substance for those who affirm atomic substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'Ahad' in Surat al-Ikhlas is used to negate that Allah is a substance, even for those who affirm indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
If Allah were an indivisible atom, every atom would be His equal – but 'Ahad' denies any equal.

This passage continues the scriptural proof from Surat al-Ikhlas. For those who hold to atomic theory, the previous proof based on divisibility does not apply. Instead, Imam Razi presents another interpretation of 'Ahad': it not only negates composition but also negates any equal or peer. The passage demonstrates that if Allah were an atomic substance, He would have many peers, contradicting His absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms like 'al-jawhar al-fard' and 'kufu'.
The passage is a self-contained argument.
2
Understand the alternative approach
Recognize that this argument is specifically for those who affirm atomic substances. For them, the earlier proof (based on divisibility) fails because atoms are indivisible.
3
Grasp the double meaning of 'Ahad'
The passage states that 'Ahad' can mean both negation of composition and negation of any equal or peer. This second meaning is crucial here.
This is a key linguistic point.
4
Follow the conditional reasoning
If Allah were an atomic substance, then every other atomic substance would be similar (mithl) to Him, and thus a peer (kufu). This contradicts 'Ahad' and the verse 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad'.
The verse is quoted for emphasis.
5
Note the conclusion
The passage concludes that Allah is neither body nor substance, and therefore not in any spatial location. This is a strong anti-anthropomorphic conclusion.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذين يثبتون الجوهر الفرد، فإنه لا يمكنهم الاستدلال على نفي كونه تعالى جوهراً من هذا الاعتبار، ويمكنهم أن يحتجوا بهذه الآية على نفي كونه جوهراً، من وجه آخر وبيانه: هو أن الأحد كما يراد به نفي التركيب والتأليف في الذات، فقد يراد به أيضاً: نفي الضد والند. فلو كان تعالى جوهراً فرداً لكان كل جوهر فرد مثلاً له. وذلك ينفي كونه أحداً. ثم أكدوا هذا الوجه بقوله تعالى: ولم يكن له كفواً أحد. ولو كان جوهراً، لكان كل جوهر فرد كفواً له. فدلت هذه السورة من الوجه الذي قررناه على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم؛ ولا بجوهر. وإذا ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر، وجب أن لا يكون في شيء من الأحياز.
And as for those who affirm the atomic substance (al-jawhar al-fard), it is not possible for them to use the previous consideration to negate that He, exalted, is a substance, but they can use this verse to negate that He is a substance in another way. Its explanation: that 'al-Ahad', just as it is intended to negate composition and compounding in the essence, it is also intended to negate the opponent and equal. For if He, exalted, were an atomic substance, then every atomic substance would be a similar (mithl) to Him. And that negates His being 'Ahad'. Then they emphasized this aspect by His statement, exalted: 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent (kufuwan ahad).' And if He were a substance, then every atomic substance would be an equivalent (kufu) to Him. So this surah, from the aspect we have established, indicates that He, exalted, is not a body; nor a substance. And when it is established that He, exalted, is not a body nor a substance, it necessarily follows that He is not in any spatial location.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage addresses those who believe in indivisible atoms (jawahir fardah). They cannot use the previous argument from divisibility, since atoms are not divisible. Instead, they use another aspect of 'Ahad': it not only negates composition but also negates any equal or peer (nidd). If Allah were an indivisible atom, then every other indivisible atom would be similar to Him in essence, making Him one among many peers. But 'Ahad' and the subsequent verse 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' affirm that He has no equivalent (kufu). Therefore, He cannot be an atom. The passage concludes that the surah proves Allah is neither a body nor a substance, and necessarily not located in any spatial location. This completes the first scriptural proof against anthropomorphism.

الضد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'kufu' in the verse refers only to family lineage or social status, not to essence.
Correction: The passage interprets 'kufu' as equality in essence, applicable to atomic substances. This shows the verse negates any being that shares the same essential nature with Allah.

Key Takeaway
The term 'Ahad' in Surat al-Ikhlas not only negates composition but also negates any equal; therefore, if Allah were an atomic substance, every atom would be His peer, contradicting His uniqueness.

Allah is Not in Space or Direction
Razi concludes from previous proofs that Allah cannot occupy space or direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi's negation of body and substance for Allah logically entails that He is free from space and direction, and how the verse 'Ahad' provides definitive proof.

Core Idea
If Allah is not a body or atom, He cannot be in any space or direction.

Razi now seals the argument. Having proven that Allah is neither a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar), he draws the necessary conclusion: such a being cannot occupy any space or direction. He presents a crisp logical dilemma—anything in a direction is either divisible or indivisible—and shows both are impossible for Allah. This passage ties the entire preceding discussion to the definitive meaning of 'Ahad'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Recall that Razi has already established that Allah is not a body (jism) and not a substance (jawhar). This is the foundation for the conclusion.
If this premise is unclear, review the previous lessons.
2
Follow the conditional dilemma
Razi argues that anything specific to a space or direction must be either divisible (making it a body) or indivisible (making it an individual substance/atom). Both have been proven false for Allah.
Notice the logical structure: 'if X is in space, then either Y or Z; both Y and Z are false, so X is not in space.'
3
Conclude and connect to Qur'an
Since both possibilities are invalid, it is impossible for Allah to be in any direction. This is the definitive (qat'iyya) meaning of the verse 'Ahad' (Qur'an 112:1).
Razi emphasizes that this is not just a philosophical deduction but a Qur'anic truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر وجب أن لا يكون في شيء من الأحياز والجهات. لأن كل ما كان مختصا بحيز وجهة فإن كان منقسما كان جسما وقد بينا إبطال ذلك وإن لم يكن منقسما كان جوهرا فردا وقد بينا أنه باطل. ولما بطل القسمان ثبت أنه يمتنع أن يكون في جهة أصلاً. فثبت أن قوله تعالى (أحد) يدل دلالة قطعية على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ولا في حيز وجهة أصلاً.
And when it is established that He, exalted, is not a body and not a substance, it is necessary that He is not in any of the spaces and directions. Because everything that is specific to a space and direction, if it is divisible, it is a body, and we have shown the invalidity of that; and if it is not divisible, it is an individual substance, and we have shown that it is false. And when the two divisions are invalid, it is established that it is impossible for Him to be in any direction at all. So it is established that His saying, exalted, 'Ahad' indicates with definitive indication that He, exalted, is not a body, not a substance, and not in any space or direction at all.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a deductive conclusion from the preceding proofs. Razi draws the necessary implication of negating body and substance: if Allah is neither a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar), then He cannot be in any space (hayyiz) or direction (jiha). He presents a two-part conditional: anything occupying space/direction is either divisible (hence a body, already refuted) or indivisible (an atom, also refuted). Since both possibilities are invalid, it is impossible for Allah to be in any direction. The verse 'Ahad' thus provides decisive (qat'iyya) proof that Allah is free from body, substance, space, and direction.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
جسم
جوهر
جوهر فرد
دلالة قطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah could be outside space yet still have a direction.
Correction: Razi shows that being in a direction necessarily entails being in space; the two are inseparable. Negating space automatically negates direction.

Checkpoint
What is the logical basis for Razi's conclusion that Allah is not in any direction?
A) Every direction requires a body, and Allah is not a body.
B) Anything in a direction is either a body or an atom, both of which are negated for Allah.
C) Direction implies limitation, which is incompatible with perfection.
D) The verse 'Ahad' explicitly denies direction.

Key Takeaway
Through a simple but decisive dilemma, Razi shows that the negation of body and substance necessarily implies the negation of space and direction, and this is the definitive meaning of Allah's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', do you consider that it negates not only partners but also any location or direction for Allah? How will you change the way you think about Allah when you say those words?
?
If Allah is beyond space and direction, what does that mean for how you seek closeness to Him? Is your worship dependent on a place or feeling, or on recognizing His absolute transcendence?
?
You have been taught that Allah is not in any direction. But in your heart, when you make du'a, do you still instinctively look up? Will you commit to training yourself to remember that Allah is not confined to any direction, and that your du'a reaches Him wherever you face?

The Proof from Direction: God is Not in a Space or Direction
Imam Razi argues from the impossibility of spatial location for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the impossibility of an infinite regress to prove that God cannot be in a direction, and how this establishes that He is not a body or substance.

Core Idea
Anything in a direction must be divisible or indivisible; both are impossible for God.

Imam Razi begins by showing that the very concept of being in a direction implies either composition (body) or indivisible atomicity (substance). Since both have been refuted earlier for God, the verse 'Ahad' confirms His transcendence beyond all spatial categories. This lesson unpacks that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it is divisible, then it is a body; if not, then it is an atom'. This is a dilemma argument.
The Arabic uses 'فإن كان... وإن لم يكن'.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Recognize that anything in a direction must be either divisible into parts (a body) or indivisible (an atom). These are the only two possibilities for a spatially located entity.
3
Apply the earlier refutations
Imam Razi refers to earlier proofs that God cannot be a body or an atom. Recall that both have been shown false for God. Thus, the premise that God is in a direction leads to a contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude the negation
Since both possibilities are impossible, God cannot be in a direction at all. Therefore, the verse 'Ahad' decisively indicates that He is not a body, not a substance, and not in any space or direction.
This conclusion is the direct implication of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهات . لأن كل ما كان مختصا بحيز وجهة ، فإن كان منقسما كان جسمها وقد بينا إبطال ذلك - وإن لم يكن منقسمها ، كان جوهرا فردا وقد بينا أنه باطل - ولما بطل القسمان ، ثبت: أنه يمتنع أن يكون في جهة أصلا ، فثبت : أن قوله تعالى ( أحد ، يدل دلالة قطعية على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ، ولا في حيز وجهة أصلا . واعلم : أنه تعالى كما نص على أنه واحد فقد نص أيضا على البرهان الذى لأجله يجب الحكم بأنه أحد. وذلك أنه قال : « هو الله أحد ، وكونه إلها يقتضى كونه غنيا عما سواه ، وكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه : غيره ، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره، وكونه إلها يمنع من كونه مفتقرا إلى غيره. وذلك يوجب القطع بكونه أحدا . وكونه أحدا يوجب القطع بأنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا في حيز وجهة . فثبت : أن قوله تعالى: وهو الله أحد : برهان قاطع على ثبوت هذه المطالب .
And directions. Because everything that is specific to a space and direction, if it is divisible, then it is a body, and we have already shown the invalidity of that; and if it is not divisible, then it is an indivisible atom, and we have already shown that it is false. Since both divisions are false, it is established that it is impossible for Him to be in any direction at all. So it is established that His statement 'Ahad' gives decisive indication that He, exalted, is not a body, nor a substance, nor in a space or direction at all. And know: He, exalted, just as He stated that He is one, He also stated the proof for which it is necessary to judge that He is Ahad. That is because He said: 'He is Allah, Ahad,' and His being Ilah necessitates His being independent of all else. Every composite is dependent on each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it, so every composite is dependent on something other than it, and His being Ilah prevents His being dependent on something other than Him. That necessitates the certainty that He is Ahad. And His being Ahad necessitates the certainty that He is not a body, nor a substance, nor in a space or direction. So it is established that His statement 'Say: He is Allah, Ahad' is a decisive proof for the establishment of these claims.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a two-fold proof for divine transcendence (tanzih) from the verse 'Allahu Ahad'. First, from the impossibility of God being in a direction (جھة): anything in a direction must be either divisible (a body) or indivisible (an atom). Both have been shown false for God, so He cannot be in a direction. Hence, the verse 'Ahad' decisively indicates that He is not a body, not a substance, and not in a space or direction. Second, Imam Razi elaborates the internal proof from the meaning of 'Ahad': because 'Ilah' (God) entails absolute independence (الغنى) from all else. Every composite (مركب) depends on its parts, which are other than itself, so composition implies dependence. Since God cannot be dependent, He cannot be composite. Thus 'Ahad' necessarily negates composition and spatial location. The passage concludes that the verse is a decisive proof for all these attributes of transcendence.

Key Terms
أحد
جسم
جوهر
حيز
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Ahad' only means numerically one, but Imam Razi shows it negates spatial location.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that 'Ahad' logically implies that God cannot be in any direction, because being in a direction would entail divisibility or atomicity, both of which contradict absolute oneness.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what are the two possibilities for anything that occupies a direction?
A) It is either a body or a substance
B) It is either divisible (body) or indivisible (atom)
C) It is either finite or infinite
D) It is either necessary or contingent

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allahu Ahad' decisively proves that God is free from all spatial attributes, as any spatial entity would be either a body or an atom, both impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Proof from 'Ahad': Divine Independence and Non-Composition
Imam Razi explains that the verse itself contains a proof: 'Ilah' implies independence, and composition implies dependence, so God must be non-composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi derives God's absolute independence (al-ghina) from the meaning of 'Ilah' in the verse, and how that negates any composition or dependence.

Core Idea
God's being 'Ilah' entails absolute independence, which is incompatible with composition.

Imam Razi now delves deeper into the verse, showing that 'Ahad' is not a mere title but carries a logical proof. Since 'Ilah' means the One who is worshipped and is independent of all, and since composition implies dependence on parts, God cannot be composite. This lesson unfolds that chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'wa'lam' to the end. Notice that Imam Razi explains that the verse itself contains the proof.
2
Understand the premise: Ilah implies independence
The word 'Ilah' (God) necessarily entails being independent (ghani) of everything else. This is a core theological concept.
3
Examine the argument against composition
Every composite (murakkab) is dependent on its parts. Since each part is other than the whole, the composite needs its parts to exist. Therefore, composition implies dependence on others.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since God is independent (by virtue of being Ilah), He cannot be dependent on anything. Therefore, He cannot be composite. Thus, He is truly Ahad, and that oneness negates body, substance, and spatial location.
This is the internal proof of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The First Argument: Composition Implies Need
Razi's first argument from Samad against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first argument from 'Allahu Samad' negates corporeality by showing that compositeness implies dependence, contradicting absolute self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would depend on His parts, contradicting His being Samad.

Imam Razi begins his proof from the verse 'Allahu Samad' by defining Samad as the master to whom all turn for needs, implying absolute independence. The first argument takes this definition and shows that corporeality—being a body—would entail composition and thus dependence, which is incompatible with being Samad.

Guided Path
1
Define Samad
Read the definition of Samad from the passage. It means the master to whom all turn for needs, implying complete independence.
This definition sets the stage.
2
Understand the logical chain
Follow the argument: body → composite → dependent on parts → dependent on other → not independent. Identify each step.
3
Apply to God
Conclude that if God were a body, He would not be absolutely Samad. Since the Quran says He is Samad, He cannot be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله سبحانه وتعالى : الله الصمد ، فالصمد هو السيد المصمود إليه فى الحوائج ، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ، وعلى أنه غير مختص بالحيز والجهة . أما بيان دلالته على الجسمية فمن وجوه : الأول : إن كل جسم فهو مركب ، وكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره . فكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى غيره ، والمحتاج إلى الغير لا يكون غنيا محتاجا إليه فلم يكن صمدا مطلقا . الثاني : لو كان مركبا من الجوارح والأعضاء لاحتاج في الإبصار إلى العين ، وفى الفعل إلى اليد ، وفى المشى إلى الرجل . وذلك ينافي كونه صمدا مطلقا . الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الأجسام متماثلة . والأشياء المتماثلة يجب اشتراكها فى اللوازم . فلو احتاج بعض الأجسام إلى بعض ، لزم كون الكل محتاجا إلى ذلك الجسم، ولزم أيضا كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه . وكل ذلك محال ، ولما كان ذلك محالا ، وجب أن لا يحتاج إلى شيء من الأجسام . ولو كان كذلك لم يكن صمدا على الإطلاق .
And as for His statement, glorified and exalted, 'Allah, al-Samad,' al-Samad is the master to whom one turns for needs. That indicates that He is not a body, and that He is not specific to space and direction. As for the demonstration of its indication regarding [negation of] corporeality, it is from several aspects: First: Every body is composite, and every composite is in need of each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it. So every composite is in need of something other than it, and the one in need of something other than it is not independent but in need of it, so he would not be absolutely Samad. Second: If He were composed of limbs and organs, He would need an eye for sight, a hand for action, and a leg for walking. That contradicts His being absolutely Samad. Third: We establish the proof that bodies are similar. Similar things must share in their necessary concomitants. So if some bodies are in need of some others, then it would necessitate that the whole is in need of that body, and also necessitate that it is in need of itself. All that is impossible. Since that is impossible, it is necessary that He not be in need of any body. And if that is the case, He would not be absolutely Samad.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides three rational arguments from the verse 'Allahu Samad' to negate corporeality (جسمية) from God. First, Imam Razi defines 'Samad' as the master to whom all turn for needs, implying absolute independence. The three arguments proceed: (1) Every body is composite, and composites depend on their parts, which are other than themselves. Dependence contradicts the self-sufficiency (الغنى) implied by Samad. (2) Composition from limbs would entail need for specific organs for specific actions (e.g., eye for sight), which contradicts absolute Samad. (3) Bodies are similar in essence, so if one body depends on another, all bodies would depend on that one, leading to circular dependence or self-dependence—both impossible. Thus, God cannot be a body. (Note: The passage also mentions that Samad negates spatiality, but the proof for that is cut off in this window.)

Key Terms
صمد
جسم
مركب
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being self-sufficient (Samad) means God is independent of creation but still could be a body.
Correction: The argument shows that bodily composition necessarily entails dependence on parts, which contradicts absolute independence. Therefore, God cannot be a body at all.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does being a body contradict being Samad?
A) Because bodies are visible
B) Because bodies are composite and depend on their parts
C) Because bodies are limited in space
D) Because bodies are created

Key Takeaway
The first argument establishes that corporeality implies composition and dependence, which is incompatible with God's absolute independence as Samad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on God as the only independent being, or do you subtly depend on created things as if they were self-sufficient? Reflect on where your heart turns in moments of need.

The argument shows that any composition implies need. Consider your own heart: what 'composite' things – people, possessions, status – do you treat as necessary parts of your life? How does that contradict the reality of God's samadiyyah?
?
If God is Samad, utterly independent and the only one to whom all turn, then your entire life's purpose is to turn to Him alone. Where do you find yourself turning to others instead, and what can you do today to redirect that dependence?

The Second Argument: Limbs Imply Need
Razi's second argument from Samad against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second argument from 'Allahu Samad' negates corporeality by showing that composition from limbs would necessitate need for specific organs, contradicting absolute Samad.

Core Idea
If God had limbs, He would need eyes to see and hands to act, denying His absolute self-sufficiency.

The second argument against corporeality focuses on the idea that a body composed of limbs would require those limbs for specific functions. Since God is Samad, meaning absolutely independent and needless, having limbs would contradict that attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
If God were a body, He would be composed of limbs (جوارح وأعضاء). Read the passage to see the examples given.
2
Identify the dependence
Notice that each limb serves a specific purpose: eye for sight, hand for action, leg for walking. This implies need for those limbs.
3
Contradiction with Samad
Being in need of limbs for actions contradicts absolute independence. Thus, God cannot have a body with limbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله سبحانه وتعالى : الله الصمد ، فالصمد هو السيد المصمود إليه فى الحوائج ، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ، وعلى أنه غير مختص بالحيز والجهة . أما بيان دلالته على الجسمية فمن وجوه : الأول : إن كل جسم فهو مركب ، وكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره . فكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى غيره ، والمحتاج إلى الغير لا يكون غنيا محتاجا إليه فلم يكن صمدا مطلقا . الثاني : لو كان مركبا من الجوارح والأعضاء لاحتاج في الإبصار إلى العين ، وفى الفعل إلى اليد ، وفى المشى إلى الرجل . وذلك ينافي كونه صمدا مطلقا . الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الأجسام متماثلة . والأشياء المتماثلة يجب اشتراكها فى اللوازم . فلو احتاج بعض الأجسام إلى بعض ، لزم كون الكل محتاجا إلى ذلك الجسم، ولزم أيضا كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه . وكل ذلك محال ، ولما كان ذلك محالا ، وجب أن لا يحتاج إلى شيء من الأجسام . ولو كان كذلك لم يكن صمدا على الإطلاق .
And as for His statement, glorified and exalted, 'Allah, al-Samad,' al-Samad is the master to whom one turns for needs. That indicates that He is not a body, and that He is not specific to space and direction. As for the demonstration of its indication regarding [negation of] corporeality, it is from several aspects: First: Every body is composite, and every composite is in need of each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it. So if some bodies are in need of some others, then it would necessitate that the whole is in need of that body, and also necessitate that it is in need of itself. All that is impossible. Since that is impossible, it is necessary that He not be in need of any body. And if that is the case, He would not be absolutely Samad.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides three rational arguments from the verse 'Allahu Samad' to negate corporeality (جسمية) from God. First, Imam Razi defines 'Samad' as the master to whom all turn for needs, implying absolute independence. The three arguments proceed: (1) Every body is composite, and composites depend on their parts, which are other than themselves. Dependence contradicts the self-sufficiency (الغنى) implied by Samad. (2) Composition from limbs would entail need for specific organs for specific actions (e.g., eye for sight), which contradicts absolute Samad. Key Terms

جوارح
أعضاء
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God could have limbs without being dependent on them, since He is all-powerful.
Correction: The argument shows that having a limb logically entails needing that limb to perform its function. Even if God could miraculously act without limbs, the possession of limbs implies potential need. The very existence of limbs as parts of a composite body entails dependence on those parts.

Checkpoint
In the second argument, what does the need for an eye indicate?
A) That God can see
B) That God is dependent on that organ
C) That God has a perfect body
D) That God is all-powerful

Key Takeaway
Composition from limbs would impose dependence on those limbs, which is incompatible with God's absolute self-sufficiency as Samad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that Allah does not need any instrument or helper to sustain you, would you be able to stop worrying about tomorrow? What is one specific worry you can hand over to Him right now, trusting that He needs no hand to provide for you?
?

How should this awe-inspiring truth transform the way you approach Him in supplication—not as a desperate beggar uncertain of His resources, but as a servant calling upon the absolutely Self-Sufficient who gives without being affected?

The Third Argument: Similarity of Bodies Implies Mutual Need
Razi's third argument from Samad against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument from 'Allahu Samad' uses the similarity of bodies to show that if God needed any body, He would need all bodies and even Himself, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If bodies are similar and one body needs another, then all bodies would need that one, leading to absurdity.

The third argument takes a different approach: it establishes that all bodies are similar (متماثلة) in essence, so they share the same necessary properties. If one body depends on another, then all bodies would depend on that one, and even that one would depend on itself, which is impossible. Hence, God cannot depend on any body.

Guided Path
1
Establish similarity
Imam Razi states that bodies are similar (متماثلة) and must share necessary concomitants (لوازم). Understand this premise.
This is a broader claim from kalam about the nature of bodies.
2
Apply dependence
If God were to need any body, then due to similarity, all bodies would need that body. Also, that body would need itself if it is also a body.
3
Reject impossibility
Self-need and mutual need are impossible. Therefore, God cannot need any body. But if He were a body, He would need something. So He cannot be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله سبحانه وتعالى : الله الصمد ، فالصمد هو السيد المصمود إليه فى الحوائج ، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ، وعلى أنه غير مختص بالحيز والجهة . أما بيان دلالته على الجسمية فمن وجوه : الأول : إن كل جسم فهو مركب ، وكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره . فكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى غيره ، والمحتاج إلى الغير لا يكون غنيا محتاجا إليه فلم يكن صمدا مطلقا . الثاني : لو كان مركبا من الجوارح والأعضاء لاحتاج في الإبصار إلى العين ، وفى الفعل إلى اليد ، وفى المشى إلى الرجل . وذلك ينافي كونه صمدا مطلقا . الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الأجسام متماثلة . والأشياء المتماثلة يجب اشتراكها فى اللوازم . فلو احتاج بعض الأجسام إلى بعض ، لزم كون الكل محتاجا إلى ذلك الجسم، ولزم أيضا كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه . وكل ذلك محال ، ولما كان ذلك محالا ، وجب أن لا يحتاج إلى شيء من الأجسام . ولو كان كذلك لم يكن صمدا على الإطلاق .
And as for His statement, glorified and exalted, 'Allah, al-Samad,' al-Samad is the master to whom one turns for needs. That indicates that He is not a body, and that He is not specific to space and direction. As for the demonstration of its indication regarding [negation of] corporeality, it is from several aspects: First: Every body is composite, and every composite is in need of each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it. So every composite is in need of something other than it, and the one in need of something other than it is not independent but in need of it, so he would not be absolutely Samad. Second: If He were composed of limbs and organs, He would need an eye for sight, a hand for action, and a leg for walking. That contradicts His being absolutely Samad. Third: We establish the proof that bodies are similar. Similar things must share in their necessary concomitants. So if some bodies are in need of some others, then it would necessitate that the whole is in need of that body, and also necessitate that it is in need of itself. All that is impossible. Since that is impossible, it is necessary that He not be in need of any body. And if that is the case, He would not be absolutely Samad.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides three rational arguments from the verse 'Allahu Samad' to negate corporeality (جسمية) from God. First, Imam Razi defines 'Samad' as the master to whom all turn for needs, implying absolute independence. The three arguments proceed: (1) Every body is composite, and composites depend on their parts, which are other than themselves. Dependence contradicts the self-sufficiency (الغنى) implied by Samad. (2) Composition from limbs would entail need for specific organs for specific actions (e.g., eye for sight), which contradicts absolute Samad. (3) Bodies are similar in essence, so if one body depends on another, all bodies would depend on that one, leading to circular dependence or self-dependence—both impossible. Thus, God cannot be a body. (Note: The passage also mentions that Samad negates spatiality, but the proof for that is cut off in this window.)

Key Terms
صمد
متماثلة
لوازم
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God could be a body but still have no needs because He is powerful.
Correction: The argument shows that being a body logically entails either needing others or being needed by all, both impossible for the absolutely independent.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what would happen if one body needed another body?
A) Only that body would need it
B) All bodies would need that body and it would need itself
C) No other body would be affected
D) It would not contradict Samad

Key Takeaway
The similarity of bodies implies that if God needed any body, He would need all bodies and even Himself, which is impossible; thus, God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this argument remind you that every created thing, including yourself, is inherently in need? What step can you take today to acknowledge your need for Allah alone?
?

Are there subtle ways you attribute need to Allah by expecting Him to act in a certain way? How can you worship Him as the absolute Samad, free from all needs and limitations?
?
The argument shows that if God were a body, He would need Himself—an absurdity. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes try to 'divide' your trust between Allah and other sources, as if Allah needs external support? What would it mean for you to trust in Allah alone as the one who is perfectly independent and self-sufficient?

Second Argument: Refuting God's Composition from Limbs
Imam Razi presents the second argument refuting that God is composed of limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's logical argument that God cannot be composed of bodily parts because it would entail need and contradict His absolute independence (Samadiyya).

Core Idea
If God had limbs, He would depend on them — but God is absolutely independent.

Imam Razi continues his series of arguments proving that God is not a body. This second argument is concise yet powerful: if God were composed of limbs, each action would require a specific limb, implying need. But God is al-Samad, the absolutely Independent, who needs nothing. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional statement (if... then...). The conclusion follows from the contradiction with samadiyya.
The term 'صمدا' is key: it means the one who is independent and to whom all turn for needs.
2
Understand the components: limbs and their functions
The passage mentions three examples: eye for sight, hand for action, leg for walking. These are representative of any bodily part. The point is that composition implies dependence on parts.
The argument doesn't require enumerating all limbs; the examples suffice to illustrate the principle.
3
Grasp the logical consequence
The conditional leads to a contradiction with the attribute of being 'صمدا مطلقا' (absolutely independent). Need and independence are mutually exclusive.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming composition leads to an impossibility, so the assumption is false.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This argument supports the doctrine of tanzih (transcendence) — God is free from any resemblance to created bodies.
Imam Razi's approach is rational and scripturally grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان مركبا من الجوارح والأعضاء لاحتاج في الإبصار إلى العين ، وفي الفعل إلى اليد ، وفي المشى إلى الرجل . وذلك ينافي كونه صمدا مطلقا
Second: If He were composed of [bodily] parts and limbs, He would need for sight the eye, for action the hand, and for walking the leg. That contradicts His being absolutely Independent (Samad).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is the second of a series of proofs that God is not a body. The author argues that if God were composed of limbs (jewarih wa a'da'), then for each action He would require that specific limb: sight would require an eye, action a hand, walking a leg. This implies dependence on parts, which negates absolute independence (samadiyya mutlaqa). The argument is concise: composition entails need, need contradicts self-sufficiency, therefore God is not composed of limbs.

Key Terms

الجوارح
الأعضاء
صمدا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributing limbs to God is harmless or metaphorical, but Imam Razi shows it is logically contradictory.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that composition implies dependence, which conflicts with God's absolute independence (Samadiyya). Therefore, attributing limbs is not only inappropriate but rationally impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's second argument shows that composition from parts implies need, which is incompatible with God's absolute independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Argument: Similarity of Bodies
Imam Razi presents the third argument from similarity of bodies to deny that God is a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the similarity of bodies and their necessary properties to prove that God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would need something, contradicting His absolute independence.

Imam Razi now presents a third argument against anthropomorphism, grounded in the nature of bodies. He argues that all bodies are similar and therefore share essential properties. By applying this principle to the assumption that God is a body, he shows that it leads to an impossible consequence—and thus must be false. This lesson walks through the logic of that reductio.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow: a claim, a principle, a conditional, and a conclusion.
The argument is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two premises: (1) all bodies are similar, (2) similar things share necessary properties. Ask: Are these reasonable?
Imam Razi assumes this without proof here.
3
Follow the reductio
Trace the conditional: if some bodies needed another, then the whole would need that body and it would need itself. Recognize why this is impossible.
The impossibility is circular dependence.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that God cannot need any body, so He is not a body. Reflect on why being a body would require need.
Need here refers to composition or dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الأجسام متماثلة . والأشياء المتماثلة يجب اشتراكها في اللوازم . فلو احتاج بعض الأجسام إلى بعض ، لزم كون الكل محتاجا إلى ذلك الجسم، ولزم أيضا كونه محتاجا إلى نفسه . وكل ذلك محال ، ولما كان ذلك محالا، وجب أن لا يحتاج إلى شيء من الأجسام . ولو كان كذلك.. لم يكن صمدا على الإطلاق .
Third: We establish the proof that bodies are similar (mutamāthila). Similar things must share in their necessary properties (lawāzim). So if some bodies needed another body, then the whole would need that body, and also it would need itself. All that is impossible. Since that is impossible, it must be that He does not need any body. And if that were so, He would not be absolutely Independent (Samad).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is the third argument showing God is not a body. The author first establishes that all bodies are similar (mutamāthila). Then he states a principle: similar things share their necessary properties (lawāzim). If a body (like God, if He were a body) needed another body, then because of similarity, all bodies would need that body, and also that body would need itself. This is impossible because a thing cannot be dependent on itself. Thus, the original assumption is false: God cannot need any body. Therefore, He is not a body at all, for if He were, He would need something, negating absolute independence.

Key Terms
متماثلة
اللوازم
محتاجا
صمدا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity (mutamāthala) means being identical in all aspects.
Correction: In this context, similarity means sharing essential properties, not complete identity. Bodies are similar in being composite and contingent.

Checkpoint

A) They must be identical
B) They share necessary properties
C) They cannot be compared
D) They are all composite

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of self-need in the reductio proves that God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you inadvertently picture a form or location? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your concept of God? What practical step can you take today to worship God as the Absolutely Independent, beyond all physical imagination?
?
The argument shows that even a self-sufficient body cannot be truly Independent (Samad). What does this teach you about your own neediness? In what areas of your life do you still cling to self-sufficiency, forgetting that only God is independent?
?
If God were a body, He would need something. Reflect on your own needs. How does recognizing God's absolute independence transform the way you approach Him in prayer and daily reliance? What can you let go of today to depend more fully on Him?
