Proof that God is Free from Place and Direction
Imam Razi proves God's transcendence via disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's proof that God is not in a place or direction using a disjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
If God were in a place, He would depend on it; but God is absolutely independent.

Imam Razi presents a concise logical proof that God is transcendent above place and direction. The argument is a two-pronged disjunctive syllogism: whether God's presence in a specific place is necessary or contingent, both lead to dependence. This establishes that God cannot be in a place at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: 'if... then...' and 'either... or...'
2
Identify the two branches of the argument
The passage considers two possibilities: either God's being in a specific place is necessary (wajib) or possible (ja'iz). Trace each branch to its conclusion.
Both branches lead to the same result: God would be in need, which contradicts His being al-Samad.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since both possibilities lead to dependence, the original assumption (that God is in a place) must be false. Therefore, God is free from place and direction.
This is a classic example of a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان دلالته على أنه تعالى منزه عن الحيز والجهة : فهو أنه سبحانه وتعالى لو كان مختصا بالحيز والجهة ، لكان إما أن يكون حصوله في الحيز المعين واجبا ، أو جائزا . فإن كان واجبا فحينئذ يكون ذاته تعالى مفتقرا في الوجود والتحقق ، إلى ذلك الحيز المعين . وذلك الحيز المعين ، يكون غنيا عن ذات الله تعالى. لأنا لو فرضنا عدم حصول ذات الله تعالى في ذلك الحيز المعين ، لم يبطل ذلك الحيز أصلا . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون تعالى محتاجا إلى ذلك الحيز ، فلا يكون صمدا على الإطلاق. وأما إن كان حصوله في الحيز المعين جائزا لا واجبا ، فحينئذ يفتقر إلى مخصص يخصصه بالحيز المعين. وذلك يوجب كونه محتاجا ، وينافي كونه صمدا .
And as for the demonstration that He is transcendent above place (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha): It is that if the Exalted were particularized by place and direction, then His occurrence in a specific place would either be necessary or possible. If necessary, then His essence would be in need of that specific place for existence and actuality. And that specific place would be independent of His essence. For if we assume the non-occurrence of God's essence in that specific place, that place would not cease to exist at all. On this estimation, the Exalted would be in need of that place, so He would not be absolutely Independent (Samad). And if His occurrence in the specific place were possible, not necessary, then He would need a specifier (mukhāṣiṣ) to specify Him to that place. That entails His being in need, and contradicts His being Samad.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage contains the proof that God is not in a place (ḥayyiz) or direction (jiha). The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: If God were in a specific place, either it is necessary or contingent. If necessary, then God's existence depends on that place, because the place can exist without God (if we imagine God not there, the place remains). That makes God need the place, violating absolute independence. If contingent, then God needs a specifier to choose that place over others, which also indicates need. Both possibilities lead to need, which contradicts the attribute of Samad (absolute independence). Therefore, God is not in a place or direction.

Key Terms
الحيز
الجهة
واجب
جائز
مخصص
صمدا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'in a place' refers only to physical locations, but the argument applies to any spatial relation, including direction or containment.
Correction: The term 'ḥayyiz' in Islamic theology includes any spatial location or direction. The argument covers all such relations, not just physical places.

Checkpoint
In the argument about place, what does it entail if God's being in a specific place is necessary?
A) God depends on that place
B) God is independent of that place
C) That place depends on God
D) Both are independent

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that ascribing a place to God leads to dependence, contradicting His absolute independence (Samad).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make dua, do you subconsciously direct your heart toward a specific place like the sky? How does this proof challenge that tendency, and what can you do today to remind yourself that God is beyond all places?
?
The argument shows that God's independence requires that nothing else be necessary for His existence. Is there any person, possession, or status you rely on as if it were essential to your well-being? What step will you take this week to shift that reliance to God alone?
?
If God is not confined to any direction, then He is equally near to you in every moment. Yet how often do you act as if God is distant or only present in certain settings? What one change in your daily routine will help you live with the awareness that He is always with you?

The Proof from 'Wa Lam Yakun Lahu Kufuwan Ahad'
Razi explains how Surah al-Ikhlas proves God is not a body or substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse 'And there is none like Him' logically negates that God could be a body or a substance.

Core Idea
If God were a substance, He would have equals — but the Quran says none is like Him.

Imam Razi begins by citing the famous verse from Surah al-Ikhlas, which declares that nothing is comparable to God. He then unfolds a logical argument: since all substances are similar to one another, if God were a substance, every substance would be His equal, contradicting the verse. The same reasoning applies to bodies, which are composed of substances. This concise proof establishes the surah as the clearest evidence of God's transcendence over physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qur'anic verse
Start by reading the verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' and notice its absolute negation of any likeness.
This verse is the foundation of the proof.
2
Understand the logical step for substances
Razi asserts that all substances are similar. If God were a substance, He would be like every other substance, making each a 'kufu' (equal). This contradicts the verse.
The term 'kufu' is key.
3
Extend the argument to bodies
A body is composed of substances. If God were a body, He would also be composed of substances, leading to the same conclusion. Thus, God is neither body nor substance.
This covers all physical categories.
4
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Razi concludes that this surah is the clearest proof of divine transcendence. Consider why this argument is so powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ولم يكن له كفوا أحد ، فهذا أيضا يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ، لأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن الجواهر متماثلة . فلو كان تعالى جوهراً ، لكان مثلا لجميع الجواهر فكان كل واحد من الجواهر : كفوا له . ولو كان جسما لكان مؤلفا من الجواهر . لأن الجسم يكون كذلك وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور . فثبت : أن هذه السورة أظهر الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ، ولا حاصل في مكان وحيز . وذلك : أنه كما أن الكفار لما سألوا الرسول عن صفة ربه ، وأجاب الله بهذه السورة ، الدالة على كونه تعالى منزها عن أن يكون جسما أو جوهراً أو مختصا بالمكان ، فكذلك فرعون سأل موسى عليه السلام عن صفة الله تعالى . فقال : « وما رب العالمين؟ ثم إن موسى لم يذكر الجواب عن هذا السؤال ، إلا بكونه تعالى خالقا للناس ومديراً لهم ، وخالقا للسموات والأرض ومديراً لهما . وهذا أيضا من أقوى الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا في جهة . لأنا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى أن كون الشيء جسما ومتحيزا ، هو عين الذات ونفسها وحقيقتها ، لا أنه صفة قائمة بالذات، وأما كونه خالقا للأشياء ومديرا لها فهو صفة
And as for His saying, 'And there is none like Him' (Quran 112:4), this also indicates that He is not a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar). For we will establish the proof that substances are similar. So if the Exalted were a substance, He would be similar to all substances, and then every substance would be equal to Him. And if He were a body, He would be composed of substances, because a body is such, and then the mentioned implication returns. So it is established that this surah is the clearest proof that the Exalted is not a body, not a substance, and not occurring in a place or space. That is: just as when the disbelievers asked the Prophet about the attribute of his Lord, and God answered with this surah, indicating that He is transcendent above being a body, a substance, or particularized by place, so also Pharaoh asked Moses peace be upon him about the attribute of God, and said: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' (Quran 26:23). Then Moses did not mention in answer to this question except that He is Creator of people and their Manager, and Creator of the heavens and the earth and their Manager. This is also among the strongest proofs that He is not in a spatial location (mutahayyiz) nor in a direction. For we will explain, God willing, that a thing's being a body and spatial is the very essence itself and its reality, not an attribute subsisting in the essence; and its being Creator of things and their Manager is an attribute.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage contains two interrelated proofs. First, from the Qur'anic verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' (And there is none like Him), Imam Razi argues that this verse shows God is not a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar). He states that substances are similar (mutamāthila). If God were a substance, He would be like all substances, meaning each substance would be an equal (kuf'u) to Him, contradicting the verse. If God were a body, He would be composed of substances, leading back to the same problem. Thus, Surah al-Ikhlas is the clearest proof of divine transcendence. Second, the author draws an analogy with the story of Moses and Pharaoh. When Pharaoh asked 'What is the Lord of the worlds?', Moses answered by describing God as Creator and Manager of people and the cosmos, not by describing a location or form. This omission indicates that God is not in a place or direction. The author then previews a future argument that being a body or spatial is identical to the essence, not a separate attribute, whereas being Creator is an attribute. This reinforces that spatial attributes cannot apply to God.

Key Terms
كفوا
جسم
جوهر
متماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And none is like Him' only rejects human-like qualities but allows for abstract physical categories like substance.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that if God were a substance, He would be similar to all substances, making each an equal, which the verse explicitly negates. So substance is also rejected.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Imam Razi draw from the verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad'?
A) That God has no physical form but can be a substance
B) That God is not a body or a substance
C) That God is not comparable to humans only
D) That God is a body composed of substances

Key Takeaway
The simple verse 'And none is like Him' contains a logical refutation of God being a body or substance, making it the clearest proof of divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad', do you mentally replace 'kufuwan' with 'nothing' or do you truly grasp that even a perfect substance would be an equal? What image of Allah do you carry in your heart that might still be your own invented 'equal'?
?
This lesson proves logically that Allah is unlike any body or substance. Yet in your daily life, do you treat someone or something as if it were on par with Allah's importance? What would it take for you to dethrone that false equal today?
?
Razi's argument rests on the premise that all substances are similar. Do you accept that premise? If so, how does it challenge the way you think about the invisible world? If not, what evidence from revelation or reason would you need to overturn a conclusion that comes directly from Surah al-Ikhlas?

The Analogy of Moses: No Place or Direction for God
Razi uses the Moses-Pharaoh story to argue God is not in a place or direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Moses' answer to Pharaoh demonstrates that God is not located in any place or direction.

Core Idea
Moses described God as Creator and Manager, not as somewhere — proving He has no spatial location.

Imam Razi draws a parallel between the revelation of Surah al-Ikhlas and the biblical encounter of Moses with Pharaoh. When Pharaoh asked about the Lord of the worlds, Moses did not describe God's location or form, but only His creative and governing attributes. This silent omission is a powerful indication that God is not a body occupying space. Razi then previews the deeper philosophical principle that being a body is identical to the essence, making spatial attributes impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Pharaoh's question
Pharaoh asked Moses 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' (Quran 26:23). Notice the context: it is a question about God's identity.
2
Observe Moses' answer
Moses answered by stating that God is the Creator and Manager of people and the heavens and earth. He did not mention any location or physical description.
This omission is deliberate.
3
Understand Razi's inference
Razi argues that if God were in a place or direction, Moses would have mentioned it. Since he didn't, it indicates God is not spatial.
This is an argument from silence.
4
Preview the philosophical principle
Razi hints at a future proof: being a body or spatial is identical to the essence, not a separate attribute. Therefore, spatial attributes cannot be added to God.
This strengthens the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ولم يكن له كفوا أحد ، فهذا أيضا يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ، لأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن الجواهر متماثلة . فلو كان تعالى جوهراً ، لكان مثلا لجميع الجواهر فكان كل واحد من الجواهر : كفوا له . ولو كان جسما لكان مؤلفا من الجواهر . لأن الجسم يكون كذلك وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور . فثبت : أن هذه السورة أظهر الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ، ولا حاصل في مكان وحيز . وذلك : أنه كما أن الكفار لما سألوا الرسول عن صفة ربه ، وأجاب الله بهذه السورة ، الدالة على كونه تعالى منزها عن أن يكون جسما أو جوهراً أو مختصا بالمكان ، فكذلك فرعون سأل موسى عليه السلام عن صفة الله تعالى . فقال : « وما رب العالمين؟ ثم إن موسى لم يذكر الجواب عن هذا السؤال ، إلا بكونه تعالى خالقا للناس ومديراً لهم ، وخالقا للسموات والأرض ومديراً لهما . وهذا أيضا من أقوى الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا في جهة . لأنا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى أن كون الشيء جسما ومتحيزا ، هو عين الذات ونفسها وحقيقتها ، لا أنه صفة قائمة بالذات، وأما كونه خالقا للأشياء ومديرا لها فهو صفة
And as for His saying, 'And there is none like Him' (Quran 112:4), this also indicates that He is not a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar). For we will establish the proof that substances are similar. So if the Exalted were a substance, He would be similar to all substances, and then every substance would be equal to Him. And if He were a body, He would be composed of substances, because a body is such, and then the mentioned implication returns. So it is established that this surah is the clearest proof that the Exalted is not a body, not a substance, and not occurring in a place or space. That is: just as when the disbelievers asked the Prophet about the attribute of his Lord, and God answered with this surah, indicating that He is transcendent above being a body, a substance, or particularized by place, so also Pharaoh asked Moses peace be upon him about the attribute of God, and said: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' (Quran 26:23). Then Moses did not mention in answer to this question except that He is Creator of people and their Manager, and Creator of the heavens and the earth and their Manager. This is also among the strongest proofs that He is not in a spatial location (mutahayyiz) nor in a direction. For we will explain, God willing, that a thing's being a body and spatial is the very essence itself and its reality, not an attribute subsisting in the essence; and its being Creator of things and their Manager is an attribute.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage contains two interrelated proofs. First, from the Qur'anic verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' (And there is none like Him), Imam Razi argues that this verse shows God is not a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar). He states that substances are similar (mutamāthila). If God were a substance, He would be like all substances, meaning each substance would be an equal (kuf'u) to Him, contradicting the verse. If God were a body, He would be composed of substances, leading back to the same problem. Thus, Surah al-Ikhlas is the clearest proof of divine transcendence. Second, the author draws an analogy with the story of Moses and Pharaoh. When Pharaoh asked 'What is the Lord of the worlds?', Moses answered by describing God as Creator and Manager of people and the cosmos, not by describing a location or form. This omission indicates that God is not in a place or direction. The author then previews a future argument that being a body or spatial is identical to the essence, not a separate attribute, whereas being Creator is an attribute. This reinforces that spatial attributes cannot apply to God.

Allah's Self-Sufficiency Negates Spatial Location
Razi argues that if Allah were in a place, He would be dependent, contradicting His attribute al-Samad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's attribute of absolute self-sufficiency (al-Samadiyyah) logically precludes His being located in a specific place.

Core Idea
Location implies dependence; al-Samad means absolute independence from all.

Imam Razi presents a concise rational argument demonstrating that Allah cannot occupy a specific place, because doing so would imply need—either for that place itself or for a particularizer to place Him there. Since Allah is al-Samad, the One who needs nothing and upon whom all depend, spatial location is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument with two conditional scenarios.
The argument moves from necessity to possibility, concluding both lead to need.
2
Analyze the first scenario
Examine the hypothesis that Allah's presence in a place is necessary. Why does this imply dependence on that place?
If the place must exist independently for Allah to be in it, then Allah depends on the place.
3
Analyze the second scenario
Consider the hypothesis that Allah's presence in a place is merely possible. What additional entity is required, and why does that also imply need?
A particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) must choose the location, indicating external influence.
4
Connect to the attribute al-Samad
Reflect on how both scenarios lead to the conclusion that spatial location contradicts absolute self-sufficiency.
Al-Samad means needing nothing; location entails need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذات الله تعالى في ذلك الحيز المعين، لم يبطل ذلك الحيز أصلا. وعلى هذا التقدير يكون تعالى محتاجا إلى ذلك الحيز، فلا يكون صمداً على الإطلاق. وأما إن كان حصوله في الحيز المعين جائزا لا واجبا، فحينئذ يفتقر إلى مخصص يخصصه بالحيز المعين. وذلك يوجب كونه محتاجا، وينافي كونه صمداً.
If the essence of Allah Most High were in that specific place, that place would not cease to exist. On this assumption, He would be in need of that place, and thus He would not be absolutely Self-Sufficient (ṣamad). And if His occurrence in the specific place is possible rather than necessary, then He would need a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to assign Him to that place. That necessitates His being in need and contradicts His being Self-Sufficient (ṣamad).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof that Allah cannot be located in a specific place (ḥayyiz) because that would undermine His attribute of absolute self-sufficiency (al-ṣamadiyyah). The argument proceeds in two conditional scenarios. First, if Allah's being in a particular place were necessary, then that place would have to exist independently and Allah would depend on it, negating His self-sufficiency. Second, if it were merely possible, then a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) would be required to choose that place over others, again indicating need. Both possibilities lead to dependence, which is incompatible with the meaning of 'al-Ṣamad' — the one who needs nothing and upon whom all depend.

Key Terms
صمد
حيز
مخصص
محتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that Allah can be in a place if that place is created by Him, as that would not imply need.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that even if the place is created, the very relationship of location implies dependence on that place's existence or on a particularizer, both of which contradict absolute self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

B) Because either way (necessary or possible) it results in need.
C) Because the place would limit His power.
D) Because He would then be comparable to bodies.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attribute of absolute self-sufficiency (al-Ṣamad) logically entails that He cannot be in any specific place, because location implies need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument proves that Allah cannot be in any place, so He is closer to you than your jugular vein without being spatially near. How does this reality affect your awareness of His presence right now? What can you do in the next hour to act as if He is truly ever-present, though not in a place?

Surah Ikhlas as Proof that Allah is Neither Body nor Substance
Razi argues that the verse 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent' negates body and substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the verse 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent' from Surah al-Ikhlas to argue that Allah is neither a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar).

Core Idea
Surah al-Ikhlas itself proves that Allah transcends all physical categories like body and substance.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, one of Islam's greatest theologians, turns to the Qur'an itself to prove that Allah is not a body or substance. In this passage, he focuses on the final verse of Surah al-Ikhlas, 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent' (kufuwan ahad). He argues that if Allah were a substance or composed of substances, He would have equals, contradicting this clear Quranic negation. Let's examine his logical reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic of Razi's argument and its English translation to get the full context.
Notice the key terms: jism (body), jawhar (substance), and kufw (equivalent).
2
Identify the main claim and the verse used
Notice that Razi bases his argument on a single Quranic verse and draws a specific conclusion about Allah's nature.
The verse is Q112:4, the final verse of Surah al-Ikhlas.
3
Follow the logical syllogism
The argument works like a chain: all substances are similar; if Allah were a substance, He would be similar to all, making each a 'kufw' (equal); the verse denies any equal; therefore Allah is not a substance. The same applies to body because body is composed of substances.
Razi premises that 'jawāhir mutamāthila' – all atoms are alike in essence.
4
Consider the implications
Razi concludes that this surah is the clearest proof that Allah is not a body, substance, or located in space. Reflect on why this is important for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى: ولم يكن له كفوا أحد، فهذا أيضا يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهراً، لأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن الجواهر متماثلة. فلو كان تعالى جوهراً، لكان مثلاً لجميع الجواهر فكان كل واحد من الجواهر كفوا له. ولو كان جسماً لكان مؤلفاً من الجواهر، لأن الجسم يكون كذلك، وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور. فثبت: أن هذه السورة أظهر الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر، ولا حاصل في مكان وحيز.
And as for His statement, the Most High: 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent (kufuwan aḥad)', this also indicates that He is not a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar), for we will establish the proof that substances (jawāhir) are similar. So if He Most High were a substance, He would be an example for all substances, and thus each one of the substances would be an equivalent (kufuwan) for Him. And if He were a body, He would be composed of substances, because a body is like that, and then the aforementioned entailment returns. Thus it is established: this surah is the clearest evidence that He Most High is not a body, not a substance, and does not occur in a place (makān) or space (ḥayyiz).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a proof from Surah al-Ikhlas (112:4) that Allah is neither a body (jism) nor a substance (jawhar). The argument proceeds in three steps. First, Razi states the premise that all substances (jawāhir) are similar (mutamāthila) — a standard position in Kalam that atoms are alike in essence. Second, he applies the syllogism: if Allah were a substance, He would be similar to all other substances, meaning each substance would be His equal (kufw), contradicting the verse 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent'. Third, the same applies if He were a body, because a body is composed of substances; thus the same objection returns. The conclusion is that the surah is the clearest proof that Allah transcends body, substance, and spatial location.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
متماثلة
كفوا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'kufuwan' only denies a specific type of likeness, e.g., equal in divinity, not essential similarity.
Correction: Razi argues that if Allah were a substance (sharing the same essence category), that would constitute an essential likeness, making each substance a 'kufw', which the verse universally negates.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that if Allah were a substance, the verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' would be violated?
A) Because substances are infinite and Allah cannot be one of them.
B) Because all substances are similar, so He would have many equals.
C) Because substances are created, and Allah is uncreated.
D) Because the verse only refers to human equals.

Key Takeaway
Surah al-Ikhlas, particularly its final verse, provides a clear Quranic proof that Allah is not a body or substance and cannot be confined to space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined Allah as a being with a form, existing somewhere in the sky? In what specific ways does this lesson from Surah al-Ikhlas demand you to uproot that mental image? What action will you take today to begin purifying your concept of Allah?
?
Razi builds his proof on a logical premise about substances being similar. Do you have a similarly clear, Quran-based reason for why Allah cannot be a body, or do you rely on vague feelings? Imagine someone challenges you: 'Why can't Allah be a physical being in Paradise?' Could you answer them directly from this surah? If not, what will you do to be ready?

Moses' Answer Proves Allah is Not Spatial
Razi argues that Moses' answer to Pharaoh shows Allah is not spatial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the conversation between Moses and Pharaoh as a rational proof that Allah is not spatial and cannot be defined by location.

Core Idea
Moses' answer proves Allah's essence is beyond location.

Razi draws on the Qur'anic story of Moses and Pharaoh to construct a powerful argument for divine transcendence. By analyzing what Moses said and what he did not say, Razi shows that spatiality cannot be part of Allah's essence. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the parallel Razi draws with the polytheists' question and Surah al-Ikhlas.
2
Understand the parallel
Razi starts by comparing two situations: the polytheists asking the Prophet about Allah and being answered with Surah al-Ikhlas (negating body, substance, place), and Pharaoh asking Moses 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Moses answering by describing Allah as Creator and Manager.
Both answers point to the same truth: Allah is not spatial.
3
Follow Razi's logical argument
Razi uses the word 'ma' in Pharaoh's question to show it asks for the essence. If Allah were spatial, the answer would mention spatiality. Moses did not do that, so his answer would be wrong if spatiality were essential. Since his answer is correct, Allah is not spatial.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنه كما أن الكفار لما سألوا الرسول عن صفة ربه، وأجاب الله بهذه السورة، الدالة على كونه تعالى منزها عن أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو مختصا بالمكان، فكذلك فرعون سأل موسى عليه السلام عن صفة الله تعالى. فقال: «وما رب العالمين؟». ثم إن موسى لم يذكر الجواب عن هذا السؤال، إلا بكونه تعالى خالقا للناس ومديراً لهم، وخالقاً للسموات والأرض ومديراً لهما. وهذا أيضا من أقوى الدلائل على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ولا في جهة. لأنا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى أن كون الشيء جسماً ومتحيزاً، هو عين الذات ونفسها وحقيقتها، لا أنه صفة قائمة بالذات، وأما كونه خالقا للأشياء ومديرا لها فهو صفة. ولفظة «ما» سؤال عن الماهية، وطلب للحقيقة. فلو كان تعالى متحيزاً، لكان الجواب عن قوله «وما رب العالمين؟» بذكر كونه متحيزاً أولى من الجواب عنه بذكر كونه خالقاً. ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لكان جواب موسى عليه السلام خطأ، ولكان طعن فرعون بأنه مجنوناً لا يفهم السؤال، ولا يذكر في مقابلة السؤال ما يصلح أن يكون جواباً متجهاً لازماً. ولما يبطل ذلك، علمنا أنه تعالى ما كان متحيزاً. فلا جرم ما كان يمكن تعريف حقيقته سبحانه وتعالى إلا بأنه خالق مدير. فلا جرم كان جواب موسى عليه السلام صحيحاً، وكان سؤال فرعون ساقطاً فاسداً. مثبت: أنه كما أن جواب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم عن سؤال الكفار عن صفة الله تعالى يدل على تنزيه الله تعالى عن التحيز، فكذلك جواب موسى عليه السلام عن سؤال فرعون عن صفة الله تعالى يدل على تنزيه الله تعالى.
Know: Just as when the disbelievers asked the Messenger about the attribute of his Lord, and Allah answered with this surah, which indicates that He Most High is transcendent from being a body, a substance, or being particularized by place, so too Pharaoh asked Moses, peace be upon him, about the attribute of Allah Most High. He said: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' Then Moses did not give an answer to this question except by stating that He Most High is the Creator of people and their Manager, and the Creator of the heavens and the earth and their Manager. And this too is among the strongest evidences that He Most High is not spatial (mutahayyiz) nor in a direction (jiha). For we will explain, if Allah wills, that something being a body and spatial is the very essence itself and its reality, not a quality subsisting in the essence, while His being the Creator of things and their Manager is a quality. And the word 'ma' is a question about the quiddity (mahiyya) and a request for the reality. So if He Most High were spatial, the answer to His statement 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' would be to mention His being spatial, rather than mentioning His being Creator. And if the matter were so, then Moses' answer would be wrong, and Pharaoh's accusation that he is mad—not understanding the question, nor mentioning in response to the question what is suitable as a valid binding answer—would be valid and necessary. But since that is invalid, we know that He Most High was not spatial. Thus, it was not possible to define His reality, glorified and exalted, except by His being Creator and Manager. Hence, Moses' answer was correct, and Pharaoh's question was invalid and corrupt. Established: just as the answer of Muhammad, peace be upon him, to the disbelievers' question about the attribute of Allah Most High indicates the transcendence of Allah Most High from spatiality, so too the answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to Pharaoh's question about the attribute of Allah Most High indicates the transcendence of Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a scriptural argument that Allah is not spatial (mutahayyiz), drawn from the story of Moses and Pharaoh in the Qur'an (Surah al-Shu'ara', 26:23-24). Razi first draws a parallel: just as the polytheists asked the Prophet about Allah's attribute and were answered with Surah al-Ikhlas, which negates body, substance, and place, so Pharaoh asked Moses 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Moses answered by describing Allah as Creator and Manager, not by giving a spatial description. This is presented as proof that Allah is not in a direction or place. The reasoning is as follows: (1) The word 'ma' in Pharaoh's question asks about the essence (mahiyya). (2) If Allah were spatial, the proper answer would describe that spatiality, because being spatial would be part of His essence. (3) But Moses did not mention spatiality; he mentioned actions. (4) If spatiality were essential, Moses' answer would be incorrect, making Pharaoh's accusation of madness valid. (5) Since we know Moses' answer is correct and Pharaoh's accusation is false, Allah cannot be spatial. Therefore, His essence cannot be defined by location but only by His creative and managerial attributes. Razi concludes that both prophetic responses—Muhammad's and Moses'—establish divine transcendence from spatiality.

Key Terms
متحيز
جهة
ماهية
خالق
مدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' answer was a non-answer or a metaphor.
Correction: Razi demonstrates it was the only correct answer because Allah's essence is not spatial; answering with spatiality would be false.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say that if Allah were spatial, Moses should have answered Pharaoh by mentioning spatiality?
A) Because spatiality is an attribute that can be described.
B) Because the question 'ma' asks for the essence, and spatiality would be part of the essence.
C) Because Pharaoh was expecting a spatial description.
D) Because Moses was commanded to speak plainly.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument from Moses' response shows that Allah's essence cannot be described by spatial attributes; He is known only through His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Moses answered Pharaoh by directing his attention to Allah's actions, not His essence. When you try to know Allah, do you focus more on theological definitions or on recognizing His actions in your daily life? How can you shift your focus to the latter?
?
Razi argues that if Moses had given a wrong answer, Pharaoh's accusation of madness would have been valid. When you encounter doubts about Allah's nature, do you ever assume that the prophets' answers were insufficient? What does this story teach you about trusting their responses?
?
Allah cannot be contained in any place. Does this truth make you feel distant from Him, or does it free you to find Him everywhere? How will you remind yourself of His constant presence today?

Abraham's Celestial Argument for Divine Transcendence
Razi explains Abraham's argument from celestial change for Allah's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abraham's argument from the change of heavenly bodies establishes that Allah is not confined to any spatial direction or location.

Core Idea
Abraham's shift from stars to Creator proves Allah is beyond direction.

In this passage, Imam Razi unpacks a profound Qur'anic argument made by Prophet Abraham (al-Khalil). Abraham observed that heavenly bodies undergo change, proving they are created and not divine. He then turned his devotion to the Creator of the heavens and earth, implying that Allah is not limited to any direction, as He created all directions. Razi begins to unfold the logical aspects of this proof for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1

The passage describes two steps: (1) reasoning from change to temporality (ḥudūth), and (2) the conclusion that he turns his face to the Creator, not the created.
3

Razi explains that this incident proves Allah's transcendence from spatiality (taḥayyuz). The very act of turning away from all directions to the Creator shows He is not in any direction.
4

Razi begins listing aspects; the first is that bodies are similar (mutamāthila). This will lead to an argument that no body can be the Creator.

Commentary
This passage introduces the argument from the story of Abraham (al-Khalil) in the Qur'an (Surah al-An'am, 6:76-79). The first aspect, which is cut off on this page, is that bodies are similar (mutamāthila) — a premise to be used in a fuller argument presumably continued on the next page. The passage thus sets up a proof from the absence of directionality in Abraham's final orientation.

Moses' Answer Proves God's Transcendence
Imam Razi shows that Moses' answer to Pharaoh proves God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the dialogue between Moses and Pharaoh to prove that God is not a body (mutahayyiz).

Core Idea
Moses' answer to Pharaoh proves God is beyond physical description.

This lesson examines a logical proof from the Quranic dialogue. In Surah Ash-Shu'ara, Pharaoh asks Moses, 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' Moses replies, 'Lord of the heavens and the earth...' Imam Razi shows that if God were a body, Moses would have answered with bodily attributes. Since he didn't, God is not a body. This argument illustrates the method of prophetic transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the key terms: 'what' (ma) as a request for essence, and 'located in a place' (mutahayyiz).
The passage is a concise logical proof; read it slowly.
2
Identify the conditional
Look for the 'if-then' structure: 'If God were mutahayyiz, then Moses' answer would have been...' This is the core of the argument.
Imam Razi uses a hypothetical to expose a contradiction.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Since the hypothetical leads to an absurdity (Moses being wrong and Pharaoh correct), the premise is false: God is not mutahayyiz.
The proof is indirect, showing what cannot be the case.
4
Connect to prophetic pattern
The passage ends by linking Moses' answer to Prophet Muhammad's response to the disbelievers, showing a consistent prophetic method of affirming divine transcendence.
This reinforces the theological principle of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لفظة « ماء سؤال عن الماهية ، وطلب للحقيقة . فلو كان تعالى متحيزاً ، لكان الجواب عن قوله « وما رب العالمين » ؟ : بذكر كونه متحيزاً : أولى من الجواب عنه بذكر كونه خالقاً . ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لكان جواب موسى عليه السلام خطأ، ولكان طعن فرعون بأنه مجنون لا يفهم السؤال ، ولا يذكر في مقابلة السؤال ما يصلح أن يكون جواباً : متجهاً لازماً . ولما يظل ذلك ، علمنا أنه تعالى ما كان متحيزاً . فلا جرم ما كان يمكن تعريف حقيقته سبحانه وتعالى إلا بأنه خالق مدير. فلا جرم كان جواب موسى عليه السلام صحيحاً، وكان سؤال فرعون ساقطاً فاسداً. مثبت : أنه كما أن جواب محمد صل الله عن سؤال الكفار عن صفة الله تعالى : يدل على تنزيه الله تعالى عن التحيز ، فكذلك جواب موسى عليه السلام عن سؤال فرعون عن صفة الله تعالى : يدل على تنزيه الله تعالى
And the word 'what' is a question about quiddity and a request for reality. So if the Exalted were located in a place (mutahayyiz), then the answer to His saying 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' would be: by mentioning His being located in a place (mutahayyiz) would be more appropriate than answering it by mentioning His being Creator. And if the matter were such, then the answer of Moses, peace be upon him, would be wrong, and the accusation of Pharaoh that he was a madman who does not understand the question, and does not mention in response to the question what is suitable as an answer, would be an unavoidable charge. And since that was not the case, we know that the Exalted was not located in a place (mutahayyiz). Hence, it was not possible to define His reality, glorified and exalted, except as Creator and Manager. Hence, the answer of Moses, peace be upon him, was correct, and the question of Pharaoh was invalid and corrupt. Confirmed: just as the answer of Muhammad, may God bless him, to the question of the disbelievers about the attribute of God the Exalted indicates the transcendence (tanzih) of God the Exalted from location (tahayyuz), so too the answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to the question of Pharaoh about the attribute of God the Exalted indicates the transcendence (tanzih) of God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof from the dialogue between Moses and Pharaoh in the Quran. The argument proceeds as follows: First, the word 'ma' (what) in Pharaoh's question 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' is a request for the essence or reality of God. If God were a body (mutahayyiz), then the appropriate answer would be to describe His bodily attributes, such as size or shape. However, Moses answered by stating that God is the Creator and Manager of the universe, not any bodily description. If God were a body, Moses' answer would be considered incorrect, and Pharaoh's accusation of madness would be valid. Since we know that Moses' answer was correct and praiseworthy, and Pharaoh's accusation was false, it follows that God is not a body. The author also parallels this with the Prophet Muhammad's response to the disbelievers regarding God's attributes, establishing a consistent pattern of prophetic answers affirming divine transcendence.

Misconception: Some think Moses' answer was merely a deflection or that he misunderstood the question.
Correction: Actually, Moses gave a precise theological answer that demonstrates God's transcendence, because if God were a body, a bodily description would have been the correct response.

Moses' answer that God is the Creator and Manager proves God is not a body, because a bodily answer would have been appropriate if He were.

Abraham's Proof: Bodies Are Similar, So God Cannot Be a Body
Imam Razi gives Abraham's argument that if God were a body, He would be subject to change, which is impossible for a deity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abraham's rational argument that God cannot be a body because all bodies share the same nature and are subject to change.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would change like other bodies—but change is impossible for God.

Imam Razi begins his theological proof from Abraham's story by establishing a key principle: all bodies share the same nature. From this, he argues that if God were a body, He would be subject to change, which would contradict His divinity. This concise but powerful argument lays the groundwork for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of Abraham's argument. Notice how the author builds from the story to the logical conclusion.
2
Identify the principle of similarity
The passage states 'al-ajsam mutamathila' (bodies are similar). This is the foundational premise. Understand what it means: if bodies share the same essence, what applies to one applies to all.
This principle is crucial for the rest of the proof.
3
Apply the principle to God
If God were a body, then change, which applies to all bodies, would also apply to Him. But Abraham judged that a changing being cannot be divine. Therefore, God cannot be a body.
This leads to the conclusion that God is free from location (tahayyuz).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الخليل الله فقد حكى الله تعالى عنه في كتابه : بأنه استدل بحصول التغير في أحوال الكواكب : على حدوثها . ثم قال عند تمام الاستدلال : « وجهت وجهي للذي فطر السموات والأرض ، حنيفاً ، والجهة واعلم : أن هذه الواقعة تدل على تنزيه الله تعالى و تقديسه عن التحيز أما دلالتها على تنزيه الله تعالى عن التحيز، فمن وجوه : أحدهما : إنما سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى : أن الأجسام متماثلة وإذا ثبت ذلك، فنقول : ما يصح على أحد المثلين ، وجب أن يصح على المثال الآخر . فلو كان تعالى جسماً أو جوهراً، وجب أن يصح عليه كل ما يصح على غيره . وأن يصح على غيره كل ما يصح عليه . وذلك يقتضى جواز التغير عليه : ولما حكم الخليل عليه السلام بأن المتغير من حال إلى حال ، لا يصلح الإلهية ، وثبت أنه لو كان جسما لصح عليه التغير : لزم القطع بأنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز أصلا .
As for al-Khalil (Abraham), God the Exalted has narrated about him in His Book that he inferred from the occurrence of change in the states of the celestial bodies their temporality (huduth). Then he said at the completion of the inference: 'I turn my face to Him who created the heavens and the earth, as a hanif.' And the direction? Know that this incident indicates the transcendence (tanzih) and sanctification of God the Exalted from location (tahayyuz). As for its indication of the transcendence of God the Exalted from location (tahayyuz), it is from several aspects: The first: We will explain, God willing, that bodies are similar. And when that is established, we say: whatever applies to one of the two similars must apply to the other similar. So if the Exalted were a body or a substance, then everything that applies to another must apply to Him, and everything that applies to Him must apply to another. And that necessitates the permissibility of change upon Him. And since al-Khalil, peace be upon him, judged that that which changes from state to state is not suitable for divinity, and it is established that if He were a body, change would be possible for Him, then it necessarily follows with certainty that the Exalted is not located in a place (mutahayyiz) at all.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs from the story of Abraham. The argument begins by noting that Abraham inferred the creation (huduth) of the celestial bodies from their changing states. After his inference, he declared his devotion to the Creator, not a body. The author states that this incident contains indications of God's transcendence. The first proof relies on the principle that bodies are similar in nature. Since bodies are similar, if God were a body, whatever applies to other bodies (like change) must apply to Him. But Abraham explicitly ruled out the possibility of change in a true deity by stating that the changing celestial bodies are not gods. Therefore, since change is impossible for God, He cannot be a body. This prevents any corporeal interpretation of God.

الأجسام متماثلة
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abraham rejected celestial worship only, without addressing God's nature.
Correction: The deeper point is that change implies contingency, and God must be changeless, so He cannot be a body.

Checkpoint

B) Bodies are similar, so what applies to one applies to another

Key Takeaway
Abraham's argument shows that because bodies are similar, if God were a body He would change, which is impossible; therefore God is not a body.

Abraham's Praise Proves God Is Not a Body
Imam Razi argues that Abraham's praise for merely knowing God as Creator shows God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abraham's praise by God for only mentioning His creatorship proves that God is not a body.

Core Idea
Knowing God as Creator is sufficient for perfect knowledge; therefore He is not a body.

Imam al-Razi presents the second proof from the story of Abraham. When Abraham declared his total devotion to the Creator of the heavens and earth, God praised him for this statement. But if God were a body with specific dimensions, then merely knowing Him as Creator would not be enough for complete knowledge, and God would not have praised Abraham for that limited acknowledgment. This passage draws out the logical implication: God is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice how Abraham's statement is quoted and how God's praise is cited.
The Arabic uses the word 'mutahayyiz' meaning located in place.
2
Identify the key premise
Abraham only mentioned God's attribute of being Creator, and God praised him for that. What does this imply about the completeness of that knowledge?
3
Understand the conditional argument
The passage says: if God were a body, then complete knowledge would require knowing His bodily attributes. Why would that be?
Because a body has specific dimensions and shape, which must be known to truly know it.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
Since Abraham was praised for merely knowing God as Creator (without bodily attributes), the conditional's antecedent must be false: God is not a body.
5
Reflect on the significance
This proof shows that true knowledge of God does not depend on physical description, affirming His transcendence (tanzih).
This is a key argument in Islamic theology against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه عليه السلام قال عند تمام الاستدلال ، وجهت وجهى للذي فطر السموات والأرض ، فلم يذكر من صفات الله تعالى إلا كونه خالقا للعالم ، والله تعالى مدحه على هذا الكلام ، وعظمه . فقال : وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه ، نرفع درجات من نشاء ، ولو كان إله العالم جسما موصوفاً بمقدار مخصوص وشكل مخصوص ، لما كمل العلم به تعالى، إلا بعد العلم بكونه جسما متحيزاً . ولو كان كذلك لما كان مستحقاً للمدح والتعظيم ، بمجرد معرفة كونه خالقا للعالم . ولما كان هذا القدر من المعرفة كافياً فى كمال معرفة الله تعالى : دل ذلك على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز .
The second: He, peace be upon him, said at the completion of the inference: 'I turn my face to Him who created the heavens and the earth.' He did not mention from the attributes of God the Exalted except that He is the Creator of the world. And God the Exalted praised him for this speech and magnified it. He said: 'And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people; We raise in degrees whom We will.' And if the Lord of the world were a body described with a specific measure and specific shape, knowledge of Him, the Exalted, would not be complete except after knowledge of His being a body located in place (mutahayyiz). And if that were the case, he would not have deserved praise and magnification just by knowing that He is the Creator of the world. And since this amount of knowledge was sufficient for the completion of knowledge of God the Exalted, that indicates that the Exalted is not located in place (mutahayyiz).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This is the second proof from Abraham's story. Abraham declared 'I turn my face to Him who created...' mentioning only God's creative attribute. God praised Abraham for this statement in the Quran. The argument is that if God were a body with specific dimensions, then complete knowledge of God would require knowing those bodily attributes. However, God praised Abraham for merely acknowledging His creatorship. This shows that such knowledge is both sufficient and praiseworthy, implying that God's essence is not dependent on bodily description. Therefore, God is not a body (not mutahayyiz).

Misconception: Some think that praising Abraham for his statement implies mere testimony or that Abraham's knowledge was incomplete.
Correction: Rather, the praise proves that the essential knowledge of God is knowing Him as Creator, and that such knowledge is complete without bodily description.

Key Takeaway

?

Proof from Abraham: God as Body Implies Partners
Imam Razi proves that if God were a body, He would have partners, contradicting Abraham's rejection of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third proof that if God were a body, He would have partners, contradicting Abraham's monotheism.

Core Idea
God as body would make Him share essence with all bodies, necessitating partners.

Imam Razi presents a concise syllogism rooted in the Abrahamic legacy. He argues that corporeality necessarily entails a shared essence among all bodies, which would violate the fundamental Islamic principle of tawhid. This passage ties theological rationalism directly to prophetic testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully and then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: 'if...then...'.
The phrase 'tamam al-mahiyya' means 'complete essence'.
2
Identify the argument components
Break down the argument into: premise (God as body), implication (all bodies share essence), conclusion (partners for God).
3
Connect to Abraham's declaration
Razi uses Abraham's statement 'I am not of the polytheists' to highlight the contradiction. Reflect on why this prophetic testimony is powerful.
Abraham is a model of pure monotheism.
4
Recognize the prophets' consensus
The final sentence asserts that great prophets were certain of God's transcendence from corporeality, substantiality, and direction. Consider how this universalizes the proof.
Tanzih is a core prophetic message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه تعالى لو كان جسماً ، لكان كل مشاركاً له في تمام المناهية . فالقول بكونه تعالى جسماً ، يقتضى إثبات الشريك الله تعالى . وذلك ينافي قوله : « وما أنا من المشركين ، فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن العظماء من الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم كانوا قاطعين بتنزيه الله تعالى وتقديسه عن الجسمية والجوهرية والجهة . وبالله التوفيق
The third: If the Exalted were a body, then everything would share with Him in complete essential nature. So the statement that the Exalted is a body necessitates affirming a partner to God the Exalted. And that contradicts His saying: 'And I am not of the polytheists.' So it is established by what we have mentioned that the great ones among the prophets, God's blessings be upon them, were certain of the transcendence (tanzih) and sanctification of God the Exalted from corporeality (jismiyya), substantiality (jawhariyya), and direction (jiha). And with God is success.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This third proof from Abraham concludes the series. The argument is that if God were a body (jism), then every other body would share in the complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya) with God. This implies that there would be multiple entities sharing the same essential nature, effectively affirming partners (shirk) with God. Abraham explicitly rejected polytheism by saying 'I am not of the polytheists.' Therefore, the belief that God is a body is incompatible with monotheism. The author then summarizes that all great prophets were certain of God's transcendence from corporeality, substantiality, and direction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'sharing in essence' means merely similarity in attributes, not identity of essence.
Correction: Razi's phrase 'tamam al-mahiyya' indicates complete identity of essential nature, which leads to multiple entities sharing the same definition—polytheism.

Checkpoint

C) God would be beyond description

Key Takeaway
If God were a body, He would not be unique, contradicting Abraham's monotheism and the prophetic consensus on tanzih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Abraham's declaration 'I am not of the polytheists' is absolute. Think about a subtle form of shirk you struggle with—perhaps seeking validation from others, fearing their opinion more than God's. Are you ready to say with Abraham with full certainty, 'I am not of those who associate partners'? What would you need to change tonight?
?
Razi proves that if God were a body, He would not be unique. Consider: Have you ever reduced God in your mind to something you can grasp, limit, or categorize? Repent of that mental 'tajsim' and commit to contemplating His transcendence in your next prayer.

The Quranic Evidence: Nothing Is Like Him
Imam Razi cites a Quranic verse as proof for God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' serves as a proof for God's absolute transcendence and negates any corporeality.

Core Idea
The Quran itself declares God's absolute incomparability.

Imam Razi continues his case for God's transcendence by turning to the Quran itself. The second evidence is a single, powerful verse that directly states God's uniqueness. This verse is a cornerstone for the doctrine of tanzih, establishing that God cannot be compared to anything in creation, including bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the brevity and directness of the proof.
2
Reflect on the meaning of 'mithl'
Consider the word 'mithl' (like/equal). The verse negates even the notion of something being 'like' God. This goes beyond mere similarity; it denies any comparable essence or attributes.
3
Connect to the argument about bodies
Why does this verse prove God is not a body? Because bodies share common attributes (dimensions, parts) and can be compared. If nothing is like God, He cannot be a body.
This verse is a direct refutation of anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية من القرآن : قوله تعالى : ليس كمثله شيء
The second evidence from the Quran: His statement, the Exalted: 'Nothing is like Him.'
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the second major evidence from the Quran for God's transcendence. It is a direct quotation of Quran 42:11: 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (There is nothing like unto Him). The author uses it as a concise proof that God cannot be a body, since a body has specific attributes and dimensions that could be compared to other bodies. The verse establishes absolute uniqueness and incomparability, negating any similarity between God and any created being, including corporeal entities.

Key Terms
ليس كمثله شيء
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse only denies exact identity but allows partial similarity, such as God having a body but being unique in form.
Correction: The verse negates any 'mithl' (like/equal), which includes any degree of similarity in essence or attributes. Therefore, it denies all bodily attributes.

Checkpoint

B) Nothing is comparable to God, so He cannot be a body
C) God has a similar in some respects
D) The verse is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' is a foundational proof for God's transcendence, negating any corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if no one and nothing is like God, or do you sometimes let your fears of people or your desires rival His uniqueness in your heart? What will you do today to realign your heart with this truth?
?

?
If God is absolutely incomparable, then your relationship with Him is unlike any other relationship. Are you treating your connection with Him as utterly unique, or are you approaching it like a human relationship? What change can you make this week to honor His incomparability?

Proof from Change: God is Not a Body
Razi presents Ibrahim's deduction that change precludes divinity, proving God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof based on the principle of similars that establishes God's transcendence beyond body and substance.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would change—but change disqualifies divinity.

Imam Razi opens his proof against divine corporeality by invoking the reasoning of Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil). He lays down a logical principle about similars and applies it to show that if God were a body, change would be possible for Him. Since Ibrahim declared that change is incompatible with divinity, the conclusion is that God is not a body or spatial entity. This lesson walks through each step of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a feel for the logical flow from principle to conclusion.
Notice that the passage is a single continuous deductive argument.
2
Identify the logical principle
The argument begins with a principle about two similars: whatever is true for one is true for the other. Find this statement and understand its role.
This principle grounds the whole proof.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi then applies the principle: if God were a body, all properties of bodies would apply to Him, including change. Trace this conditional step.
4
Consider Ibrahim's ruling
The proof invokes Ibrahim's judgment that change disqualifies something from divinity. Reflect on why a changing being cannot be God.
This is the crucial premise linking change to divinity.
5
Understand the conclusion
Combine the conditional and Ibrahim's ruling to derive the conclusion: God is not a body or any spatial entity. See how the conclusion is necessitated.
The conclusion is absolute: 'not a spatial entity at all.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ما نصح على أحد المثلين ، وجب أن يصح على المثال الآخر . فلو كان تعالى جسمها أو جوهراً، وجب أن يصح عليه كل ماصح على غيره . وأن يصح على غيره كل ما صبح عليه . وذلك يقتضى جواز التغير عليه : ولما حكم الخليل عليه السلام بأن المتغير من حال إلى حال ، لا يصلح الإلهية ، وثبت أنه لو كان جسما لصح عليه التغير : لزم القطع بأنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز أصلا .
We say: Whatever is verified for one of two similars must be verified for the other similar. So if the Exalted were a body or a substance, then whatever is verified for other bodies must be verified for Him, and whatever is verified for Him must be verified for them. And that necessitates the possibility of change upon Him. And since al-Khalil (peace be upon him) judged that that which changes from state to state is not suitable for divinity, and it is established that if He were a body, change would be possible for Him, it necessarily follows that the Exalted is not a spatial entity at all.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against divine corporeality based on the reasoning of Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil). Razi states a general logical principle: if something applies to one of two similar things, it must apply to the other similar as well. Then he applies it to the hypothesis that God is a body or substance. If God were a body, then all properties that apply to other bodies would also apply to Him, and vice versa. This would imply that change could occur in God. But Ibrahim (peace be upon him) ruled that anything subject to change from one state to another cannot be worthy of divinity. Since change is established as possible for a body, it follows necessarily that God is not a body or spatial entity.

Key Terms
المثلين
المثال
الجسم
الجوهر
التغير
المتحيز
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is merely an analogy, not a logical deduction.
Correction: The proof uses the logical principle of similars (tamāthul) which is a deductive rule: if two things are identical in essence, they share all properties. It is not a weak analogy but a necessary consequence.

Checkpoint
What is the key logical principle used in the first proof?
A) Whatever applies to one of two similars applies to the other.
B) Change is impossible for any being.
C) God is not a substance.
D) Bodies are all similar.

Key Takeaway
The proof from change shows that if God were a body, He would be subject to change, which contradicts the necessary perfection of divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God does not change, does your worship reflect a relationship with a constant and perfect Being, or do you sometimes expect God to change His will based on your desires? What would it mean to trust in an unchanging God in your daily struggles?
?
The proof shows that any change disqualifies divinity. Is there an area of your life where you have subtly attributed changeability to God, perhaps by thinking that He might approve of something He clearly forbids if your circumstances change? How does this affect your obedience?
?
Ibrahim's judgment that change is unfit for divinity was a foundational truth that shaped his entire worldview. What foundational truths about God do you hold that actively reshape your decisions today? Or are they merely theoretical?

Knowledge of Creator Sufficies
Razi argues that knowing God as Creator is sufficient, so He need not be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibrahim's simple declaration of God as Creator proves that God is not a body.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's praise for mere creation shows God needs no body.

This lesson explores a powerful proof from the example of Prophet Ibrahim. At the moment of his greatest argument, Ibrahim simply turned to the Creator of the heavens and earth—nothing more. God praised him for that very statement. Imam Razi uses this to show that complete knowledge of God does not require knowing Him as a body; knowing Him as Creator is enough, so He is not spatial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original once, then the translation. Notice the flow: Ibrahim's statement, God's praise, then Razi's logical argument.
The passage is a conditional argument: if God were a body, then... but since... therefore.
2
Identify Ibrahim's key claim
Ibrahim only mentions that God is the Creator ('fatar' the heavens and earth). He doesn't mention any bodily attributes. Why would that be significant?
3
Trace Razi's conditional reasoning
Razi argues: If God were a body, then complete knowledge would require knowing His size and shape. But God praised Ibrahim for knowing only that He is Creator. Therefore, God is not a body.
Notice the logical structure: major premise, minor premise, conclusion.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This proof shows that knowing God as Creator is enough for true praise and complete knowledge. It defends tanzih (transcendence) by showing that bodily attributes are unnecessary.
This connects to Islamic theology's insistence on God's incomparability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه عليه السلام قال عند تمام الاستدلال ، وجهت وجهى للذي فطر السموات والأرض ، فلم يذكر من صفات الله تعالى إلا كونه خالقا للعالم ، والله تعالى مدحه على هذا الكلام ، وعظمه . فقال : وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه ، نرفع درجات من نشاء (۱)، ولو كان إله العالم جسما موصوفاً بمقدار مخصوص وشكل مخصوص ، لما كمل العلم به تعالى، إلا بعد العلم بكونه جسما متحيزاً . ولو كان كذلك لما كان مستحقاً للمدح والتعظيم ، بمجرد معرفة كونه خالقا للعالم . ولما كان هذا القدر من المعرفة كافياً فى كمال معرفة الله تعالى : دل ذلك على أنه تعالى . ليس بمتحيز .
The second: He (peace be upon him) said at the conclusion of the argument: 'I have turned my face toward He who created the heavens and the earth' – and he mentioned none of God's attributes except His being Creator of the world. And God the Exalted praised him for this speech and magnified him, saying: 'And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people; We raise in degrees whom We will.' Commentary
This passage presents a second proof from the example of Prophet Ibrahim. At the climax of his argument, Ibrahim said: 'I have turned my face toward He who created the heavens and the earth.' He only mentioned that God is the Creator, not any bodily attributes. God praised him for this statement, indicating it was sufficient. Razi argues that if God were a body with specific size and shape, then complete knowledge of God would require knowing that He is a spatial body. In that case, merely knowing that He is the Creator would not be enough to deserve praise. But since God did praise Ibrahim for that limited knowledge, it shows that knowledge of God's creative power suffices; thus God is not a body.

Third Proof: Corporeality Implies Partners
Razi presents a logical argument: divine corporeality implies a partner, contradicting monotheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that if God were a body, He would share essence with all bodies, leading to polytheism, and why prophets affirmed divine transcendence.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would have a partner — so He is not.

Razi presents a concise yet powerful argument against corporeality. He shows that affirming God as a body logically entails sharing complete quiddity with all other bodies, which necessarily implies a partner. This contradicts the prophetic declaration of monotheism. The prophets, peace be upon them, were therefore certain of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Notice the 'if...then' structure: if God is a body, then every body shares quiddity with Him. What does this imply?
3
Connect to the Quranic proof
Razi cites the verse 'I am not of the polytheists'. How does this negate the implication?
The proof relies on the exclusive nature of the verse.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that prophets were certain of God's transcendence from body, substance, and direction. Why is this significant?
This establishes the prophetic consensus on tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه تعالى لو كان جسما ، لكان كل مشاركاً له في تمام المناهية . فالقول بكونه تعالى جسما ، يقتضى إثبات الشريك الله تعالى . وذلك ينافي قوله : « وما أنا من المشركين »، فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن العظماء من الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - كانوا قاطعين بتنزيه الله تعالى وتقديسه عن الجسمية والجوهرية (والجهة . وبالله التوفيق)
The third: If the Exalted were a body, then every [body] would share with Him in complete quiddity [ماهية]. So the statement that He is a body necessitates affirming a partner to God the Exalted. And that contradicts His statement: 'And I am not of the polytheists.' So it is established by what we have mentioned that the great prophets – peace be upon them – were absolutely certain of God's transcendence and sanctification from corporeality, substantiality, and direction. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof against divine corporeality. Razi asserts that if God were a body, then every other body would share the same complete quiddity (essence) with Him. This would mean that God is like other bodies, which implies a partner equal in essence. This contradicts the statement of Ibrahim: 'And I am not of the polytheists.' Therefore, the great prophets were absolutely certain that God is transcendent above body, substance, and direction. The proof relies on the principle that all bodies are similar in their essential nature, so affirming God as a body would put Him in the same category as contingent beings, violating divine uniqueness.

Key Terms
الجسمية
الجوهرية
الجهة
الماهية
الشريك
المشركين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if all bodies are exactly identical in every detail.
Correction: Actually, the argument relies on sharing the complete quiddity (essential definition) of 'body', not accidental features. Even if bodies differ in accidents, they still share the same genus/species essence, which is sufficient to imply a partner.

Checkpoint

A) That God is unique in His form.
B) That God has a partner.
C) That God is not a creator.
D) That God is spatial.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows that corporeality entails polytheism, so God must be absolutely transcendent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you train yourself to affirm God's transcendence while still feeling His nearness? Make a concrete plan to replace anthropomorphic thoughts with awe.
?
Razi's proof shows that the prophets were 'absolutely certain' (قاطعين) of God's transcendence. Are you absolutely certain, or do you allow doubts about God's nature to linger? What step will you take today to strengthen your conviction that He is unlike anything?

Second Quranic Proof: 'Nothing Like Him' and the Refutation of Corporeality
Razi presents the Quranic verse, objection, and two answers against divine corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quranic verse 'Nothing is like Him' to argue that God cannot be a body, and how he refutes the objection that a unique body is possible.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would need a determiner, contradicting His Lordship.

Imam Razi now turns to a second Quranic proof for divine transcendence: the verse 'Nothing is like Him' (Q 42:11). He argues that if God were a body, He would share essential similarity with all bodies, contradicting the verse. An objector may suggest that God could be a unique body, just as humans and horses are both bodies yet differ. Razi's response shows why this analogy fails and why any form of corporeality leads to contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the second answer is cut off, but the main argument is clear.
The passage includes an objection and two answers; the first answer is complete.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that God could be a body unlike other bodies, using the analogy of humans and horses. Find this part and understand the challenge.
3
Analyze the first answer
Razi's first answer insists that all bodies are identical in complete quiddity. Difference only occurs in accidental attributes. If God were a body, His essence would be like others, and His particular attributes would be contingent, requiring a determiner.
This leads to a contradiction with God's Lordship.
4
Consider the second answer (incomplete)
The second answer begins by granting a difference in quiddity but then argues that this would still lead to an unacceptable consequence. Though cut off, you can infer the direction.
The text ends at 'وقوع', indicating the argument continues.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Summarize why Razi concludes that no form of corporeality is compatible with divinity. Connect to the core idea: a body cannot be God because it would be contingent.

Source Text

The second proof from the Qur'an: His statement: 'Nothing is like Him.' If He were a body, He would be like other bodies in complete quiddity. For we will show (if God wills, with brilliant proofs) that all bodies are similar. And that contradicts this text. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that He the Exalted, even if He is a body, is different from other bodies? Just as man and horse, even if they share in corporeality, differ in states and attributes, and it is not permissible to say the horse is like man, so here? The answer is from two aspects: The first: We will establish the proof (if God wills) that all bodies are similar in complete quiddity. So if He were a body, His essence would be like other bodies, and that contradicts this text. Man and horse – the essence of each is similar to the essence of the other. Difference only occurs in attributes and accidents. And when two essences are similar, the specification of each with its particular attributes is among contingent matters, not necessary ones. Because things similar in complete essence and quiddity cannot differ in their necessary concomitants. So if the Creator the Exalted were a body, His specification with His particular attributes would have to be contingent. And if that were so, He would depend on a director and specifier, which invalidates the statement that He is the Lord of the world. The second: On the assumption that He the Exalted shares with other bodies in corporeality but differs in His particular quiddity, this necessitates the occurrence

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof from the Quran: the verse 'Nothing is like Him' (Q 42:11). If God were a body, He would be like other bodies in essence, contradicting this verse. An objection is raised: perhaps God is a body but unlike other bodies, similar to how humans and horses are both bodies but differ in attributes. Razi responds with two answers. First, he asserts (and promises to prove later) that all bodies are identical in their complete quiddity; differences are only in accidental attributes. If God were a body, His essence would be like other bodies, contradicting the verse. Moreover, if essences are identical, then having particular attributes would be contingent, meaning God would need a determiner, which is impossible for the Lord. The second answer (cut off in the text) is that even if we suppose He differs in quiddity, that would entail something problematic (likely that He would be composite or contingent). The argument demonstrates that no form of corporeality is compatible with divinity.

Correction: Razi shows that all bodies are essentially identical; differences are only accidental, making any particular attributes contingent. This would require a determiner for God, which is impossible.
