Refuting the Objection of God's Distinct Bodiliness
Imam Razi refutes the claim that God could be a body distinct from other bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God could be a body distinct from other bodies is rationally untenable, and learn two arguments (from essential similarity and from composition) that refute it.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would be contingent in both essence and attributes.

Imam Razi here engages with an objection that might arise from those who believe God is a body but unique. The objector uses the analogy of man and horse: both bodies but different in qualities. Razi dismantles this analogy and provides two powerful arguments showing that any claim of divine bodiliness leads to absurdities like contingency and composition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection starting from 'فإن قيل' and identify the analogy: just as man and horse share body-ness but differ, why can't God be a body but differ from other bodies?
Notice how the objector tries to preserve God's uniqueness while admitting bodiliness.
2
First answer: essential similarity
Focus on the first 'الوجه الأول'. Razi argues that all bodies are alike in complete essence, so if God were a body, His attributes would be contingent and require a determiner, contradicting His necessity.
This argument relies on the premise that bodies are essentially identical.
3
Second answer: composition
Examine the second 'الوجه الثاني'. Even if God shares only body-ness but differs in quiddity, this implies composition (tarkīb) in His essence, leading to possibility and need for a creator.
Note the logical structure: composition implies contingency.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider why both answers are considered decisive. They show that any form of bodiliness entails dependence, which contradicts the very definition of God as the Necessary Being.
This lesson reinforces the core Islamic doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان جسماً، لكان مثلاً لسائر الأجسام في تمام الماهية. لانا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى بالدلائل الباهرة أن الأجسام كلها متماثلة. وذلك كالمناقض لهذا النص، فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى وإن كان جسماً، إلا أنه مخالف لغيره من الأجسام. كما أن الإنسان والفرس، وإن اشتركا في الجسمية، لكنهما مختلفان في الأحوال والصفات. ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: العرس مثل الإنسان، فكذا هنا؟ والجواب من وجهين: الأول: إنا سنقم الدلالة إن شاء الله تعالى على أن الأجسام كلها متماثلة في تمام الماهية، فلو كان تعالى جسماً، لكان ذاته مثلاً لسائر الأجسام. وذلك مخالف لهذا النص. والإنسان والفرس ذات كل واحد منهما مماثلة لذات الآخر. والاختلاف إنما وقع في الصفات والأعراض. والذاتان إذا كانتا متماثلتين، كان اختصاص كل واحدة منهما بصفاتها المخصوصة من الجائزات، لا من الواجبات. لأن الأشياء المتماثلة في تمام الذات والماهية، لا يجوز اختلافها في اللوازم. فلو كان البارئ تعالى جسماً، لوجب أن يكون اختصاصه بصفاته المخصوصة من الجائزات. ولو كان كذلك لزم افتقاره إلى المدير والمخصص. وذلك يبطل القول بكونه تعالى إله العالم. الثاني: إن بتقدير أن يكون هو تعالى مشاركاً لسائر الأجسام في الجسمية، ومخالفاً لها في ماهيته المخصوصة، فهذا يوجب وقوع الكثرة في ذات الله تعالى. لأن الجسمية مشترك فيها بين الله تعالى وبين غيره، وخصوصية ذاته غير مشتركة فيما بين الله تعالى وبين غيره، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة. وذلك يقتضي وقوع التركيب في ذاته المخصوصة. وكل مركب ممكن لا واجب، على ما بيناه. فثبت: أن هذا السؤال ساقط والله أعلم.
And if He were a body, He would be an example [similar] to the rest of bodies in the complete essence. Because we will show, God willing, with evident proofs, that all bodies are similar. And that is contradictory to this text [of revelation]. For if it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, even if He is a body, yet He is different from other bodies, just as man and horse, though they share in body-ness, yet they differ in states and attributes? And why is it not permissible to say that the horse is like man, so likewise here? The answer is in two ways: The first: We will establish the proof, God willing, that all bodies are similar in complete essence. So if the Exalted were a body, His essence would be similar to the rest of bodies. That contradicts this text. And man and horse: the essence of each of them is similar to the essence of the other. The difference only occurs in the attributes and accidents. And when two essences are similar, the specification of each of them with its particular attributes is among the contingent things, not among the necessary. Because things that are similar in complete essence and quiddity cannot differ in their necessary concomitants. So if the Creator, exalted, were a body, then His specification with His particular attributes would necessarily be among the contingent things. And if that were the case, it would necessitate His need for a director and a specifier. That invalidates the claim that He, exalted, is the God of the world. The second: On the assumption that He, exalted, shares with the rest of bodies in body-ness, and differs from them in His particular quiddity, this necessitates the occurrence of multiplicity in the essence of God, exalted. Because body-ness is shared between God, exalted, and others, and the particularity of His essence is not shared between God, exalted, and others, and that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is distinction. That necessitates the occurrence of composition in His particular essence. And every composite is possible, not necessary, as we have explained. So it is established that this question is invalid, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Imam Razi begins by stating a conclusion from a previous argument: if God were a body, He would be similar to all other bodies in complete essence. He then references an upcoming proof that all bodies are similar (mutamāthila), which would contradict the Quranic texts affirming God's transcendence. The objector then poses a challenge: why can't God be a body but differ from other bodies, just as man and horse share body-ness but differ in qualities? The objector argues that if we don't say horse is like man in every respect, similarly we shouldn't say God is like other bodies in every respect. The response is given in two ways (wajhayn). The first answer: Imam Razi asserts the proof that all bodies are identical in complete essence. Therefore, if God were a body, His essence would be exactly like other bodies, contradicting revelation. The analogy of man and horse is analyzed: their essences are similar (since both are bodies), and their differences are only in contingent attributes and accidents. When two essences are similar, their particular attributes are contingent, not necessary. If God were a body, His attributes would be contingent, requiring a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ), which implies need and invalidates His being the Creator. The second answer: even if we grant that God shares body-ness but differs in specific quiddity (māhiyya khāṣṣa), this would entail multiplicity (kathra) in God's essence. Body-ness is a shared aspect, and the particularity is a distinct aspect. The combination of sharing and distinction implies composition (tarkīb) in God's essence. Everything composite is possible (mumkin), not necessary (wājib). Therefore, the objection fails.

المخصص (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ)
الجسمية (al-jismiyya)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God were a body, He could still be unique by having different properties, similar to how man and horse differ despite sharing body-ness.
Correction: The passage shows that any bodily form entails essential similarity with other bodies (first answer) or composition (second answer), both incompatible with God's necessity and transcendence.

Checkpoint

A) He would have necessary attributes like other bodies.
B) His specific attributes would be contingent, requiring a determiner.
C) He would be identical to other bodies in every way.
D) He would be free from need.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih) is rationally preserved by showing that any notion of God as a body leads to contingency and composition, contradicting His necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Third Proof: God's Independence Negates Bodiliness
Imam Razi proves God's independence (ghinā) negating bodiliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses God's attribute of self-sufficiency (ghinā) to refute the claim that God has a body.

Core Idea
God's absolute independence proves He cannot be a body.

In this proof, Imam Razi turns to the Quranic verse declaring Allah as al-Ghaniyy (the Self-Sufficient) to argue against corporealism. He shows that a body's composite nature and need for direction contradict true self-sufficiency. Let's examine this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's Quranic foundation
Understand the verse 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (47:38) and how it establishes God's independence.
This verse is the starting point for the proof.
2
Grasp the first logical link
Why a body cannot be self-sufficient: every body is composite (murakkab) and therefore dependent on its parts.
Dependence on parts contradicts absolute independence.
3
Understand the second argument
If God were confined to a direction (jiha), He would be dependent on that direction, further negating independence.
Direction is another form of dependence.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Both reasons together establish that God is not a body. The Quranic attribute of al-Ghaniyy is incompatible with being a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: قوله تعالى: «والله الغني، وأنتم الفقراء» دلّت هذه الآية على كونه تعالى غنياً، ولو كان جسماً لما كان غنياً، لأن كل جسم مركب، وكل مركب محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وأيضاً: لو وجب اختصاصه بالجهة، لكان محتاجاً إلى الجهة، وذلك يقدح في كونه غنياً على الإطلاق.
The third proof: His statement, exalted: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (Quran 47:38). This verse indicates that He, exalted, is Self-Sufficient (ghaniyy). And if He were a body, He would not be Self-Sufficient, because every body is composite, and every composite is in need of each one of its parts. And also: if His being confined to a direction were necessary, He would be in need of that direction. And that detracts from His being Self-Sufficient absolutely.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) against the view that God is a body. The proof is based on the Quranic verse: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the needy' (Quran 47:38). Imam Razi argues that this verse establishes God's absolute independence (ghinā). If God were a body, He would not be genuinely independent, because every body is composite (murakkab). A composite entity is dependent on each of its parts for its existence, which contradicts self-sufficiency. Additionally, if God were necessarily confined to a specific direction (jiha), He would be dependent on that direction, further negating absolute independence. Thus, the verse refutes corporeality.

Key Terms
الغني (al-ghaniyy)
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
الجهة (al-jiha)
الافتقار (al-iftiqār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's self-sufficiency is only about not needing creation, not about not being dependent on parts or location.
Correction: This proof shows that self-sufficiency is absolute: it negates any dependence, whether on parts, direction, or creation.

Checkpoint

A) Bodies are invisible.
B) Every body is composite and hence dependent on its parts.
C) Bodies are material and impure.
D) The verse mentions 'poor' not 'body.'

Key Takeaway
The attribute of absolute independence (ghinā) logically entails that God cannot be a body, because bodies are composite and dependent on direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God is absolutely independent and in need of nothing, including your worship, why then does He command you to worship? How does this truth reframe your intention and sincerity in the next prayer you perform?

The Third Proof: Divine Self-Sufficiency Negates Corporeality
Razi presents a proof from Quranic self-sufficiency against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quranic attribute of divine self-sufficiency (al-Ghaniyy) to refute the claim that Allah is a body, and why corporeality is incompatible with absolute independence.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would need His parts — and a needy being cannot be God.

Razi continues his series of proofs against corporealism by turning to a well-known Quranic verse that describes Allah as al-Ghaniyy, the Self-Sufficient. The argument is elegantly simple: absolute self-sufficiency leaves no room for the dependence that comes with being a body. As you read this passage, notice how Razi draws out the logical implications of the verse to establish a key doctrinal point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi presents two distinct arguments within the same proof.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two sub-arguments: first from composition (every body is composite, composite is needy), second from direction (if body occupies direction, it needs that direction). Find each one in the text.
The word 'أيضاً' (also) marks the start of the second argument.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how each argument independently shows that corporeality contradicts absolute self-sufficiency. Why does Razi conclude that such a being cannot be God?
This connects to the core idea that neediness is incompatible with divinity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قوله تعالى: «والله الغنى، و أنتم الفقراء»، هذه الآية على كونه تعالى غنياً، ولو كان جسماً لما كان غنيا . لأن كل جسم مركب. وكل مركب محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه وأيضاً : لو وجب اختصاصه بالجهة ، لكان محتاجاً إلى الجهة . وذلك يقدح في كونه غنياً على الإطلاق.

Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against corporealism, based on the Quranic verse stating that Allah is al-Ghaniyy (the Self-Sufficient) while people are needy. Razi argues that if Allah were a body, He could not be truly Self-Sufficient. The reasoning follows two lines: first, every body is composite (murakkab), and every composite is dependent upon its parts. Second, if it were necessary for Him to occupy a direction (jiha) – as corporealists claim – He would be in need of that direction. Both implications contradict the attribute of absolute Self-Sufficiency. Thus the verse serves as a decisive proof that Allah is not a body.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Razi uses the classical kalam definition: a body (jism) is composed of indivisible parts (atoms) and accidents. This is a metaphysical, not empirical, claim.

Key Takeaway

The Fourth Proof: al-Qayyum and Dependence
Razi proves qayyumiyya excludes body and location, answering an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the divine name al-Qayyum to argue that Allah cannot be a body, and how he distinguishes between essential attributes (like knowledge) and accidental dependencies (like location) to answer an objection.

Core Idea
al-Qayyum means absolute independence – a body cannot be independent.

This lesson presents Razi’s fourth proof for Allah’s transcendence, drawn from the Qur’anic name al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting). Razi shows that if Allah were a body, He would be dependent on His parts and on place, contradicting qayyumiyya. He then answers a powerful objection: why does knowledge, which is also an attribute, not create dependency? The answer lies in the distinction between attributes necessitated by the essence and external requirements not required by the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, answer.
2
Identify the two arguments against corporeality
Razi gives two arguments based on qayyumiyya: the argument from parts and the argument from place. Find each and see how they show dependence.
3
Understand the objection about knowledge
The objector says: knowledge is a necessary attribute but doesn't negate qayyumiyya, so why should location? Pause and see if you anticipate the answer.
4
Study Razi’s distinction
Razi’s answer hinges on whether the attribute is intrinsic (necessitated by the essence) or extrinsic. See how he applies this to knowledge vs. location.
This is the key insight of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قوله تعالى «لا إله إلا هو ، الحى القيوم». والقيوم مبالغة في كونه غنياً عن كل ما سواه . وكونه مقوماً لغيره : عبارة عن احتياج كل ما سواه إليه . فلو كان جسماً لكان هو مفتقراً إلى غيره . وهو جزؤه . ولكان غيره غنياً عنه . وهو جزوه . وحينئذ لا يكون قيوماً . وأيضاً : لو وجب حصوله في شيء من الأحيان ، لكان مفتقراً محتاجاً إلى ذلك الحيز . فلم يكن قيوماً على الإطلاق . فإن قبل : ألستم تقولون : إنه تعالى يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بالعلم، ولم يقدح ذلك عندكم في كونه قيوماً ؟ فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : إنه يجب أن يحصل في حيز معين، ولم يقدح ذلك في كونه قيوماً ؟ قيل : عندنا أن ذاته كالموجب لتلك الصفة ، وذلك لا يقدح في وصف الذات بكونه قيوما ، أما ههنا فلا يمكن أن يقال : إن ذاته توجب ذلك الحيز المعين . لأن بتقدير أن لا يكون حاصلا في ذلك الحيز ، لم يلزم بطلان ذلك ولا عدمه ، فكان الحيز غنيا عنه ، وكان هو مفتقرا إلى ذلك الحيز . فظهر الفرق والله أعلم
The fourth proof: His statement, Exalted is He, 'There is no god but He, the Living, the Self-Subsisting' (Al-Baqara 255). And al-Qayyum is an intensive form indicating His being Self-Sufficient beyond all others, and His being the Sustainer of others is an expression of the need of all others for Him. So if He were a body, He would be in need of another, namely His part, and that other would be independent of Him, namely His part, and then He would not be Qayyum. Also, if it were necessary for Him to occur in some place, He would be in need of that place, so He would not be Qayyum absolutely. If it is said: Do you not say that He, Exalted, must be described with knowledge, and that does not diminish in your view His being Qayyum? So why is it not permissible to say that He must occur in a specific place, and that does not diminish His being Qayyum? It is said: With us, His essence is as the necessitator of that attribute, and that does not diminish the description of the essence as Qayyum. But here it cannot be said that His essence necessitates that specific place, because on the assumption that He is not present in that place, it would not entail the nullification or non-existence of anything, so the place would be independent of Him, and He would be in need of that place. Thus the difference is clear. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, citing the verse 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' (the Living, the Self-Subsisting). Razi explains that al-Qayyum is a hyperbolic form signifying absolute independence from everything else and the dependence of all else upon Him. If Allah were a body, He would be composed of parts, and then He would be dependent on those parts (since a body's existence requires its parts), while the parts would be independent of Him. That would contradict qayyumiyya. Similarly, if He were necessarily located in a specific place (as corporealists claim), He would be dependent on that place. An objection is raised: knowledge is an attribute that He must have, but that does not negate qayyumiyya, so why should location negate it? The answer distinguishes: knowledge is necessitated by His essence (His essence is such that it necessarily has knowledge), so it is not a dependency on something external. But location is not necessitated by His essence; if He were not in a given place, nothing about Him would change, so the place would be independent of Him and He would be dependent on it. This shows the difference.

Key Terms
قيوم
حيز
علم
جزء
مفتقر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any necessary attribute (like knowledge) implies dependency, so location should also be permissible without negating qayyumiyya.
Correction: Razi clarifies that knowledge is intrinsic to the essence—it is not a dependency because the essence itself requires it. Location, however, is extrinsic; if Allah were not in a specific place, He would still be perfect, so location would be an external need.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the necessity of knowledge not negate qayyumiyya, while the necessity of location would?
A) Knowledge is also a dependency, so the objection is valid.
B) The essence necessitates knowledge, so it is not a dependency; location is not necessitated by the essence.
C) Location is a form of knowledge, so it is similar.
D) The verse only negates location, not knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the name al-Qayyum to argue that Allah cannot be a body because a body depends on its parts and place; He distinguishes essential attributes (like knowledge) from accidental ones (like location) to answer an objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Ya Qayyum' in your du'a, do you truly feel that your entire existence hangs on His self-subsistence every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient in your daily affairs? What concrete change can you make today to shift your reliance from yourself to Him?
?
Razi argues that if Allah were in a place, He would be dependent on that place. Reflect: Is there any place, person, or possession that you subconsciously treat as indispensable for your well-being? How can you realign your heart to see that only al-Qayyum is truly indispensable?
?
Razi distinguishes between attributes that are intrinsic (like knowledge) and those that imply dependence (like location). Are there beliefs you hold about Allah that, upon reflection, impose a subtle limitation on His absolute independence? How can you purify your understanding to exalt Him as truly al-Qayyum?

The Fifth Proof: Do You Know Any Likeness? (Quran 19:65)
Razi uses Quran 19:65 to prove God has no equal, refuting corporealism via linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 19:65 and linguistic analysis to prove that Allah has no likeness and does not occupy space.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, every atom would be like Him; the Quran denies any likeness.

Imam Razi presents the fifth proof for divine transcendence based on the verse 'Do you know any likeness to Him?' (Maryam 65). He argues that if Allah were a spatial entity, all atoms would be similar to Him, contradicting the verse. This lesson unpacks Razi's argument and his refutation of alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from Ibn Abbas
Start by reading the verse and Ibn Abbas's interpretation that 'samiyyan' means 'like' (mithl). This is the foundational meaning for the proof.
Notice that Ibn Abbas equates samiyyan with mithl.
2
Follow Razi's logical move
Razi argues that if Allah were mutahayyiz (occupying space), then every atom (jawhar) would be a likeness to Him, because all atoms share the same essential property of spatial extension. This contradicts the verse.
This refutes corporealism and anthropomorphism.
3
Examine Razi's rejection of alternative interpretations
Razi considers and refutes the reading of 'samiyyan' as 'namesake', showing why it is far-fetched and insufficient for tanzih.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the correct meaning is 'likeness' (shabīh), which directly negates any similarity between Allah and created things, affirming His transcendence.
This is the intended meaning that supports the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قوله تعالى : « هل تعلم له سميا »؟ قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنه : هل تعلم له مثلا ، ولو كان متحيزا ، لكان كل واحد من الجواهر مثلا له . وقوله تعالى : ( هل تعلم له سميا ) ؟ معناه : أن الكفار كانوا يسمون الأصنام : آلهة ويسمونها : اله ولكن لم يسموا أى صنم : الله فيكون المعنى : لا أحد من الكفار سمى سمى صنيه : الله فهل تعلم له سميا ، أي لا يوجد اله : اسمه الله الا الله تعالى كما قال عن يحيى عليه السلام : لم نجعل له من قبل سميا ( أى لم يسم به أحد قبله ) . أن أهل الحق لم يظهر فيهم من عبد غير الله وسماه باسم الله . واذا ظهر فى أهل الباطل من عبد غير الله وسماه باسم الله . فلأنه على باطل تكون تسميته لغوا من القول ، فلا يعتد بها. وهذه المعاني بعيدة . ( هل تعلم له سميا ) أي مثلا وشبيها وهذا هو المراد - أي لا اله مثل الله يرجى نفعه ويخشى بأسه . أى لا اله الا الله فسميا ليس من الاسم وانما هي من حقيقة الاله . ولو كانت من الاسم . فكثير من الناس يسمون بمحمد صلى الله عليه و سلم . وليسوا شبها به الا في الاسم
The fifth proof: His statement, Exalted is He, 'Do you know any likeness to Him?' (Maryam 65). Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Do you know any like (mithl) to Him?' And if He were occupying space (mutahayyiz), then every single atom (jawhar) would be like Him. And His statement, Exalted is He, 'Do you know any samiyyan to Him?' Its meaning: that the disbelievers used to call idols 'gods' (aliha) and they called them 'ilāh', but they did not call any idol 'Allah'. So the meaning is: none of the disbelievers called his idol 'Allah'—so do you know any samiyyan to Him? That is, there is no god whose name is Allah except Allah, Exalted, as He said about Yahya, peace be upon him: 'We did not make for him before any namesake (samiyyan)' — i.e., no one was named by that name before him. The people of truth never had among them anyone who worshipped other than Allah and named him with Allah's name. And if it appears among the people of falsehood that someone worships other than Allah and names him with Allah's name, then because he is upon falsehood, his naming is vain speech and is not taken into account. And these meanings are far-fetched. ('Do you know any samiyyan to Him?') means 'any like and similar', and this is the intended meaning — that is, there is no god like Allah whose benefit is hoped for and whose severity is feared. That is, there is no god but Allah. So 'samiyyan' is not from 'name' (ism), but rather it is from the reality of divinity. And if it were from 'name', then many people are named Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, yet they are not similar to him except in name.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof and then provides a lengthy exegetical discussion. The proof itself: the verse 'Hal taʿlamu lahu samiyyan?' (Do you know any likeness to Him?) — Ibn Abbas interprets samiyyan as 'like' (mithl). Razi argues that if Allah were occupying space (mutahayyiz), then every atom (jawhar) would be similar to Him (since all atoms are identical in their substance), thus contradicting the verse which denies any likeness. The subsequent commentary explores alternative interpretations of samiyyan: some suggested it means 'namesake' (someone named after Him), but Razi rejects these as far-fetched. He explains that if it meant 'namesake', the disbelievers did not call their idols 'Allah', so the verse would be trivial. Moreover, if it were about names, many people share names with prophets without being similar. Therefore, the correct interpretation is samiyyan as 'likeness' (mithl shabīh), which directly supports the negation of corporeality.

Key Terms
سمي
مثل
متحيز
جواهر
شبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'samiyyan' in the verse means 'namesake', so the verse only denies that anyone else is named 'Allah'.
Correction: Razi argues that the correct meaning is 'likeness' (shabīh), which is necessary for the proof against corporealism. If it meant namesake, the verse would be trivial, and many people share names without being similar.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument in Razi's fifth proof against corporealism?
A) If God were a body, He would have a name like other bodies.
B) The verse denies any likeness to God, but if He were a body, every atom would be like Him.
C) The verse says God has no namesake, so He cannot be named.
D) Ibn Abbas interpreted samiyyan as 'namesake', so God is unique in name.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Do you know any likeness to Him?' proves that Allah has no equal, and if He were a body, all atoms would be equal to Him, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart, treat any person, object, or ambition as if it holds the same ultimate authority as Allah? How does the verse 'Do you know any likeness to Him?' expose that as shirk? What will you change about your attachments today?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Greatest,' do you truly feel that nothing else deserves your ultimate fear and hope? Razi proves that no being is like Allah. Can you honestly say none of your fears or hopes are directed at something other than Him? If not, what is one step you can take this week to realign your heart?
?
You have read that 'samiyyan' means likeness, not just names. Are you content with merely calling yourself a Muslim, or does your life reflect that you believe nothing is truly comparable to Allah? In what area of your daily decisions do you act as though something else is like Him? Commit to one specific change starting tomorrow.

The Quranic Argument: 'Is There Any Equal to Him?'
Imam Razi refutes corporealism using the verse 'هل تعلم له سميا'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the Quranic verse 'هل تعلم له سميا' to refute the belief that God is a body.

Core Idea
If God were a body, every atom would be His equal – but the Quran says there is none.

Imam Razi presents a powerful scriptural argument against corporealism. The verse 'هل تعلم له سميا' (Maryam 19:65) is interpreted by Ibn Abbas as asking about a likeness. Razi then derives a logical consequence: if God were a body, every atom would be equivalent to Him, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks the argument and its two interpretations of 'samiyyan'.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and initial interpretation
Start by reading the Quranic verse and Ibn Abbas's comment that 'samiyyan' means 'likeness'. This sets the stage for the argument.
2
Understand the logical implication
Notice the conditional: if God were a body, then every atom would be like Him. Why is that a problem? Because it implies multiple gods, contradicting the verse.
3
Examine the two interpretations of 'samiyyan'
Imam Razi presents two possible meanings: one based on the name 'Allah' being exclusive, and the other based on 'likeness'. He favors the latter. Understand why the first is 'far-fetched'.
The second interpretation is the one that supports the argument against corporealism.
4
Summarize the argument's structure
The argument moves from verse to interpretation to logical deduction. See how it all fits together to prove God's transcendence from physicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: قوله تعالى: {﴿هَلۡ تَعۡلَمُ لَهُۥ سَمِيّٗا﴾} قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنه: هل تعلم له مثلا. ولو كان متحيزا، لكان كل واحد من الجواهر مثلا له. وقوله تعالى: {هل تعلم له سميا}؟ معناه: أن الكفار كانوا يسمون الأصنام آلهة، ولكن لم يسموا صنما اسمه الله، فيكون المعنى: لا أحد من الكفار سمى سميه الله، فهل تعلم له سميا؟ أي لا يوجد إله اسمه الله إلا الله تعالى. ثم قال: وهذا المعنى بعيد. ووجه ثان: هل تعلم له سميا أي مثلا وشبيها، وهذا هو المراد: أي لا إله مثل الله يرجى نفعه ويخشى بأسه. فسميا ليس من الاسم وإنما هي من حقيقة الإله.
The fifth proof: His saying, exalted is He: {Do you know any equal to Him?} Ibn Abbas (may God be pleased with him) said: Do you know any likeness of Him? If He were occupying space, then every one of the atoms would be a likeness of Him. And His saying, exalted is He: {Do you know any equal to Him?} Its meaning: the disbelievers used to name idols 'gods', but they never named any idol 'Allah'. So the meaning is: None of the disbelievers named any being equal to Him 'Allah'. So do you know any equal to Him? That is, there is no god named Allah except Allah. Then he said: This meaning is far-fetched. A second aspect: 'Do you know any equal to Him' meaning any likeness or similitude, and this is what is intended: that is, there is no god like Allah whose benefit is hoped for and whose might is feared. So 'samiyyan' is not from 'ism' (name) but from the reality of divinity.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against corporealism. It begins by citing the Quranic verse "هل تعلم له سميا" (Maryam 65). Imam Razi reports Ibn Abbas's interpretation that it means "Do you know any likeness of Him?" Then he makes the logical argument: if God were a body (mutahayyiz), then every atom (jawhar) would be like Him, meaning there would be multiple beings equal to God, which contradicts His uniqueness. The rest of the passage discusses the possible meanings of "samiyyan" – whether it refers to a namesake or a likeness – and concludes that the intended meaning is "likeness" to assert God's incomparability. The author weighs two interpretations: one based on the name "Allah" being exclusive, and another based on the concept of likeness. He favors the latter as the correct exegesis.

Misconception: One might think 'سمي' means namesake, so the verse only denies that any being is named Allah.
Correction: The argument requires the meaning of 'likeness' to refute corporealism; otherwise, the logical deduction about atoms being like God would not follow.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 'هل تعلم له سميا' proves God's absolute uniqueness by negating any equal or likeness; if God were a body, every atom would be His equal, which is impossible.

Sixth Proof: Creator and Fashioner Negate Corporeality
Al-Razi argues from divine names al-Khaliq and al-Musawwir against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the divine names al-Khaliq (Creator) and al-Musawwir (Fashioner) to demonstrate that Allah cannot be a body.

Core Idea
The Creator cannot be confined by measure; the Fashioner cannot be shaped by form.

Al-Razi presents a subtle linguistic argument: the very names by which Allah describes Himself rule out corporeality. If He were a body, He would be finite and have a form, but as the Determiner of all measures and the Fashioner of all forms, He cannot be subject to measure or form Himself. This proof shows that divine names themselves are theological evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the two parallel arguments.
Pay attention to the linguistic definition of al-Khaliq as al-muqaddir.
2
Understand the definition of al-Khaliq
Al-Razi states that al-Khaliq linguistically means 'the determiner' (al-muqaddir). This is the key premise for the first argument.
3
Follow the reductio from al-Khaliq
Assume Allah is a body leads to finitude and specific measure. But as the Determiner of all measures, He cannot be determined by a measure Himself—impossible.
This shows that the attribute of being Creator is incompatible with corporeality.
4
Follow the reductio from al-Musawwir
Assume Allah is a body leads to having a shape/form. But as the Fashioner of all forms, He cannot fashion Himself—impossible.
This reinforces the conclusion from a different divine name.
5
Conclude the implication
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: Allah is transcendent beyond form and body.
This proof uses sifāt (attributes) as evidence for tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : قوله تعالى : ( هو الله الخالق البارئ المصور (٤٩) وجه الاستدلال به : إنا بينا فى سائر كتبنا أن الخالق في اللغة هو المقدر . ولو كان تعالى جسماً لكان متناهياً ، ولو كان متناهياً لكان مخصوصاً بمقدار معين ، ولما وصف نفسه بكونه خالقاً ، وجب أن يكون تعالى المقدر لجميع المقدرات بمقاديرها المخصوصة ، وإذا كان هو مقدراً في ذاته بمقدار مخصوص لزم كونه مقدراً لنفسه ، وذلك محال . وأيضا : لو كان جسماً ، لكان متناهياً . وكل متناه ، فإنه محيط به حد (واحد)(٥٠) أو حدود مختلفة . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو مشكل . وكل مشكل فله صورة. فلو كان جسماً لكان له صورة . ثم إنه تعالى وصف نفسه بكونه مصوراً ، فيلزم كونه مصوراً لنفسه . وذلك محال . فيلزم أن يكون منزهاً عن الصورة والجسمية . حتى لا يلزم هذا المحال .
The sixth proof: His saying, exalted: 'He is Allah, the Creator, the Maker, the Fashioner.' The way of using it as evidence: We have explained in all our books that 'Creator' (al-Khaliq) in language means the determiner (al-muqaddir). And if He—exalted—were a body, He would be finite, and if finite, He would be specified by a particular measure. And since He described Himself as 'Creator', it must be that He—exalted—is the determiner of all determined things by their particular measures. And if He Himself is determined in His essence by a particular measure, then He would be a determiner of Himself, and that is impossible. Also: if He were a body, He would be finite. And every finite thing is surrounded by a limit, single (50) or different limits. And everything like that is shaped. And every shaped thing has a form. So if He were a body, He would have a form. Then He—exalted—described Himself as 'Fashioner' (al-Musawwir), so it follows that He would be a fashioner of Himself. That is impossible. Thus it follows that He is transcendent beyond form and corporeality, so that this impossibility does not occur.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof from divine names against corporeality. Al-Razi begins by defining 'Creator' (al-Khaliq) linguistically as 'the determiner' (al-muqaddir), i.e., the one who assigns measure and proportion. He then argues: if Allah were a body, He would be finite (mutanāhī) and thus possessed of a specific quantity. But as the Creator, He determines the measures of all things; therefore, He cannot Himself be limited by a measure, as that would make Him the determiner of His own essence, which is absurd. The argument then considers the name 'Fashioner' (al-Musawwir): a body, being finite, has a shape (shakl) and form (ṣūra). But Allah describes Himself as the Fashioner of forms, so if He had a form, He would be fashioning Himself, which is also impossible. Hence, Allah must be transcendent beyond both form and corporeality.

Key Terms
الخالق (al-Khaliq)
المقدر (al-muqaddir)
المصور (al-Musawwir)
متناه (mutanāhī)
صورة (ṣūra)
جسم (jism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think 'al-Khaliq' simply means 'Creator' in the sense of bringing from nothing, without the nuance of determination (taqdir).
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that al-Khaliq linguistically means al-muqaddir (the determiner), which is essential for the proof.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the linguistic meaning of 'al-Khaliq'?
A) The one who brings something from nothing
B) The determiner (al-muqaddir)
C) The fashioner of forms
D) The eternal

Key Takeaway
The divine names al-Khaliq and al-Musawwir logically entail that Allah cannot be a body, because a determiner cannot be self-determined and a fashioner cannot be self-fashioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is beyond any measure, or do you subconsciously imagine Him within spatial or mental limitations? Examine your heart: when you think of Allah, do you sense any hidden tendency to confine Him? Commit to correcting that by reciting al-Khaliq and al-Musawwir with understanding.
?
If a determined creator would be self-contradictory, what does that imply about your own attempts to 'measure' Allah with your intellect? Will you accept His transcendence even when your mind struggles? Make a firm intention to submit to His names as they are.
?
The passage shows that Allah cannot be shaped. How does this affect your posture in worship? Are you aware that you cannot confine Him to any form, even in your imagination? Pledge to purify your concept of Allah from any resemblance to creation.

Seventh Proof: Al-Zahir and Al-Batin
Al-Razi uses divine names al-Zahir and al-Batin to negate corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the divine names al-Zahir and al-Batin to argue that Allah cannot be a body.

Core Idea
The same thing cannot be both wholly apparent and wholly hidden if it has parts.

Al-Razi continues his series of proofs against corporeality by turning to two of Allah's beautiful names: al-Zahir (the Apparent) and al-Batin (the Hidden). These names, found in the Quran itself, provide a powerful logical argument. Before we read his reasoning, consider: if something is a physical object, it has an outside and an inside. Could the same object truly be described as both 'apparent' and 'hidden' without contradiction? Al-Razi shows us why not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the two parts of the argument.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument uses the literal spatial meanings of 'apparent' (surface) and 'hidden' (depth). If Allah were a body, His apparent part would be different from His hidden part, so He could not be both in the same sense.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument relies on the interpretation of commentators: 'apparent' means evident through proofs, 'hidden' means imperceptible to senses and imagination. If Allah were a body, He would be perceptible, contradicting 'hidden'.
4
See the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: corporeality is incompatible with the divine names. Reflect on how names of Allah contain theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى: «هو الأول والآخر والظاهر والباطن (۰۱) وصف نفسه بكونه ظاهراً وباطناً . ولو كان جسماً لكان ظاهره غير باطنه . فلم يكن الشيء الواحد موصوفاً بأنه ظاهر وبأنه باطن، لأن على تقدير كونه جسماً ، يكون الظاهر منه سطحه ، والباطن منه عمقه فلم يكن الشيء الواحد ظاهراً وباطناً . وأيضاً : فالمفسرون (۰۲) قالوا : إنه ظاهر بحسب الدلائل ، باطن بحسب أنه لا يدركه الحس ، ولا يصل إليه الخيال. ولو كان جسماً لما أمكن وصفه بأنه لا يدركه الحس ، ولا يصل إليه الخيال .
The seventh proof: His saying, exalted: 'He is the First and the Last, the Apparent and the Hidden.' He described Himself as being Apparent and Hidden. And if He were a body, then His external would be other than His internal. So the one thing would not be described as being both Apparent and Hidden, because on the assumption of His being a body, the Apparent of Him is His surface, and the Hidden of Him is His depth, so the one thing is not Apparent and Hidden. Also: the commentators said: He is Apparent in terms of proofs, Hidden in terms that the senses do not perceive Him, nor does imagination reach Him. And if He were a body, it would not be possible to describe Him as not being perceived by the senses nor reached by imagination.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the divine names al-Zahir (the Apparent) and al-Batin (the Hidden). Al-Razi presents two arguments. First, if Allah were a body, He would have an external surface distinct from an internal depth; thus the same thing could not simultaneously be both fully apparent (surface) and fully hidden (depth). Second, according to authoritative commentators, 'Apparent' means His existence is evident through proofs, while 'Hidden' means He is imperceptible to sensory and imaginative faculties. If He were a body, He would be capable of being perceived by sense or imagination, contradicting the meaning of al-Batin. Therefore, corporeality is impossible.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-Zahir)
الباطن (al-Batin)
سطح (saṭḥ)
عمق (ʿumq)
حس (ḥiss)
خيال (khayāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'Apparent' (al-Zahir) means physically visible to the eye.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that the commentators interpret 'Apparent' as manifest through rational proofs (dalāʾil), not through sensory sight.

Checkpoint

B) Manifest through proofs
C) Above all creation
D) The first in time

Key Takeaway
Allah's simultaneous description as al-Zahir and al-Batin is incompatible with corporeality, because a body would have spatial parts or be perceptible to the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The commentators say Allah is apparent through proofs. Have you ever personally examined the proofs for His oneness and transcendence, or do you accept them secondhand? What specific proof from this lesson will you internalize today, and how will it change your certainty about who Allah is?

Eighth Proof: Non-Encompassment with Objection and Answer
Al-Razi presents a proof from Quranic verses and refutes an objection about size.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses Quranic verses to argue that Allah cannot be a body, and how he refutes the objection of extreme size.

Core Idea
Allah's unencompassability proves He is not a body, not that He is too big.

The eighth proof in al-Razi's series draws on two Quranic verses that deny encompassment by knowledge and sight. He argues that if Allah were a body, He would be finite and thus subject to encompassment. But what if He is an extremely large body? The objector raises this possibility. Al-Razi's answer shows the fallacy: the same would apply to all large bodies, making the attribute non-exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof verses and inference
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi cites the verses and immediately draws the conclusion that Allah is free from measure, shape, and form.
The verses are specific negations of encompassment.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objector's point: maybe Allah is a large body and that is why He cannot be encompassed. Consider why this might seem plausible.
This objection assumes size is the reason for non-encompassment.
3
Analyze al-Razi's refutation
See how al-Razi turns the objection on its head: if size were the reason, then all large bodies would share this property, making the Quranic description pointless.
The refutation uses logical consistency and the principle of uniqueness.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Conclude that Allah's unencompassability is not due to magnitude but to His non-corporeality. This reinforces tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : ( ولا يحيطون به علما (٥٣) وقوله تعالى: " لا تدركه الأبصار ، (٥٤) وذلك يدل على كونه تعالى منزهاً عن المقدار و الشكل و الصورة . وإلا لكان الإدراك والعلم محيطين به . وذلك على خلاف هذين النصين . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان جسماً كبيراً ، فلهذا المعنى لا يحيط به الإدراك والعلم ؟ قلنا : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لصح أن يقال : بأن علوم الخلق وأبصارهم لا تحيط بالسموات ولا بالجبال ولا بالبحار ولا بالمفاوز ، فإن هذه الأشياء : أجسام كبيرة ، والأبصار لا تحيط بأطرافها ، والعلوم لا تصل إلى تمام أجزائها . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما كان في تخصيص ذات الله تعالى بهذا الوصف فائدة .
The eighth proof: His saying, exalted: 'They cannot encompass Him with knowledge' and His saying, exalted: 'Sights do not perceive Him.' And that indicates that He—exalted—is transcendent beyond measure, shape, and form. Otherwise, perception and knowledge would encompass Him. And that contradicts these two texts. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that He—even if He is a large body—because of that meaning, perception and knowledge do not encompass Him? We say: If that were the case, it would be correct to say that the knowledge and sights of creatures do not encompass the heavens, nor the mountains, nor the seas, nor the deserts, for these things are large bodies, and sights do not encompass their extremities, and knowledge does not reach all their parts. And if that were the case, there would be no benefit in singling out the essence of Allah—exalted—with this description.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof from Quranic verses that negate encompassment by knowledge (20:110) and sight (6:103), followed by an objection and its refutation. Al-Razi argues that if Allah were a body, He would be finite and thus subject to intellectual and visual encompassment, contradicting the texts. The objector suggests that perhaps Allah is a large body, too vast to be fully known or seen. Al-Razi answers: if that were valid, the same would apply to all large bodies like heavens and mountains, making the attribute non-exclusive to Allah. Since the Quran specifically describes Allah as unencompassed, this shows He is not a body at all—neither small nor large.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrāk)
علم (ʿilm)
مقدار (miqdār)
شكل (shakl)
صورة (ṣūra)
جسم كبير (jism kabīr)
فائدة (fāʾida)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is unencompassable because He is an extremely large body that cannot be fully seen or known.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if size were the reason, the same would apply to all large bodies, negating the unique emphasis in the Quran. Thus unencompassability proves non-corporeality.

Checkpoint

B) Allah could be a large body too great to be fully seen
C) The verse is metaphorical
D) Sights can perceive Him in dreams

Key Takeaway
Allah's unique unencompassability confirms He is not a body, neither small nor large; otherwise the Quranic attribute would lose its exclusive significance.

Divine Nearness Refutes Spatial Location
Al-Razi uses the verse of nearness and hadith context to refute spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse of divine nearness and the accompanying hadith refute the notion that Allah is spatially located.

Core Idea
Divine nearness in the Quran is non-spatial, refuting physical location.

Al-Razi presents a scriptural proof that divine nearness cannot be understood as spatial proximity. The Prophet was asked whether God is near so that we whisper to Him, or far so that we call out. The verse that was revealed answers by affirming closeness. Al-Razi argues that if God were physically located in heaven or upon the Throne, He would be far from His servants on earth, contradicting the verse. Therefore, God is not a body occupying space.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic verse and the hadith context. Then review the English translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
The verse is Quran 2:186, and the hadith provides the occasion of revelation.
2
Understand the hadith context
The companions asked whether God is near (so they can whisper) or far (so they must call out). The verse's revelation shows that God is near, not far.
This context is crucial for Al-Razi's argument.
3
Grasp the logical inference
If God were physically in heaven or on the Throne, He would be far from earth. But the verse says He is near. Therefore, God is not spatially located.
This refutes anthropomorphists who claim a physical location for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : «وإذا سألك عبادي عني ، فإني قريب . أجيب دعوة الداع إذا دعانى . فليستجيبوا لي، وليؤمنوا بي . لعلهم ير شدون ، (٥٥) وسئل النبي صلى الله عليه (٥٦) وسلم: أقريب ربنا فنناجيه ، أم بعيد فنناديه ؟ فأنزل الله تعالى هذه الآية. ولو كان تعالى في السماء أو في العرش ، لما صح القول بأنه تعالى قريب من عباده .
The ninth proof: His saying, exalted: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, indeed I am near. I answer the call of the caller when he calls Me. So let them respond to Me and believe in Me, that they may be guided.' And the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) was asked: Is our Lord near so we whisper to Him, or far so we call out? So Allah revealed this verse. And if He—exalted—were in the heaven or upon the throne, it would not be correct to say that He—exalted—is near to His servants.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quranic verse (2:186) about divine nearness and the accompanying hadith. The Prophet was asked whether God is near (so they can whisper in prayer) or far (requiring them to call out). The verse was revealed affirming God's nearness. Al-Razi argues: if God were physically located in heaven or on the Throne, He would be far from His servants on earth, contradicting the verse's clear declaration of nearness. Therefore, God is not a body occupying space.

Key Terms
قريب (qarīb)
بعيد (baʿīd)
مناجاة (munājāt)
نداء (nidāʾ)
السماء (al-samāʾ)
العرش (al-ʿarsh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nearness' in the verse means spatial proximity, implying Allah is close in distance.
Correction: Al-Razi uses the hadith context to show that if Allah were spatially located (e.g., in heaven or on the Throne), He would be far from earth. The verse affirms nearness, so it cannot be spatial; it must be a non-corporeal closeness.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Divine nearness in the Quran is not spatial; it affirms Allah's transcendence above physical location.

The Eighth Proof: Divine Transcendence from Encompassment
Razi presents the eighth proof from Quranic verses on encompassment and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quranic verses to argue that God cannot be a body, and why the objection of a 'large body' fails.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He could be encompassed—but the Quran denies this.

Razi presents a powerful argument from two Quranic verses: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' and 'Eyes do not perceive Him.' He shows that these verses logically require God to be free from physical attributes like size and shape. A clever objection is raised and swiftly refuted, strengthening the case for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of Razi's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a question-and-answer format here.
2
Identify the two Quranic verses and the conclusion
Find the two verses: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' and 'Eyes do not perceive Him.' Razi concludes that God is free from quantity, shape, and form. Understand the logical link: if God had these, He could be encompassed.
The conclusion is not just stated; it is implied by the verses.
3
Understand the objection
Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps God is a large body, and that is why He cannot be encompassed. This objection tries to reconcile the verses with a corporealist view.
The objection is presented with 'فإن قيل' (if it is said).
4
See Razi's refutation
Razi responds ('قلنا') that if large size prevented encompassment, then the same would apply to all large bodies like heavens and mountains, making the Quranic specification of God pointless. Therefore, the only consistent interpretation is that God is not a body.
This refutation uses reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider the strength of the proof: it is based on explicit Quranic texts and logical reasoning. The objection is preempted and refuted, leaving no room for a corporeal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : ( ولا يحيطون به علما ) وقوله تعالى: " لا تدركه الأبصار ، وذلك يدل على كونه تعالى منزهاً عن المقدار و الشكل و الصورة . وإلا لكان الإدراك والعلم محيطين به . وذلك على خلاف هذين النصين ، فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان اسكنه جسم كبير ، فلهذا المعنى لا يحيط به الإدراك والعلم ؟ قلنا : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لصح أن يقال : بأن علوم الخلق وأبصارهم لا تحيط بالسموات ولا بالجبال ولا بالبحار ولا بالمفاوز ، فإن هذه الأشياء : أجسام كبيرة ، والأبصار لا تحيط بأطرافها ، والعلوم لا تصل إلى تمام أجزائها ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما كان في تخصيص ذات الله تعالى بهذا الوصف فائدة
The eighth argument: His statement, exalted: 'And they do not encompass Him with knowledge' (20:110) and His statement: 'Eyes do not perceive Him' (6:103). This indicates that He, exalted, is transcendent over quantity, shape, and form. Otherwise, perception and knowledge would encompass Him. That contradicts these two texts. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that even if He is a large body, for this reason perception and knowledge do not encompass Him? We say: If the matter were so, it would be correct to say that the knowledge and sight of creatures do not encompass the heavens, mountains, seas, and deserts—for these things are large bodies; sight does not encompass their extremities, and knowledge does not reach all their parts. If that were the case, there would be no benefit in specifying the essence of God, exalted, with this description.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof by argument from Quranic texts. Razi begins by citing two verses: 'They do not encompass Him with knowledge' (20:110) and 'Eyes do not perceive Him' (6:103). He argues that these verses prove God is transcendent over quantity (miqdar), shape (shakl), and form (surah) because if He had these attributes, He would be subject to being encompassed by knowledge or perception, which the verses deny. He then presents a potential objection: even if God were a huge body, perhaps He still could not be fully encompassed due to His size. Razi refutes this by noting that if this were acceptable, the same could be said of any large created object like the heavens and mountains, making the Quranic specification pointless. Thus, the only consistent interpretation is that God is not a body at all.

Key Terms
الإحاطة
الإدراك
المقدار
الشكل
الصورة
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is a large body that cannot be fully encompassed due to size.
Correction: Razi shows that if that were the case, the same would apply to all large bodies, making the Quranic specification meaningless; therefore God must not be a body at all.

Checkpoint

B) If God were a body, He could be encompassed, contradicting Quranic denial.
C) The Quran says God has form but we cannot see it.
D) God is near to us despite being in the sky.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's denial that anything encompasses God proves He is not a body, because even a huge body would be subject to potential encompassment in principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Ninth Proof: Divine Closeness Denies Spatial Direction
Razi presents the ninth proof: a Quranic verse and hadith refuting spatial direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quranic verse of closeness and the occasion of revelation to argue that Allah is not spatially located.

Core Idea
If Allah were in a place, He would not be equally near to all servants.

The Companions once asked the Prophet whether their Lord was near or far. The Quranic response — 'I am near' — seems simple, but for Razi it becomes a powerful argument against anthropomorphism. In this lesson, we will examine how the verse of closeness and its occasion of revelation lead to the conclusion that Allah transcends spatial direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its occasion of revelation
First, read the Arabic verse, the hadith context, and Razi's conclusion. Notice that the Companions' question is about the mode of prayer — whispering or calling out — which implies a concern about spatial distance.
2
Identify Razi's logical deduction
Razi argues that if Allah were in the sky or on the Throne, it would be incorrect to say He is near to all servants. Why? Because spatial nearness varies; something cannot be equally near to people in different locations. Therefore, Allah's nearness is not spatial.
This refutes those who claim Allah is literally in a direction.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
What does this tell us about Allah's transcendence? Razi shows that God's closeness is by knowledge and power, not by physical location. This guards against associating Allah with creation.
This is a key argument in kalam for tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : «وإذا سألك عبادي عني ، فإني قريب . أجيب دعوة الداع إذا دعانى . فليستجيبوا لي، وليؤمنوا بي . لعلهم بر شدون ، وسئل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أقريب ربنا فنناجيه ، أم بعيد فنناديه ؟ فأنزل الله تعالى هذه الآية. ولو كان تعالى في السماء أو في العرش ، لما صح القول بأنه تعالى قريب من عباده .
The ninth argument: His statement, exalted: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then indeed I am near. I answer the call of the supplicant when he calls Me. So let them respond to Me and believe in Me, that they may be guided.' (2:186) The Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, was asked: Is our Lord near so we may whisper to Him, or far so we may call out? Then God revealed this verse. And if He, exalted, were in the sky or on the Throne, it would not be correct to say that He, exalted, is near to His servants.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quranic verse of closeness (qurb). Razi cites the verse (2:186) where God says 'I am near' and mentions the occasion of revelation: the Companions asked the Prophet whether their Lord was near or far. The verse answers that He is near. Razi then deduces that if God were spatially located in the sky or on the Throne, it would be false to say He is 'near' to all servants equally, since nearness in space would vary. Therefore, God's closeness must be by knowledge and power, not by spatial location. This refutes anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
قريب
السماء
العرش
الدعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse means Allah is physically close in space.
Correction: Razi argues that if Allah were in a specific direction, He could not be equally near to all, so the nearness must be non-spatial.

Checkpoint

A) To prove God answers prayers.
B) To show God is not physically distant.
C) To show the Quran is true.
D) To prove the Prophet's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the Quranic statement 'I am near' and the occasion of revelation to prove that Allah is not spatially located, because spatial nearness would vary.

The Tenth Proof: God Cannot Be in a Direction Above
Razi proves that being in a direction above would make God a created thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's linguistic and logical argument that God cannot be in a direction above, because it would entail His being created.

Core Idea
If God were above, He would be a 'sky' — and the Quran says all skies are created.

Razi presents a sophisticated proof that the divine attribute of elevation is impossible. He first argues that any elevated entity is linguistically and logically a 'sky' (sama'), then shows that the Quran explicitly states all skies are created. Thus, placing God in an upward direction would imply that He is created — a clear contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice Razi's two-part structure: first linguistic, then logical.
The passage is a complete proof chain.
2
Understand the linguistic proof
Razi argues that the word 'sky' derives from 'height', so anything elevated is a sky. He cites Quranic usage calling clouds 'sky' because they are high.
This establishes that if God is above, He qualifies as a 'sky' linguistically.
3
Understand the logical proof
Razi uses an analogy: if God were above the Throne, an observer on the Throne would see God's extremity as the sky. Thus God would be a 'sky' for throne-inhabitants.
This reinforces the linguistic point with a thought experiment.
4
See the conclusion
Since the Quran says all heavens (samawat) are created, if God were a sky, He would be created—an impossibility. Therefore, God cannot be in a direction above.
The passage ends before restating the final conclusion, but it is logically implied.

The tenth argument: If He, exalted, were in a direction above, He would be sky (sama'), and if He were sky, He would be a creator of Himself. That is impossible. So His being in a direction above is impossible. We said that He (exalted) if He were in a direction above would be sky for two reasons: First: Sky is derived from suww (height), so everything that is high (sama) is sky. This is the original linguistic derivation. And the custom of the Quran is established upon it, as they mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'And He sends down from the sky mountains of hail' (24:43) — that it means the clouds. They said: Calling clouds 'sky' is permissible because it has the meaning of height. And they also mentioned in interpretation of His statement: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' (25:48) — that it is from the clouds. So it is established that the linguistic derivation and the Quranic custom agree upon naming everything described with height and elevation as 'sky.' Second: If He, exalted, were above the Throne, then whoever sits on the Throne and looks upward would see nothing but the extremity of God's essence. Thus the ratio of the limit of the last surface of God's essence to the inhabitants of the Throne would be like the ratio of the last surface of the heavens to the inhabitants of the earth. This necessarily determines that if He were above the Throne, His essence would be like a sky for the inhabitants of the Throne. So it is established that if He, exalted, were specifically in a direction above, His essence would be sky. And we said: if His essence were sky, then His essence would be created — by His statement: 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the high heavens' (20:4). The word 'the heavens' (al-samawat) is a plural noun with the definite article. This necessitates that all heavens are created by God. So if He, exalted, were a sky, it would imply that He is the creator of Himself. Likewise, His statement: 'Indeed your Lord is God who created the heavens and the earth in six days' (7:54) indicates what we mentioned. So it is established that He...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof by linguistic and logical reasoning. Razi argues that if God were in an upward direction, He would be called 'sky' (sama') based on two points: (1) Linguistic derivation: 'sama'' comes from 'suww' meaning height, so anything high is called sky. The Quran itself calls clouds 'sama'' because they are high. (2) Logical: If God were above the Throne, one looking up from the Throne would see only God's surface, making God's essence analogous to a sky for throne-inhabitants, just as the physical sky is for earth-dwellers. Therefore, God would be a 'sky' in that sense. Then, since the Quran explicitly states that all heavens (samawat) are created (e.g., 20:4, 7:54), if God were a sky, He would be created, which implies He created Himself — an impossibility. Therefore, God cannot be in a direction above. The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is that God is not in a direction.

Linguistic and Quranic Definition of 'Heaven'
Imam Razi defines 'heaven' linguistically and Quranically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic term 'heaven' (السماء) linguistically refers to anything that is elevated, and that this is confirmed by Quranic usage.

Core Idea
In Arabic, 'heaven' means anything that rises above you—not just the sky.

Imam Razi begins by establishing the meaning of a key term: 'heaven'. Before any argument about Allah's location can be made, we must know what the word actually means. He shows that both language and the Quran agree: 'heaven' refers to anything elevated, including clouds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the linguistic derivation, then Quranic evidence, then conclusion.
The passage uses two Quranic verses as proof.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
Imam Razi states that 'heaven' (السماء) is derived from 'height' (السمو). Therefore, anything that rises above you is a 'heaven'. This is the original linguistic meaning.
This is a key point: the term is not limited to the sky.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses cited. In 24:43, 'heaven' is interpreted as clouds. In 25:48, water is sent down from 'heaven', meaning clouds. This shows the Quran uses 'heaven' for what is elevated.
The commentators confirm this broader meaning.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The linguistic derivation and Quranic usage agree: everything described by elevation and height can be called 'heaven'. This broad definition is essential for later theological arguments.
This lays the groundwork for the next discussion on divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن السماء مشتق من السمو ، فكل شيء سماك فهو سماء . فهذا هو الاشتقاق الأصلى اللغوى . وعرف القرآن أيضاً : متقرر عليه بدليل أنهم ذكروا في تفسير قوله تعالى : ( وينزل من السماء ، من جبال ، فيها من برد، (۰۸) : إنه السحاب . قالوا : وتسمية السحاب بالسماء جائز ، لأنه . حصل فيه معنى السمو . وذكروا أيضاً في تفسير قوله تعالى : «وأنزلنا من السماء ماء طهور (۰۹) : إنه من السحاب . فثبت : أن الاشتقاق اللغوى، والعرف القرآنى متطابقان على تسمية كل ما كان موصوفا بالسمو والعلو : ( بأنه ) (٦٠) سماء .
First: The word 'heaven' (السماء) is derived from 'height' (السمو), so everything that rises above you is a heaven (سماء). So this is the original linguistic derivation. And the Quranic usage also confirms it, by the evidence that they mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'And He sends down from the sky, from mountains [of hail] within it' (Quran 24:43) that it is the clouds. They said: Naming the clouds as 'heaven' is permissible because it involves the meaning of height. And they also mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'And We send down from the sky pure water' (Quran 25:48) that it is from the clouds. So it is established that the linguistic derivation and the Quranic usage agree in naming everything that is described by elevation and height as 'heaven'.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the meaning of the term 'heaven' (سماء). Imam Razi first gives the linguistic derivation: 'heaven' comes from 'height' (السمو), so anything that rises above you is called 'heaven'. He then shows from Quranic exegesis that even clouds are called 'heaven' in the Quran because they are elevated. He cites two verses: one where 'heaven' is interpreted as clouds, and another where 'water from the heaven' means from clouds. The conclusion is that the linguistic and Quranic usage agree: everything characterized by elevation and height can be called 'heaven'. This lays the groundwork for the next argument: if Allah were above the Throne, He would be a 'heaven' and hence a created thing.

The Impossibility of Allah Being Above the Throne
Imam Razi proves Allah cannot be above the Throne via reductio and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that Allah cannot be above the Throne and how he responds to objections based on linguistic usage.

Core Idea
If Allah were above the Throne, He would be a created thing—an impossibility.

Imam Razi presents a powerful logical argument to demonstrate that Allah cannot be in a direction. Using a reductio ad absurdum, he shows that the assumption that Allah is above the Throne leads to the absurd conclusion that Allah would be a created being. He then addresses potential objections rooted in language and necessity, reinforcing the absolute transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a main argument followed by two objections and their answers.
Pay attention to the logical chain: assumption → consequence → impossibility.
2
Understand the reductio argument
Trace the reasoning: if Allah were above the Throne, His essence would be like a heaven to those on the Throne. Since all heavens are created, Allah would be created, which is impossible. Therefore, the assumption is false.
This is a classic theological proof of divine transcendence.
3
Analyze the objections
Two objections are raised: (1) 'heaven' normally refers to celestial bodies; (2) the word can be specified to exclude Allah. Understand how Razi refutes each.
Note Razi's use of proportion argument and necessity principle.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: How does this argument guard against anthropomorphism? What does it establish about Allah's relation to creation?
This connects to the broader concept of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه تعالى لو كان فوق العرش ، لكان من جلس في العرش ونظر إلى فرق ، لم ير إلا نهاية ذات الله تعالى. فكانت نسبة نهاية السطح الأخير من ذات الله تعالى إلى سكان العرش ، كنسبة السطح الأخير من السموات إلى سكان الأرض. وذلك يقتضى القطع بأنه لو كان فوق العرش لمكان ذاته كالسماء لسكان العرش. فثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان مختصاً بجهة فوق ، لكان ذاته سماء . وإنما قلنا : إنه لو كان ذاته سماء ، لكان ذاته مخلوقا . لقوله تعالى : « تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى (٦١). و لفظة « السموات ، : لفظة جمع مقرونة بالألف واللام . وهذا يقتضى كون كل السموات مخلوقه الله تعالى . فلو كان هو تعالى سماء ، ازم کونه خالقا لنفسه . وكذلك أيضاً : قوله تعالى : إن ربكم الله الذي خلق السموات والأرض في ستة أيام (٦٢) بدل على ما ذكرناه . فثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان مختصاً بجهة فوق ، لكان سماء ، ولو كان سماء ، لكان. مخلوقا لنفسه . وهذا محال . فوجب أن لا يكون مختصاً بجهة فوق .

فإن قيل : لفظ السماء مختص فى العرف بهذه الأجرام المستديرة .. وأيضاً : فهم أن هذا اللفظ في أصل الوضع يتناول ذات الله تعالى ، إلا أن تخصيص العموم جائز . قلنا : أما الجواب عن الأول : فهو أن هذا الفوق (٦٣) ممنوع . وكيف لا نقول ذلك ، وقد دللنا على أن بتقدير أن يكون الله تعالى مختصاً بجهة فوق ) إلا أن (٦٤)) نسبة ذاته تعالى إلى سكان العرش كنسبة السماء إلى سكان الأرض؟ فوجب القطع بأنه لو كان مختصا بجهة فوق ، لكان سماء . وأما الجواب عن الثاني : فهو أن تخصيص العموم إنما يصار إليه عند الضرورة . فلو قام دليل قاطع عقلى على كونه تعالى مختصا بجهة فوق ، لزمنا المصير إلى هذا التخصيص . وإذا لم يقم (٦٠) شيء من الدلائل على ذلك، بل قامت القواطع العقلية والنقلية على امتناع كونه تعالى في الجهة ، لم يكن بنا إلى التزام هذا التخصيص: ضرورة . فسقط هذا الكلام .
Second: If He, exalted, were above the Throne, then whoever sits on the Throne and looks above would see nothing but the end of the essence of Allah. So the proportion of the last surface of the essence of Allah to the inhabitants of the Throne would be like the proportion of the last surface of the heavens to the inhabitants of the earth. That necessitates the definitive conclusion that if He were above the Throne, His essence would be like the heaven for the inhabitants of the Throne. So it is established that if He, exalted, were specific with the direction of above, then His essence would be a heaven. And we said: If His essence were a heaven, then His essence would be created, because of His statement: 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the high heavens' (Quran 20:4). And the word 'heavens' (السموات) is a plural word with the definite article. This necessitates that all heavens are created by Allah, the Exalted. So if He, exalted, were a heaven, it would necessitate His being the creator of Himself. And similarly, His statement: 'Indeed your Lord is Allah who created the heavens and the earth in six days' (Quran 7:54) indicates what we have mentioned. So it is established that if He were specific with the direction of above, He would be a heaven; and if He were a heaven, He would be the creator of Himself. This is impossible. So it is necessary that He is not specific with the direction of above.

If it is said: The word 'heaven' is specific in common usage to these spherical bodies... And also, it is understood that this word in its original coinage encompasses the essence of Allah, but specification of the general is permissible. We say: As for the answer to the first: It is that this 'being above' is prevented. How can we not say that, when we have proven that on the assumption that Allah, exalted, is specific with the direction of above, the proportion of His essence to the inhabitants of the Throne is like the proportion of the heaven to the inhabitants of the earth? So it is definitively concluded that if He were specific with the direction of above, He would be a heaven. As for the answer to the second: It is that specification of the general is only resorted to in necessity. So if a definitive intellectual proof existed for His being specific with the direction of above, we would be compelled to resort to this specification. But when no proof exists for that, rather intellectual and transmitted proofs exist for the impossibility of His being in a direction, we have no need to commit to this specification necessarily. So this statement falls.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reductio ad absurdum followed by an objection-and-answer. Imam Razi first argues: if Allah were above the Throne, then those on the Throne would see the 'end' of Allah's essence, making His essence like a heaven relative to them. Since everything called 'heaven' is created (as per Quranic verses), His essence would be created, implying He created Himself, which is impossible. Therefore, Allah cannot be above the Throne. The objector then raises two points: (1) 'heaven' is commonly used only for celestial spheres, and (2) if the word originally applies to Allah, it can be specified to exclude Him. Razi answers: (1) the proportion argument shows that on the assumption of being above, the term 'heaven' applies necessarily; (2) specification requires necessity, but no proof for direction exists, while proofs against it are decisive. Thus the objection falls.

تخصيص العموم

Misconception: Allah is literally above the Throne in a spatial direction.
Correction: Imam Razi shows this belief leads to the impossible conclusion that Allah would be a created being, so it must be rejected.

Checkpoint

A) Allah is above the Throne
B) Allah cannot be above the Throne
C) The Throne is eternal
D) Heaven is uncreated

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be in a direction because that would imply He is created, an impossibility.
