Proof from Hadith: God Existed Before All Things
Razi argues that if God occupied place, place would have coexisted with Him, contradicting the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the famous hadith to argue that God does not occupy place or direction.

Core Idea
If God were in a place, that place would be a thing coexisting with God.

This lesson presents Razi's eighteenth proof for divine transcendence. Drawing on the well-known hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) states that God existed with nothing else, Razi argues that if God were limited to a specific location, that location would itself be a thing accompanying Him—directly contradicting the hadith. We will examine this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Razi's argument
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi cites the hadith directly.
2
Understand the implication of place
Razi argues: if God were in a specific place (hayyiz) or direction (jiha), then that place would be a thing existing with God. Ask yourself: why would that be a problem?
3
Recognize the contradiction
The hadith says 'nothing was with Him.' But if God occupied place, that place would be something with Him. That is a direct contradiction. Therefore, God cannot be in place or direction.
This is the logical conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة عشرة : الحديث المشهور : وهو ما روى أن عمران بن الحصين قال : يا رسول الله أخبرنا عن أول هذا الأمر . فقال : كان الله ، ولم يكن معه شيء ، وقد دللنا مرارا كثيرة على أنه تعالى لو كان مختصا بالحيز والجهة ، لكان ذلك الحيز شيئا موجودا معه . وذلك على نقيض هذا النص
The eighteenth proof: The famous hadith, which is what was narrated that 'Imran ibn Husayn said: O Messenger of God, inform us about the beginning of this matter. He said: 'God existed, and nothing was with Him.' And we have demonstrated many times that if He, exalted, were particularized by spatial location and direction (al-hayyiz wa al-jiha), then that spatial location would be something existing with Him. And that is contrary to this text.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the famous hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn. The Prophet said: 'There was God, and nothing was with Him.' Razi argues that if God were restricted to a place (hayyiz) or direction (jiha), then that place would be a thing existing alongside Him, contradicting the hadith's statement that nothing existed with Him. Therefore, God is not in place or direction.

Key Terms

Misconception: Some interpret 'nothing' as 'no created thing', allowing God to be in place as long as no creation is there.
Correction: Razi argues that even place itself would be a 'thing' existing with God, contradicting the hadith's absolute statement 'nothing was with Him'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'There was God and nothing with Him' proves that God is not confined to place or direction.

Proof from Hadith: The Word 'Ama and Negation of Direction
Razi analyzes a hadith to argue for the qasr reading that negates direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about 'ama (blindness) supports divine transcendence by negating spatial direction.

Core Idea
The correct reading of 'ama in the hadith negates all direction from Allah.

Imam Razi continues his proofs for tanzih by examining a famous hadith. The key lies in the vocalization of the word 'ama: with madd it means a cloud (implying place), but with qasr it means blindness (negating visibility and direction). Razi shows that the latter is the correct reading and is strengthened by the rest of the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hadith and Translation
First, read the Arabic hadith and its literal translation. Notice the question 'Where was our Lord?' and the answer using the word 'ama.
The hadith is well-known; pay attention to the word in question.
2
Understand the Two Readings
Learn the two possible vocalizations: 'ama (with madd) meaning a fine cloud, and 'ama (with qasr) meaning blindness. Each leads to a different theological implication.
The difference is a single letter: madd vs. qasr.
3
Why the Qasr Reading is Preferred
Scholars argue that the qasr reading better supports tanzih because blindness implies the absence of direction (jiha). If Allah were in a direction, that direction would be visible; blindness negates visibility.
Razi explicitly sides with those who prefer the qasr reading.
4
Support from the Rest of the Hadith
The Prophet's addition 'no water under Him, nor air above Him' reinforces that there is no spatial environment, confirming the negation of direction.
This phrase eliminates the possibility of a location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشرة : روى أن النبي ﷺ قيل له : أين كان ربنا ؟ قال كان في عماء ليس تحته ماء ، ولا فوقه هواء . قيل : العماء - بالمد - : الغيم الرقيق. وأما العمى بالقصر - : فهو عبارة عن الحالة المضادة للبصر . وقال بعض العلماء : يجب أن تكون الرواية الصحيحة هي الرواية بالقصر حيث إذ تدل على نفي الجهة . لأن الجهة إذا لم تكن موجودة ، لم تكن مرئية . فأمكن جعل العمى عبارة عن عدم الجهة . ويتأكد هذا بقوله عليه السلام : ليس تحته ماء ، ولا فوقه هواء
The nineteenth proof: It was narrated that the Prophet ﷺ was asked: 'Where was our Lord?' He said: 'He was in 'ama' (عماء/عمى): there is no water under Him, nor air above Him.' It was said: 'Al-'ama' (with madd) is the fine cloud. But al-'ama (with qasr) is the condition opposite to sight.' Some scholars said: 'The correct narration must be the one with qasr, since it indicates negation of direction (jiha), because if direction does not exist, it is not visible. So it is possible to make 'ama (blindness) an expression for the absence of direction.' And this is strengthened by his statement: 'There is no water under Him, nor air above Him.'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a proof from a hadith about the Prophet's answer to 'Where was our Lord?' The answer uses the word 'ama'. Razi discusses two readings: 'ama (with madd) meaning a cloud, and 'ama (with qasr) meaning blindness. He sides with scholars who prefer the qasr reading, because it supports negation of direction (jiha): if God were in a direction, that direction would be visible, but 'blindness' implies invisibility and absence of direction. The additional phrase 'no water under Him, nor air above Him' reinforces that there is no surrounding environment, hence no spatial location.

Key Terms
عماء
عمى
الجهة
الغيم الرقيق
عدم الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith uses 'ama to mean a cloud, implying Allah is in a physical place.
Correction: Razi shows the correct reading is 'ama (blindness), which negates direction and thus any spatial location.

Checkpoint

A) It is grammatically more sound.
B) It supports the negation of direction.
C) It appears in more narrations.
D) It is easier to pronounce.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about 'ama, when correctly read as blindness, powerfully negates any spatial direction from Allah.

Razi's Conclusion: The Proofs of Tanzih
Razi's concluding note on the proofs of tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that despite varying strength, the cumulative evidence from Quran and hadith firmly establishes Allah's transcendence beyond space and direction.

Core Idea
The cumulative evidence for tanzih is decisive, even if some proofs are stronger than others.

After presenting several proofs, Imam Razi summarizes his findings. He acknowledges that not all proofs are equally strong, but the overall conclusion is clear: the Quran and hadith are replete with evidence for God's transcendence. This lesson captures his final note before moving to a textual discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's concluding note
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that Razi does not claim all proofs are equally strong, but that the overall weight is sufficient.
This is a typical scholarly acknowledgment of varying strength.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the words تنزيه, الحيز, الجهة. Understand that these are central to the doctrine of transcendence.
3
Reflect on the cumulative argument
Consider why Razi mentions the varying strength. It shows intellectual honesty while affirming the final conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه التي ذكرناها ، بعضها قوى ، وبعضها ضعيف . وكيفما كان الأمر فقد ثبت أن في القرآن والأخبار دلائل كثيرة ، تدل على تنزيه الله تعالى عن الحيز والجهة . وبالله التوفيق .
(٨٤) قال عبد الله بن مسلم بن قتيبة المتوفى سنة ٢٧٦ هـ في كتابه « تأويل مختلف الحديث » : قالوا : رويتم في حديث أبي رزين العقيلي ، من روابة حماد ابن سلمة ، أنه قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : أين كان ربنا ، قبل أن يخلق السموات والأرض ؟ فقال : « كان في عماء ، فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء » . قالوا : وهذا تحديد وتشبيه . قال أبو محمد : ونحن تقول : ان حديث أبي رزين هذا مختلف فيه ، وقد جاء من غير هذا الوجه بألفاظ تستشنع أيضا ، والنقلة له أعراب . ووكيع بن حدس الذي روى عنه حديث حماد بن سلمة أيضا لا يعرف . غير أنه قد تكلم في تفسير هذا الحديث : أبو عبيد ، القاسم بن سلام . حدثنا عنه أحمد بن سعيد اللحياني ، أنه قال : « العماء » : السحاب . وهو كما ذكر في كلام العرب ، ان كان الحرف ممدودا . وإن كان مقصورا ، كأنه كان في عمى . فانه أراد كان في عمى عن معرفة الناس . كما تقول : عميت عن هذا الأمر ، فأنا أعمى عنه ، عمى : اذا أشكل عليك ، لم تعرفه ، ولم تعرف جهته . وكل شيء خفي عليك ، فهو في عمى عنك . وأما قوله : « فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء » فان قوما زادوا فيه ما فقالوا : « ما فوقه هواء ، وما تحته هواء » استيحاشا من أن يكون فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء ، ويكون بينهما . والرواية هي الأولى والوحشة لا تزول بزيادة « ما » لأن فوق وتحت باقيان . والله أعلم .
(۸۵) أقوى من بعض : خ
And know: that these proofs that we have mentioned, some are strong and some are weak. However the matter may be, it has been established that in the Quran and the hadiths there are many evidences indicating the transcendence (tanzih) of Allah, exalted, above spatial location (al-hayyiz) and direction (al-jiha). And success is with Allah.
(84) 'Abdullah bin Muslim bin Qutayba (d. 276 AH) said in his book 'Ta'wil Mukhtalif al-Hadith': They said: You have narrated in the hadith of Abu Razin al-'Uqayli, from the transmission of Hammad ibn Salama, that he said to the Prophet (ﷺ): Where was our Lord before He created the heavens and the earth? He said: 'He was in 'ama, above Him is air, and below Him is air.' They said: This is specification and anthropomorphism (tashbih). Abu Muhammad said: We say: This hadith of Abu Razin is disputed, and it has come from other chains with expressions that are also objectionable, and its transmitters are Bedouins. And Waki' ibn Hudus, from whom Hammad ibn Salama narrated, is not known. However, Abu 'Ubaid al-Qasim ibn Sallam has spoken on the interpretation of this hadith. Ahmad ibn Sa'id al-Lihyani narrated to us from him that he said: 'Al-'ama' (with madd) means the cloud. And it is as mentioned in the speech of the Arabs, if the letter is lengthened. But if it is shortened (maqsur), it is as if he meant 'was in blindness ('ama) regarding the knowledge of people.' As you say: 'I became blind ('amitu) about this matter, so I am blind (a'ma) about it,' meaning it became obscure to you, you did not know it and did not know its direction. And everything that is hidden from you is in 'ama (blindness) concerning you. As for his statement: 'above Him is air, and below Him is air,' some people added 'ma' to it and said: 'there is no air above Him and no air below Him,' out of discomfort that there is air above Him and air below Him, and He is between them. The first narration is the correct one, and the discomfort is not removed by adding 'ma', because 'above' and 'below' remain. And Allah knows best.
(85) 'Stronger than some' in manuscript.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, Razi offers a concluding note: although some of the proofs he presented are strong and some weak, the overall result is that both Quran and hadith contain abundant evidence for God's transcendence (tanzih) beyond space and direction. Second, he quotes a long extract from Ibn Qutayba's 'Ta'wil Mukhtalif al-Hadith' which addresses a variant of the 'ama hadith that includes the phrase 'above Him is air, below Him is air'. Ibn Qutayba critiques the chain, noting that the transmitters are unknown or Bedouins, and then offers the interpretation of Abu 'Ubaid: if 'ama is read with a short vowel (qasr), it means 'blindness'—i.e., God was unknown in terms of direction or location. He also discusses the addition of 'ma' in the phrase 'no air above Him, no air below Him', arguing that adding 'ma' does not eliminate the implication of 'above' and 'below', so the discomfort remains. The marginal note (85) indicates a variant reading 'stronger than some' in another manuscript.

Key Terms
تنزيه
الحيز
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all proofs for tanzih are equally strong.
Correction: Razi explicitly says some are strong and some weak, but the overall conclusion remains supported.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the proofs he presented?
A) All are equally strong.
B) Some are strong and some are weak.
C) All are weak.
D) Only one is strong.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that the Quran and hadith provide many evidences for Allah's transcendence beyond space and direction, even though some proofs are stronger than others.

Ibn Qutayba's Commentary on the 'ama Hadith
Ibn Qutayba's commentary on the 'ama hadith addressing its chain and interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Qutayba's response to the objection that the 'ama hadith implies anthropomorphism, including his critique of its chain and his linguistic interpretation.

Core Idea
The 'ama hadith can be interpreted non-anthropomorphically through linguistic analysis and isn't sound.

One problematic hadith that seems to suggest Allah was in a location is the 'ama hadith. Objectors argue it implies tashbih. Imam Razi cites Ibn Qutayba's detailed refutation. In this lesson, we explore Ibn Qutayba's multifaceted response: he questions the chain, offers a linguistic interpretation of 'ama, and shows that adding 'ma' does not fix the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Ibn Qutayba's entire commentary. Note the structure: objection, critique of chain, interpretation from Abu 'Ubaid, discussion of the 'ma' addition.
This is a classic example of hadith criticism and ta'wil.
2
Analyze the chain critique
Ibn Qutayba points out that the transmitters are unknown or Bedouins, and the hadith is disputed. Consider why chain criticism is important.
3
Understand Abu 'Ubaid's interpretation
If 'ama is read short (qasr), it means 'blindness' – i.e., God was unknown regarding direction. If long (madd), it means cloud, but that is also non-spatial.
The key is that both readings avoid spatial location.
4
Reflect on the 'ma' addition
Some added 'ma' to say 'no air above, no air below' to deny space, but Ibn Qutayba argues that 'above' and 'below' still imply direction.
This shows that simply negating the air doesn't negate the spatial language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه التي ذكرناها ، بعضها قوى ، وبعضها ضعيف . وكيفما كان الأمر فقد ثبت أن في القرآن والأخبار دلائل كثيرة ، تدل على تنزيه الله تعالى عن الحيز والجهة . وبالله التوفيق .
(٨٤) قال عبد الله بن مسلم بن قتيبة المتوفى سنة ٢٧٦ هـ في كتابه « تأويل مختلف الحديث » : قالوا : رويتم في حديث أبي رزين العقيلي ، من روابة حماد ابن سلمة ، أنه قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : أين كان ربنا ، قبل أن يخلق السموات والأرض ؟ فقال : « كان في عماء ، فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء » . قالوا : وهذا تحديد وتشبيه . قال أبو محمد : ونحن تقول : ان حديث أبي رزين هذا مختلف فيه ، وقد جاء من غير هذا الوجه بألفاظ تستشنع أيضا ، والنقلة له أعراب . ووكيع بن حدس الذي روى عنه حديث حماد بن سلمة أيضا لا يعرف . غير أنه قد تكلم في تفسير هذا الحديث : أبو عبيد ، القاسم بن سلام . حدثنا عنه أحمد بن سعيد اللحياني ، أنه قال : « العماء » : السحاب . وهو كما ذكر في كلام العرب ، ان كان الحرف ممدودا . وإن كان مقصورا ، كأنه كان في عمى . فانه أراد كان في عمى عن معرفة الناس . كما تقول : عميت عن هذا الأمر ، فأنا أعمى عنه ، عمى : اذا أشكل عليك ، لم تعرفه ، ولم تعرف جهته . وكل شيء خفي عليك ، فهو في عمى عنك . وأما قوله : « فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء » فان قوما زادوا فيه ما فقالوا : « ما فوقه هواء ، وما تحته هواء » استيحاشا من أن يكون فوقه هواء ، وتحته هواء ، ويكون بينهما . والرواية هي الأولى والوحشة لا تزول بزيادة « ما » لأن فوق وتحت باقيان . والله أعلم .
(۸۵) أقوى من بعض : خ
And know: that these proofs that we have mentioned, some are strong and some are weak. However the matter may be, it has been established that in the Quran and the hadiths there are many evidences indicating the transcendence (tanzih) of Allah, exalted, above spatial location (al-hayyiz) and direction (al-jiha). And success is with Allah.
(84) 'Abdullah bin Muslim bin Qutayba (d. 276 AH) said in his book 'Ta'wil Mukhtalif al-Hadith': They said: You have narrated in the hadith of Abu Razin al-'Uqayli, from the transmission of Hammad ibn Salama, that he said to the Prophet (ﷺ): Where was our Lord before He created the heavens and the earth? He said: 'He was in 'ama, above Him is air, and below Him is air.' They said: This is specification and anthropomorphism (tashbih). Abu Muhammad said: We say: This hadith of Abu Razin is disputed, and it has come from other chains with expressions that are also objectionable, and its transmitters are Bedouins. And Waki' ibn Hudus, from whom Hammad ibn Salama narrated, is not known. However, Abu 'Ubaid al-Qasim ibn Sallam has spoken on the interpretation of this hadith. Ahmad ibn Sa'id al-Lihyani narrated to us from him that he said: 'Al-'ama' (with madd) means the cloud. And it is as mentioned in the speech of the Arabs, if the letter is lengthened. But if it is shortened (maqsur), it is as if he meant 'was in blindness ('ama) regarding the knowledge of people.' As you say: 'I became blind ('amitu) about this matter, so I am blind (a'ma) about it,' meaning it became obscure to you, you did not know it and did not know its direction. And everything that is hidden from you is in 'ama (blindness) concerning you. As for his statement: 'above Him is air, and below Him is air,' some people added 'ma' to it and said: 'there is no air above Him and no air below Him,' out of discomfort that there is air above Him and air below Him, and He is between them. The first narration is the correct one, and the discomfort is not removed by adding 'ma', because 'above' and 'below' remain. And Allah knows best.
(85) 'Stronger than some' in manuscript.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, Razi offers a concluding note: although some of the proofs he presented are strong and some weak, the overall result is that both Quran and hadith contain abundant evidence for God's transcendence (tanzih) beyond space and direction. Second, he quotes a long extract from Ibn Qutayba's 'Ta'wil Mukhtalif al-Hadith' which addresses a variant of the 'ama hadith that includes the phrase 'above Him is air, below Him is air'. Ibn Qutayba critiques the chain, noting that the transmitters are unknown or Bedouins, and then offers the interpretation of Abu 'Ubaid: if 'ama is read with a short vowel (qasr), it means 'blindness'—i.e., God was unknown in terms of direction or location. He also discusses the addition of 'ma' in the phrase 'no air above Him, no air below Him', arguing that adding 'ma' does not eliminate the implication of 'above' and 'below', so the discomfort remains. The marginal note (85) indicates a variant reading 'stronger than some' in another manuscript.

Key Terms
عماء
عمى
تأويل مختلف الحديث
تحديد
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the 'ama hadith proves Allah was in a specific location before creation.
Correction: Ibn Qutayba shows the hadith is weak and can be interpreted metaphorically (blindness or cloud) to avoid anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Qutayba, what is one reason the hadith of Abu Razin is not reliable?
A) It is not in the Quran.
B) The transmitters are unknown or Bedouins.
C) It contradicts the Bible.
D) It has no chain.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Qutayba defends divine transcendence by questioning the authenticity of the 'ama hadith and offering non-spatial interpretations of its language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that appears to describe God in physical terms, do you rush to accept it literally or do you question its authenticity and seek scholarly interpretation like Ibn Qutayba did? What does your default reaction reveal about your reverence for Allah's transcendence? Will you commit to not forming opinions on such hadith without consulting reliable scholars?
?
This lesson shows that even the addition of a single word ('ma') does not solve deeper theological problems. In your own life, do you sometimes make superficial adjustments to your beliefs to avoid discomfort, while the core issue remains? Will you allow this lesson to drive you to a deeper, more consistent adherence to tawhid in all your thoughts?
?
Ibn Qutayba did not simply accept a famous hadith but examined its chain and interpretation. How often do you critically examine the sources of your religious knowledge? Are you willing to dig deeper when something conflicts with the clear principles of tawhid? What step will you take today to ensure your understanding of Allah is based on sound scholarship rather than popular narrations?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
Razi's Concluding Scriptural Proof for God's Transcendence
Razi concludes scriptural proofs for tanzih by interpreting 'ama' as absence of direction and citing a supporting hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi interprets the 'ama' hadith as the absence of direction and concludes that scripture provides ample evidence for God's transcendence from space and direction.

Core Idea
The 'ama' hadith, properly interpreted, affirms God's transcendence from direction.

In concluding his scriptural arguments for God's transcendence, Razi revisits the challenging 'ama' hadith and offers an interpretation that harmonizes with divine transcendence. He reinforces this with another Prophetic saying that explicitly denies spatial limitations, and then summarizes that the overall scriptural evidence is sufficient. This lesson examines his final synthesis of textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation of Razi's concluding remarks on the 'ama' hadith.
2
Understand Razi's interpretation
Notice that Razi interprets 'ama' as 'absence of direction' (al-jiha). He also cites a hadith that denies water beneath or air above God, which negates any spatial location.
3
See the overall claim
Razi acknowledges that some of his arguments are strong and some weak, but insists that the cumulative evidence from Quran and hadith clearly establishes God's transcendence from space (hayz) and direction.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Place this conclusion within Razi's larger project of defending God's transcendence (tanzih) against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأمكن جعل العمى عبارة عن عدم الجهة. ويتأكد هذا بقوله عليه السلام: ليس تحته ماء، ولا فوقه هواء. واعلم: أن هذه الوجوه التي ذكرناها، بعضها قوى، وبعضها ضعيف. وكيفما كان الأمر فقد ثبت أن في القرآن والأخبار: دلائل كثيرة، تدل على تنزيه الله تعالى عن الحيز والجهة. وبالله التوفيق. قال عبد الله بن مسلم بن قتيبة المتوفى سنة ٢٧٦ هـ في كتابه «تأويل مختلف الحديث»: قالوا: رويتم في حديث أبي رزين العقيلي، من روابة حماد ابن سلمة، أنه قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أين كان ربنا، قبل أن يخلق السموات والأرض؟ فقال: «كان في عماء، فوقه هواء، وتحته هواء». قالوا: وهذا تحديد وتشبيه. قال أبو محمد: ونحن نقول: إن حديث أبي رزين هذا مختلف فيه، وقد جاء من غير هذا الوجه بألفاظ تستشنع أيضا، والنقلة له أعراب. ووكيع بن حدس الذي روى عنه حديث حماد بن سلمة أيضا لا يعرف. غير أنه قد تكلم في تفسير هذا الحديث: أبو عبيد، القاسم بن سلام. حدثنا عنه أحمد بن سعيد اللحياني، أنه قال: «العماء»: السحاب. وهو كما ذكر في كلام العرب، إن كان الحرف ممدودا. وإن كان مقصورا، كأنه كان في عمى. فإنه أراد كان في عمى عن معرفة الناس. كما تقول: عميت عن هذا الأمر، فأنا أعمى عنه، عمى: إذا أشكل عليك، لم تعرفه، ولم تعرف جهته. وكل شيء خفي عليك، فهو في عمى عنك. وأما قوله: «فوقه هواء، وتحته هواء» فإن قوما زادوا فيه «ما» فقالوا: «ما فوقه هواء، وما تحته هواء» استيحاشا من أن يكون فوقه هواء، وتحته هواء، ويكون بينهما. والرواية هي الأولى، والوحشة لا تزول بزيادة «ما» لأن فوق وتحت باقيان. والله أعلم.
So it is possible to make 'blindness' ('ama) an expression for the absence of direction. And this is strengthened by his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no water beneath Him, nor air above Him.' And know: that these aspects which we have mentioned, some of them are strong, and some are weak. And whatever the case may be, it has been established that in the Qur'an and the traditions there are many proofs that indicate the transcendence (tanzih) of God Almighty from space (hayz) and direction (jiha). And with God is success. 'Abd Allah ibn Muslim ibn Qutayba (d. 276 AH) said in his book 'Ta'wil Mukhtalif al-Hadith': They said: You have related in the hadith of Abu Razin al-'Uqayli, from the narration of Hammad ibn Salama, that he said to the Prophet (peace be upon him): Where was our Lord before He created the heavens and the earth? He said: 'He was in an 'ama (cloud/unknowable), with air above Him and air beneath Him.' They said: This is determination and anthropomorphism. Abu Muhammad said: But we say: This hadith of Abu Razin is disputed, and there has come from other than this path words that are also objectionable, and its transmitters are Bedouins. And Waki' ibn Hudus, from whom the hadith of Hammad ibn Salama is related, is also unknown. However, Abu 'Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam has spoken on the interpretation of this hadith. Ahmad ibn Sa'id al-Lihyani narrated to us from him that he said: 'Al-'ama' is the cloud. And it is as mentioned in the speech of the Arabs if the letter is long (mamdud). And if it is short (maqsura), it would be as if he was in 'ama (blindness) from knowledge of the people. For he intended that he was in blindness ('ama) regarding knowledge of the people. As you say: I was blind ('amaytu) about this matter, so I am blind (a'ma) concerning it, 'ama: when something is obscure to you, you do not know it, and you do not know its direction. And everything that is hidden from you is in blindness ('ama) from you. As for his statement 'with air above him and air beneath him,' some people added 'ma' (what) and said 'what is above him is air and what is beneath him is air,' out of uneasiness that there should be air above him and air beneath him and he would be between them. The narration is the first, and the uneasiness is not removed by adding 'ma' because 'above' and 'beneath' remain. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary and a supporting citation. The author first states that the notion of 'blindness' (al-'ama) can be interpreted as the absence of direction (jiha), and he strengthens this interpretation with the Prophet's reported saying: 'There is no water beneath Him, nor air above Him' — which denies spatial location. He then acknowledges that among the arguments he has presented, some are strong and some are weak, but overall the Qur'an and hadith provide sufficient evidence for God's transcendence beyond space (hayz) and direction. To further support this, he quotes Ibn Qutayba's detailed explanation of the hadith about 'ama. Ibn Qutayba addresses the objection that this hadith seems anthropomorphic by arguing that the hadith is weak or metaphorical. He reports two interpretations: either 'ama' means 'cloud' (if the word is pronounced long), or it means 'blindness' or 'unknowability' (if short), indicating that God was unknown to people before creation. He also discusses the added word 'ma' in some versions and explains that it does not remove the problem of spatial terms. Thus the author uses this citation to show that even if the hadith is authentic, it does not imply spatial location when properly interpreted.

Key Terms
تَنْزِيه (tanzih, transcendence)
العَمَاء (al-'ama, cloud/unknowability)
الجِهَة (al-jiha, direction)
الحَيْز (al-hayz, space)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if some scriptural proofs are weak, the entire case for tanzih is weak.
Correction: Razi acknowledges variation in strength but insists the overall evidence from Quran and hadith is sufficient to establish God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim about the 'ama' hadith?
A) It should be rejected as weak.
B) It can be interpreted as absence of direction.
C) It proves God is in a cloud.
D) It has no scriptural basis.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that even the 'ama' hadith, when properly interpreted, supports the transcendence of God from direction and space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith or verse that seems to describe God in physical terms, do you instinctively take it literally, or do you immediately seek the interpretation that preserves God's transcendence? What does your default reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's words and the scholars' explanations? This week, commit to pausing before interpreting such texts and reminding yourself of Allah's absolute tanzih.
?
Razi admits that some of his arguments are weak yet still maintains the overall conclusion. In your own faith journey, do you ever reject a core Islamic belief because you find one of its arguments unconvincing? How can you follow Razi's example in holding onto the truth even when some evidences seem imperfect?
?
The hadith says there is no water beneath Allah nor air above Him. Do you truly believe that Allah is beyond all spatial concepts, or does your mind still picture Him as being 'somewhere'? This week, practice internalizing that Allah is not confined by any direction or place. How might this change the way you feel connected to Him in your prayers?

Ibn Qutayba's Interpretation of the 'Ama' Hadith
Ibn Qutayba critiques the 'ama' hadith's authenticity and offers linguistic interpretations to negate anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Qutayba's critical analysis of the 'ama' hadith, including its authenticity issues and his two linguistic interpretations, and how this supports the rejection of anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Ibn Qutayba defends God's transcendence by showing the 'ama' hadith is either weak or metaphorical.

After Razi's own conclusion, he cites the respected scholar Ibn Qutayba to provide a detailed refutation of those who used the 'ama' hadith to claim that God exists in a physical location. Ibn Qutayba addresses both the chain of transmission and the meaning of key terms, showing that the hadith does not entail anthropomorphism. This lesson unpacks his multi-layered response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation of Ibn Qutayba's discussion of the 'ama' hadith.
2
Understand the objection
Notice that opponents cited the hadith to claim that God was in a specific location with air above and beneath, implying determination (tahdid) and anthropomorphism (tashbih).
3
Analyze Ibn Qutayba's response on authenticity
He first questions the hadith's authenticity by pointing to unknown transmitters and other problematic versions.
4
Examine the linguistic interpretations
See how he explains 'ama' as either 'cloud' (if long) or 'unknowability' (if short), and how the addition of 'ma' does not remove the spatial language.
5
Conclude the lesson
Recognize that this analysis shows that even if the hadith were authentic, it can be interpreted in a way consistent with tanzih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a hadith that seems to challenge God's transcendence, do you instinctively reject it or do you, like Ibn Qutayba, pause to seek a deeper understanding? What does your response reveal about your trust in both divine revelation and reasoned theology? Commit to studying such texts with humility and scholarly rigor.
?
Ibn Qutayba offered two interpretations of 'ama'—one metaphorical (cloud) and one epistemological (unknowability). Which interpretation challenges you more to accept that some truths about God remain hidden? How can you cultivate contentment with not knowing everything about your Lord?
?
The narrators who added 'ma' to the hadith were uneasy with the literal wording. Have you ever rephrased a religious text in your mind to make it more palatable? What would it look like to submit to God's wisdom even when it unsettles your assumptions?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Definition of Mutahayyiz and Its Implications
Razi defines mutahayyiz and explains its entailment of body or atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of 'mutahayyiz' and why proving that God is not mutahayyiz automatically proves He is neither a body nor an indivisible atom.

Core Idea
To disprove God as body or atom, first prove He is not mutahayyiz.

Razi opens the third chapter of his work by stating his goal: to present rational proofs that God is not a body or something occupying space. He first defines 'mutahayyiz' — anything that occupies space — and shows that it must be either divisible (a body) or indivisible (an atom). This definition is the key that unlocks the entire argument. By proving God is not mutahayyiz, He is simultaneously cleared of being a body or an atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise definitional style.
2
Understand the definition of mutahayyiz
Identify the two cases: if something occupies space and is divisible, it is a body; if it is not divisible, it is an indivisible atom.
This dichotomy covers all possible entities that occupy space.
3
Connect to the overall argument
Realize that Razi's strategy is to prove God is not mutahayyiz, which then rules out both body and atom in one move.
The passage ends by announcing that proofs (wujuh) will follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في إقامة الدلائل العقلية على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز البتة. اعلم: أنا إذا دللنا على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز، فقد دللنا على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر فرد، لأن المتحيز، إن كان منقسما فهو الجسم، وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو الجوهر الفرد. فنقول: الذي يدل على أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز: وجوه.
The third chapter: On establishing rational proofs that He, exalted, is not at all occupying space (mutahayyiz). Know that if we prove that He, exalted, is not occupying space, then we have proven that He, exalted, is not a body (jism) nor an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), because that which occupies space (al-mutahayyiz): if it is divisible then it is the body, and if it is not divisible then it is the indivisible atom. So we say: That which indicates that He, exalted, is not occupying space: [are] aspects.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the third chapter. The author states his objective: to produce rational proofs that God (ta'ala) is not a body or something occupying space (mutahayyiz). He gives a definition: anything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) must be either divisible or indivisible. If divisible, it is a body (jism); if indivisible, it is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Therefore, proving God is not mutahayyiz automatically proves He is neither a body nor an indivisible atom. He then announces that he will present several proofs (wujuh) for the claim that God is not mutahayyiz. The actual proofs begin after this passage. The definition is precise and sets the terms for the subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
مُتَحَيِّز (mutahayyiz, occupying space)
جِسْم (jism, body)
جَوْهَر فَرْد (jawhar fard, indivisible atom)
دَلائِل عَقْلِيَّة (dala'il 'aqliyya, rational proofs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'mutahayyiz' refers only to material bodies.
Correction: Razi clarifies that mutahayyiz includes both divisible bodies and indivisible atoms; anything occupying space is mutahayyiz, regardless of divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's definition, what is a 'jawhar fard' (indivisible atom)?
A) A body that can be divided infinitely
B) That which occupies space and is not divisible
C) A spiritual substance
D) A celestial sphere

Key Takeaway
Razi's definition of mutahayyiz establishes that if God is not mutahayyiz, He is automatically not a body nor an atom, simplifying the theological proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as being 'somewhere'—like in the sky or inside a mosque? How does Razi's definition of mutahayyiz challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for how you direct your worship?
?
If God is not in any place, then your prayer is not 'reaching' a physical location. Does that change how you feel when you raise your hands in supplication? Examine your heart: have you ever felt God is distant because you think He is 'up there'?
?
Razi says proving God is not mutahayyiz automatically proves He is not a body nor an atom. Are you willing to accept a God who cannot be pinned down by any spatial concept? What comfort or discomfort does that bring you?

First Proof: Spatiality Forces Essential Similarity
Razi presents the first rational proof that God cannot be a body, leading to essential similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that God cannot be a spatial being, focusing on the argument that spatiality would entail essential similarity.

Core Idea
If God were spatial, He would share the same essence as all spatial things.

Razi begins his defense of divine transcendence with a clean logical proof. He shows that if God were a body, He would necessarily be similar to other bodies in their very essence. This lesson walks through the first half of that argument, establishing the logical structure and the key conclusion that spatiality forces essential likeness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's claim
Read the opening statement: If God were spatial, He would be similar in complete essence. This is the premise to be proved.
2
Follow the two divisions
Notice how Razi divides the possibilities: either God differs in specific essence or not. The first is false, so the second is determined.
The first division being false is a key move.
3
Analyze the first branch
Razi then examines the case where essence equals spatiality. This leads to the conclusion that if God were spatial, His essence would be similar to other spatial essences.
This is the heart of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول: إنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مماثلا لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية. وهذا ممتنع. فكونه متحيزاً ممتنع.
وإنما قلنا إنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مماثلا لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية: لأنه لو كان متحيزاً، لكن مساويا لسائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً، ثم بعد هذا لا يخلو إما أن يقال: إنه يخالف غيره من الأجسام في ماهيته المخصوصة، وإما أن لا يخالفه في ماهيته المخصوصة. والقسم الأول باطل فتعين الثاني. وحينئذ يحصل منه: أنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مثلا لسائر المتحيزات.
فيحتاج ههنا إلى بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون مساويا لسائر المتحيزات، في عموم المتحيزية، ومخالفا لها في ماهيته المخصوصة.
فنقول: الدليل على أن ذلك ممتنع: هو أنه بتقدير أن يكون مساويا لسائرها في المتحيزية، ومخالفا لها في الخصوصية: كان ما به الاشتراك مغايرا لا محالة لما به الامتياز. وحينئذ يكون عموم المتحيزية مغايرا لخصوص ذاتها المخصوصة.
وحينئذ نقول: إما أن تكون الذات هي المتحيزية، وتكون تلك الخصوصية صفة لتلك الذات، وإما أن يقال: المتحيزية صفة، وتلك الخصوصية هي الذات.
أما القسم الأول فإنه يقتضي حصول المقصود. لأنه إذا كان مجرد المتحيزية هو الذات، وثبت أن مجرد المتحيزية أمر مشترك فيه بينه وبين سائر المتحيزات، فحينئذ يحصل منه أن بتقدير أن يكون تعالى متحيزاً، تكون ذاته مماثلة لذوات سائر المتحيزات. وليس المطلوب إلا ذلك.
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: الذات هي الخصوصية، والصفة هي المتحيزية. فنقول: هذا محال. وذلك لأن تلك الخصوصية من حيث إنها هي هي، مع قطع النظر عن المتحيزية، إما أن يكون لها اختصاص بالحيز، وإما أن لا يكون لها ذلك. والأول محال. لأن كل ما كان حاصلا في حيز وجهة على سبيل الاستقلال، كان متحيزاً. فلو كانت تلك الخصوصية التي فرضناها خالية عن التحيز، حاصلة في الحيز، لكان الخالي عن التحيز متحيزاً. وذلك محال.
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: إن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات. فنقول: إنه يمتنع أن تكون المتحيزية صفة قائمة بها، لأن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات، والمتحيزية أمر لا يعقل إلا أن يكون حاصلا في الجهات. والشيء الذي يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الجهات، يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا في الشيء الذي لا اختصاص له بالجهات.
The first proof: If He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence. And this is impossible. So His being spatial is impossible.
And we say that if He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence, because if He were spatial, He would be equal to other spatial beings in being spatial; then after this, it is not free from either it being said that He differs from other bodies in His specific essence, or that He does not differ from them in His specific essence. The first division is false, so the second becomes determined. Then from this it follows that if He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings.
So here we need to show that it is impossible for Him to be equal to other spatial beings in the generality of spatiality, and different from them in His specific essence.
We say: The evidence that this is impossible is that on the assumption that He is equal to them in spatiality and different from them in specificity, then that by which there is commonality would necessarily be different from that by which there is distinction. Then the generality of spatiality would be different from the specificity of the particular essence.
Then we say: Either the essence is spatiality, and that specificity is an attribute of that essence; or it is said: spatiality is an attribute, and that specificity is the essence.
As for the first division, it necessitates the attainment of the objective. Because if mere spatiality is the essence, and it is established that mere spatiality is a common matter between Him and other spatial beings, then from that it follows that on the assumption that He, exalted, is spatial, His essence would be similar to the essences of other spatial beings. And that is exactly what is sought.
As for the second division, which is that it is said: the essence is the specificity, and the attribute is spatiality. We say: This is impossible. That is because that specificity, in itself, without regard to spatiality, either has a connection to space (hayyiz) or does not have that. The first is impossible. Because everything that exists in a space and direction independently is spatial. So if that specificity which we assumed to be free from spatiality existed in space, then that which is free from spatiality would be spatial. And that is impossible.
As for the second division, which is that it is said: That specificity is not connected to any space or directions. We say: It is impossible for spatiality to be an attribute subsisting in it, because that specificity is not connected to any space or directions, and spatiality is something not conceivable except as existing in directions. And a thing that must exist in directions is impossible to exist in that which has no connection to directions.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage contains the first rational proof that God is not a spatial being. Razi argues that if God were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya). This is impossible because God is unique. The proof proceeds as follows: (1) If God were spatial, He would share the common property of being spatial with all other spatial things. (2) Either He differs from them in His specific essence or He does not. (3) If He differs, then that common property (spatiality) and the specific essence would be distinct. This leads to two possibilities: either the essence is spatiality and specificity is an attribute, or the essence is specificity and spatiality is an attribute. (4) If the essence is spatiality, then since spatiality is common, God's essence would be identical to other essences—which is the required conclusion (that similarity is entailed, which is impossible). (5) If the essence is specificity and spatiality is an attribute, then this is impossible because the specific essence (without spatiality) must either be connected to space or not. If connected, then it would itself be spatial, contradicting the assumption that it is free from spatiality. If not connected, then spatiality cannot be an attribute of it, because spatiality necessarily exists in directions, while the specific essence has no connection to directions; thus an attribute that requires direction cannot subsist in something not connected to direction. Therefore, the only coherent possibility is that the essence is spatiality itself, leading to similarity, which is impossible. Hence, God cannot be spatial.

Key Terms
تمام الماهية
مساواة
مباينة
متحيزية
خصوصية
ذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God could be a body but still be unique in some way not sharing the essence.
Correction: Razi shows that if God were a body, He would share the common property of spatiality. That commonality forces essential similarity unless there is a difference in specific essence, which leads to absurdities. The only coherent outcome is essential similarity, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, what is the consequence of assuming that God's essence is spatiality itself?
A) His essence would be unique and not similar to others.
B) His essence would be similar to other spatial beings.
C) Spatiality would be an attribute, not the essence.
D) The proof fails because spatiality is not a common property.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof shows that spatiality forces essential similarity, so God cannot be spatial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be like any spatial thing, then your mental image of Him—if you have one—is necessarily false. Will you strive to worship God without imagining a form, even when your mind wants to picture Him?
?
The proof shows that any similarity to creation is impossible for God. Have you ever, in moments of desperation, thought of God as if He were a powerful human who might be swayed by your tears? How will you replace that thought with the true understanding of His transcendence?
?
Razi’s argument depends on precise reasoning. Are you willing to engage in such rigorous thinking about your beliefs, or do you prefer comfortable but inaccurate ideas about God? What step will you take today to purify your tawheed from any trace of anthropomorphism?

Divine Transcendence

Second Branch: Refuting Spatiality as Attribute
Razi refutes the second branch of the argument, showing that spatiality cannot be an attribute of a non-spatial essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the possibility that spatiality could be an attribute of a non-spatial essence.

Core Idea
Spatiality cannot be an attribute of anything non-spatial, so God cannot be a body.

In the previous lesson, we saw that if God's essence were spatiality, similarity follows. But there is another logical possibility: maybe God's essence is something unique and spatiality is just an attribute. In this lesson, Razi dismantles that possibility by showing it leads to two absurdities. This completes the first proof against divine corporeality.

Guided Path
1
State the second possibility
Read: 'the essence is the specificity, and the attribute is spatiality'. This is the alternative to be refuted.
2
First subcase: specificity connected to space
If the specific essence has a connection to space, then it would itself be spatial, contradicting the assumption that it is free from spatiality.
This is a contradiction.
3
Second subcase: specificity not connected to space
If the specific essence has no connection to space, then spatiality cannot be an attribute of it, because spatiality requires existence in directions, and that which has no connection to directions cannot support such an attribute.
This is also impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول: إنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مماثلا لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية. وهذا ممتنع. فكونه متحيزاً ممتنع.
وإنما قلنا إنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مماثلا لسائر المتحيزات في تمام الماهية: لأنه لو كان متحيزاً، لكن مساويا لسائر المتحيزات في كونه متحيزاً، ثم بعد هذا لا يخلو إما أن يقال: إنه يخالف غيره من الأجسام في ماهيته المخصوصة، وإما أن لا يخالفه في ماهيته المخصوصة. والقسم الأول باطل فتعين الثاني. وحينئذ يحصل منه: أنه تعالى لو كان متحيزاً، لكان مثلا لسائر المتحيزات.
فيحتاج ههنا إلى بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون مساويا لسائر المتحيزات، في عموم المتحيزية، ومخالفا لها في ماهيته المخصوصة.
فنقول: الدليل على أن ذلك ممتنع: هو أنه بتقدير أن يكون مساويا لسائرها في المتحيزية، ومخالفا لها في الخصوصية: كان ما به الاشتراك مغايرا لا محالة لما به الامتياز. وحينئذ يكون عموم المتحيزية مغايرا لخصوص ذاتها المخصوصة.
وحينئذ نقول: إما أن تكون الذات هي المتحيزية، وتكون تلك الخصوصية صفة لتلك الذات، وإما أن يقال: المتحيزية صفة، وتلك الخصوصية هي الذات.
أما القسم الأول فإنه يقتضي حصول المقصود. لأنه إذا كان مجرد المتحيزية هو الذات، وثبت أن مجرد المتحيزية أمر مشترك فيه بينه وبين سائر المتحيزات، فحينئذ يحصل منه أن بتقدير أن يكون تعالى متحيزاً، تكون ذاته مماثلة لذوات سائر المتحيزات. وليس المطلوب إلا ذلك.
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: الذات هي الخصوصية، والصفة هي المتحيزية. فنقول: هذا محال. وذلك لأن تلك الخصوصية من حيث إنها هي هي، مع قطع النظر عن المتحيزية، إما أن يكون لها اختصاص بالحيز، وإما أن لا يكون لها ذلك. والأول محال. لأن كل ما كان حاصلا في حيز وجهة على سبيل الاستقلال، كان متحيزاً. فلو كانت تلك الخصوصية التي فرضناها خالية عن التحيز، حاصلة في الحيز، لكان الخالي عن التحيز متحيزاً. وذلك محال.
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: إن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات. فنقول: إنه يمتنع أن تكون المتحيزية صفة قائمة بها، لأن تلك الخصوصية غير مختصة بشيء من الأحياز والجهات، والمتحيزية أمر لا يعقل إلا أن يكون حاصلا في الجهات. والشيء الذي يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الجهات، يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا في الشيء الذي لا اختصاص له بالجهات.
The first proof: If He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence. And this is impossible. So His being spatial is impossible.
And we say that if He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence, because if He were spatial, He would be equal to other spatial beings in being spatial; then after this, it is not free from either it being said that He differs from other bodies in His specific essence, or that He does not differ from them in His specific essence. The first division is false, so the second becomes determined. Then from this it follows that if He, exalted, were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings.
So here we need to show that it is impossible for Him to be equal to other spatial beings in the generality of spatiality, and different from them in His specific essence.
We say: The evidence that this is impossible is that on the assumption that He is equal to them in spatiality and different from them in specificity, then that by which there is commonality would necessarily be different from that by which there is distinction. Then the generality of spatiality would be different from the specificity of the particular essence.
Then we say: Either the essence is spatiality, and that specificity is an attribute of that essence; or it is said: spatiality is an attribute, and that specificity is the essence.
As for the first division, it necessitates the attainment of the objective. Because if mere spatiality is the essence, and it is established that mere spatiality is a common matter between Him and other spatial beings, then from that it follows that on the assumption that He, exalted, is spatial, His essence would be similar to the essences of other spatial beings. And that is exactly what is sought.
As for the second division, which is that it is said: the essence is the specificity, and the attribute is spatiality. We say: This is impossible. That is because that specificity, in itself, without regard to spatiality, either has a connection to space (hayyiz) or does not have that. The first is impossible. Because everything that exists in a space and direction independently is spatial. So if that specificity which we assumed to be free from spatiality existed in space, then that which is free from spatiality would be spatial. And that is impossible.
As for the second division, which is that it is said: That specificity is not connected to any space or directions. We say: It is impossible for spatiality to be an attribute subsisting in it, because that specificity is not connected to any space or directions, and spatiality is something not conceivable except as existing in directions. And a thing that must exist in directions is impossible to exist in that which has no connection to directions.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage contains the first rational proof that God is not a spatial being. Razi argues that if God were spatial, He would be similar to all other spatial beings in complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya). This is impossible because God is unique. The proof proceeds as follows: (1) If God were spatial, He would share the common property of being spatial with all other spatial things. (2) Either He differs from them in His specific essence or He does not. (3) If He differs, then that common property (spatiality) and the specific essence would be distinct. This leads to two possibilities: either the essence is spatiality and specificity is an attribute, or the essence is specificity and spatiality is an attribute. (4) If the essence is spatiality, then since spatiality is common, God's essence would be identical to other essences—which is the required conclusion (that similarity is entailed, which is impossible). (5) If the essence is specificity and spatiality is an attribute, then this is impossible because the specific essence (without spatiality) must either be connected to space or not. If connected, then it would itself be spatial, contradicting the assumption that it is free from spatiality. If not connected, then spatiality cannot be an attribute of it, because spatiality necessarily exists in directions, while the specific essence has no connection to directions; thus an attribute that requires direction cannot subsist in something not connected to direction. Therefore, the only coherent possibility is that the essence is spatiality itself, leading to similarity, which is impossible. Hence, God cannot be spatial.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that even if God's essence is non-spatial, He could still have spatial attributes.
Correction: Razi shows that spatiality as an attribute cannot subsist in something non-spatial because spatiality necessarily involves direction, and that which has no connection to direction cannot support it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the problem with assuming that the specific essence is not connected to space?
A) Then it would be impossible for spatiality to be an attribute of it.
B) Then it would be the same as other essences.
C) Then it would be spatial.
D) There is no problem; it is a valid assumption.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation of the second branch shows that spatiality cannot be an attribute of a non-spatial essence, completing the proof that God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, attributed a place or direction to God when you pray? How does Razi's argument challenge that, and what will you do to purify your understanding of God's transcendence?
?
If God is not spatial, does that distance Him from you in your heart? How can you feel His nearness (qurb) without imagining Him in a place? This lesson demands that you hold both His transcendence and immanence—do you, or do you let go of one?
?
Razi proves that spatiality cannot be an attribute of God. But do you still find yourself thinking of God as 'up there' or 'in the heavens' in a literal sense? What concrete step will you take today to replace that thought with the correct belief that God is beyond all spaces and directions?
